St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


October 21, 2008 Tuesday
THIRD EDITION


Forget Obama and McCain: I'm your man


BYLINE: JOE HOLLEMAN


SECTION: EVERYDAY; Pg. EV2


LENGTH: 703 words


You have only 14 days, America, to decide who you want to lead this country. And IMHO - that's hip, cyber-techno Internet talk for "in my humble opinion" - that person should be me.

Some say I don't have a chance, that a vote for the Life Sherpa is a vote wasted. Well, did you feel that way when you cast your ballot for Ralph Nader? Well, OK, those were wasted votes, but I think you get my point.

It's not like my candidacy hasn't struck a chord. If you go to my column online (stltoday.com/lifestyle) you will see my official campaign poster, as well as the electronic media's belated coverage of my quest.

Also, my campaign recently held a major rally at the Bar That Can't Be Named and made some real progress on some vital issues. For example, do you know how much better a White Russian tastes when it's made with vanilla-flavored vodka?

After many hours at the rally, we also concluded that my campaign needs to enlist the help of an untapped demographic, those folks who have crossed the U.S.-Mexico border in a, shall we say, less than technically correct manner.

Since you are in this country using our school systems, our hospitals and our social programs without paying any taxes, it would be great if you could also find a way to get to the polls and cast a vote for your ol' amigo, the Life Sherpa.

The future is now. Destiny has arrived. Do the right thing.

Dear Life Sherpa: With the presidential election right around the corner, there is one thing you need to do to ensure success: Stop answering questions that are asked of you! If someone asks you about strip joints, steer them towards your economic policy. If they inquire as to your favorite sports team, look them in the eye and tout your foreign policy beliefs. Answering questions directly never got anyone elected. Time is running out and the election is slipping away.

- Concerned Countian

Dear Concerned: I truly appreciate your heartfelt concern about my candidacy in these crucial days leading up to the election. I've thought about dodging the tough questions, and simply providing the same type of safe, stock answers that my two main opponents offer up ad nauseam. But the Life Sherpa doesn't play that game.

One candidate promises change. What the hell, I AM change. No more business as usual! No more catering to the whining left-wing tree-hugging cadre, or the blustering right-wing money-grubbing cabal.

With the election right around the corner, it's time to answer the questions, take off the gloves, cut to the chase and address a few issues:

- White-collar crime: Crooks are crooks, so lose the adjectives. White-collar criminals are simply criminals, just like crack dealers and car-jackers. Sell a rock of crack, you go to jail. Bilk investors and pillage their retirement accounts and you go to St. Tropez. That is no way to run a railroad.

- Affirmative action: Whether you favor it or oppose it, affirmative action is based on the notion that two wrongs make a right. As Pulitzer Prize-winning columnist Charles Krauthammer once wrote: "It frontally violates the principle of equal treatment and thus inevitably creates, in a society founded on the ideal of equality under law, new and bitter racial divisions." Also bad railroad management.

- Abortion: It involves babies, not fetuses. Federal law provides a person with the opportunity to make a choice on this divisive, emotional issue. I am not opposed to that. But please don't change the words to conveniently lessen the impact. Or in the interest of fairness, let's start calling death-row prisoners "rabid dogs." It makes it so much easier to put them down when the time comes.

- Energy: Hey, it takes wattage to manufacture Birkenstocks. So don't tell me you're serious about finding alternative energy sources to wean this nation from foreign petroleum, but then turn around and say you are opposed off-shore drilling for oil, nuclear power and burning coal. Maybe if you just click your heels three times, Dorothy, everything will run on solar panels and windmills.

Can you feel it?

It's Life Sherpa on-line chat time, coming up at noon today. As always, there's no cover charge and no drink minimum. Submit questions at STLtoday.com/lifestyle. Find the Sherpa's face and click.

Missouri Lawyers Weekly


October 20, 2008


Castle veep: Memphis attorney to be on ballot as Constitution Party's candidate for vice president


BYLINE: Allison Retka


SECTION: NEWS


LENGTH: 1042 words


When Missouri voters report to their polling places on Nov. 4, members of the state's legal community may recognize one of the names near the very top of the ballot: Darrell L. Castle.

Castle is the founding partner of the Castle Law Offices, a trio of bankruptcy and personal injury firms that includes offices in St. Louis and Kansas City.

Castle, a soft-spoken Memphis attorney, is the Constitution Party's candidate for vice president of the United States. In 37 states, including Missouri, his name will appear on the ballot just below Sens. Barack Obama and John McCain. 

The Constitution Party, a staunchly Christian conservative party that's seen growing membership in recent years, will be stumping in Kansas City later this week. Castle and the party's presidential pick, Florida Baptist preacher Chuck Baldwin, will speak at two campaign events on Friday.

The platform of the Constitution Party is seeped in Christian language and asserts that the nation was founded by Christians. The party bills itself as the only 100 percent anti-abortion ticket and advocates for a stronger border between the United States and Mexico.

Castle's run for the nation's second-highest office is a strenuous effort that's gone largely unnoticed even among attorneys here and in Castle's native Tennessee.

Both the executive director of the Tennessee Bar Association and the chair of the group's bankruptcy section knew Castle by name and by his solid reputation. Neither men knew he was running for a national office.

Castle, a Marine Corps veteran and an attorney for 30 years, admitted his candidacy has been flying under the radar, although not by choice.

"The national media don't seem to be lining up to interview me," he said last week from his law office in Memphis. "So that part's been difficult. We're making progress. "

Castle put most of his law practice on hold this year after being added to the ticket at the party's nominating convention, held in April in Kansas City.

His monthly visits to the firm's offices in Missouri have subsided, replaced by campaign stops in North Carolina, Pennsylvania and Arizona. Late last week Castle was slated to speak in New York at an anti-bailout rally on Wall Street.

Jason Amerine, the managing partner of the Castle Law Office's operations in Kansas City, said news of Castle's candidacy has reached some clients in Missouri. A few have called the office asking if the "Castle" running for vice president is the same "Castle" that gave the law firm its name.

"We tell them, 'yes,' " Amerine said. "They're all pretty surprised. I think a lot of them are happy to hear that. It kind of builds up credibility for this office here, knowing that the owner is running for vice president. "

Castle said most of his clients have no knowledge of his political aspirations.

"I've taken pains to prevent that from happening," he said. "I did not want to use this as a business-building tool. But at the same time, quite honestly, I've had very little news exposure. "

The Constitution Party in Missouri

Constitution Party candidates have not appeared on Missouri ballots since 2004, when the party failed to earn 2 percent of the vote in any of the statewide races that featured its candidates.

Party supporters made a deliberate push this year, collecting more than 16,000 signatures to regain the party's position on the ballot.

Castle is the only lawyer on the Constitution Party ticket in Missouri, said party chairwoman Donna Ivanovich, although not for want of effort. She said the party approached several attorneys in Rolla as potential Constitution Party candidates for an open judgeship in the area, only to come up empty.

Ivanovich, who made two unsuccessful runs as a party candidate for Missouri Secretary of State, is no stranger to the legal community. She's the assistant to Nicholas Terry, the senior associate dean of faculty at Saint Louis University Law School.

After leaving the Republican Party, Ivanovich participated in the formation of the Constitution Party in 1992, as did Castle.

She said she hasn't done much to advertise Castle's ties to Missouri, except to send invitations to federal attorneys for a campaign event in St. Louis that was eventually canceled.

"I haven't made a big effort to contact the law firms," Ivanovich said. "I just figure it's their obligation to know who they're voting for. If they feel they only have two candidates [for president], well, we have five candidates in Missouri. Unfortunately, people don't do their homework. "

The pressure is on for Missouri's Constitution Party. If the group can secure 2 percent of the vote in any of the five statewide races featuring Constitution Party candidates, then the party will stay on the ballot until 2010.

In 2004, Missourians cast 5,355 for the Constitution Party candidates for president and vice president. That segment was 0.2 percent of the presidential vote in Missouri.

Leading by example

For Castle, the last few months of campaigning have been an education in politics. This attempt at the vice presidency is his first-ever run for political office.

He said his family spurred him to throw his hat into the ring. His wife, Joan Castle, is active in Constitution Party activities in Tennessee and is the office manager of the 13-lawyer firm's Memphis office.

"I learned a lot about leadership in the Marine Corps," Castle said. "Not the way you might think, in an authoritative or dictatorial fashion. The Marine Corps teaches you to lead by example.

"If you require your Marines or your employees to be to work at 8, then you need to be there at 7:30. "

Amerine, the managing partner in the firm's Kansas City office, described Castle as a quiet person with sound legal judgment.

"He's very calm," Amerine said. "Nothing rattles him, no matter what the situation is. "

When Amerine joined the firm seven years ago, right out of law school at the University of Missouri-Kansas City, he had never heard of the Constitution Party.

Now, as an undecided voter, Amerine said he's researching the group and generally likes what he sees.

"I think his party and Chuck Baldwin, they have a good chance of providing a lot of positive change to our economy," Amerine said.

"I'm supportive of him. I know him well enough to know he could do some good. "

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


October 18, 2008 Saturday
THIRD EDITION


Who is next to head Treasury? Presidential candidates praise Warren Buffett, who sounds uninterested.


BYLINE: By Tom Raum The Associated Press


SECTION: BUSINESS; Pg. A32


LENGTH: 766 words


WASHINGTON - The next president's choice of a Treasury secretary may well be the most important appointment he'll make - someone to grab the reins of a trillion-dollar-plus program to shore up the world's largest economy, probably in the middle of a severe recession, and at the right hand of a chief executive who'll be a rookie himself.

In normal times, the job can be demanding enough. In an economic crisis, as now, it can be one of the most powerful and stressful jobs in the government. Just ask Henry Paulson, the former investment banker who's made it clear he's not interested in keeping the title.

If not Paulson, then who?

Both Republican John McCain and Democrat Barack Obama showered praise on legendary investor Warren Buffett when asked during a presidential debate whom they might put at Treasury. Buffett said maybe Paulson should stay for a while.

Obama has a couple of Bill Clinton's Treasury secretaries as economic advisers: Robert Rubin and Lawrence Summers. McCain has current and former CEOs of major companies, including Meg Whitman of eBay and Carly Fiorina of Hewlett-Packard.

As for Buffett, McCain said when asked in the debate, "He has already weighed in and helped stabilize some of the difficulties in the markets and with companies and corporations, institutions today."

Obama agreed that "Warren would be a pretty good choice - Warren Buffett, and I'm pleased to have his support."

It's a little hard to imagine the oracle of Omaha putting his money in a blind trust - or leaving Omaha for that matter. He previously has responded to such suggestions by saying he loves running his company, and a government position would be outside his "circle of confidence." Would he respond to an appeal to help his country in a time of need? Who knows?

On PBS' "The Charlie Rose Show" recently, Buffett said Paulson would do the next president a favor by staying in the job at least a year.

Paulson has appeared to rule this out, saying he's told both candidates he would "start working with whoever the new Treasury secretary is as soon as he is appointed and work to make the transition a first-rate transition."

Obama and McCain both are surrounded by experienced economic advisers.

Obama's team includes Buffett, former Federal Reserve Chairman Paul Volcker and and the two former Treasury secretaries: Rubin, now an executive with Citigroup, and Summers.

Summers has been put before the public a lot by the Obama campaign. But he's not without controversy. After serving as Clinton's final Treasury secretary, he became president of Harvard. He drew worldwide attention for his comments that biological differences may partly explain the dearth of women among the very highest achieving scientists, a controversy that led to his resignation.

Two seasoned economic professionals who might be tapped for the post regardless of who wins: Timothy F. Geithner, president of the Federal Reserve Bank of New York; and Sheila Bair, head of the Federal Deposit Insurance Corp.

Both, along with Paulson and Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke, have played major roles in putting together the government's series of bold steps to restore financial order in a world coming unglued.

Geithner is a Democrat, and Bair is a Republican. Also mentioned as a possible contender: JPMorgan Chase CEO Jamie Dimon.

McCain is close to eBay Chief Executive Meg Whitman - the only other person he mentioned at the presidential debate as a possible Treasury secretary. "She knows what it's like to be out there in the marketplace," McCain said. "She knows how to create jobs."

McCain also counts as a principal economic adviser former Hewlett-Packard CEO Carly Fiorina. The company's board fired her in 2005 after a string of earnings disappointments. She also drew criticism for launching an effort at Hewlett-Packard to identify company employees who were speaking to reporters anonymously.

Texan Phil Gramm, a close friend of McCain and a former Senate Banking Committee chairman, also was considered a possible candidate for Treasury secretary - until he was forced to leave McCain's campaign team during the summer after he decried some Americans as a "nation of whiners."

Two long-shot possibilities if McCain is elected: New York Mayor Michael Bloomberg, a former Democrat; and New York Attorney General Andrew Cuomo, the Democratic son of former Gov. Mario Cuomo. McCain suggested both of them, as well as Buffett, to head a bailout oversight board.

For Obama, other possibilities include Daniel Tarullo, his top adviser on financial issues, and New Jersey Gov. Jon Corzine, who is a Goldman Sachs alum, like Paulson.

BYLINE: From News Services

HOLLAND, OHIO

Joe the Plumber's story doesn't check out

Joe the Plumber's story sprang a few leaks Thursday.

Turns out that the man who was held up by John McCain as the typical, hard-working American taxpayer isn't really a licensed plumber. And court documents show he owes nearly $1,200 in back taxes.

"Joe," whose name is Samuel J. Wurzelbacher, was cited repeatedly in Wednesday night's final presidential debate by McCain for questioning Barack Obama's tax policy. He became a media celebrity, fielding calls during the debate and facing reporters outside his home near Toledo, Ohio, on Thursday morning for an impromptu nationally televised news conference.

WASHINGTON

Congressman apologizes for 'racist' accusation

Democratic Rep. John Murtha apologized Thursday for saying his home base of western Pennsylvania is racist in talking about Barack Obama's prospects for winning the state.

In a statement issued by his office, the 17-term congressman said: "I apologize for making the comment that western Pennsylvania is a racist area."

WASHINGTON

Lobbyist denies she had an affair with McCain

A lobbyist has broken months of silence to deny the main subtext of a New York Times story in February - that she was romantically involved with John McCain.

"I never had an affair or an inappropriate relationship with Senator McCain," Vicky Iseman told the National Journal magazine.

PHILADELPHIA

McCain makes a point of getting to Letterman

John McCain learned the hard way not to disappoint David Letterman, so he flew by helicopter to New York on Thursday to tape the "Late Show with David Letterman" after a weather delay grounded his campaign plane.

McCain was supposed to appear on the show Sept. 24 but abruptly canceled after briefly suspending his campaign to return to Washington to deal with the financial crisis. Since then, Letterman has regularly lampooned the Arizona senator on the show.

NEW YORK

World Series game will be delayed for Obama ad

Major League Baseball has agreed to push back the start time of a potential World Series Game 6 by eight minutes to allow Democrat Barack Obama to purchase a half-hour of air time on the Fox network.

DUBLIN, IRELAND

Irish bookmaker calls Obama the winner

The race is over as far as Ireland's biggest bookmaker is concerned.

Paddy Power PLC says it is so sure Barack Obama will win the U.S. presidential election next month that it paid off Thursday on all bets it had taken backing the Democratic candidate. It said it shelled out more than 1 million euros ($1.35 million).

WASHINGTON

FBI looking into ACORN activities

The FBI is investigating whether the community activist group ACORN helped foster voter registration fraud around the nation before the presidential election.

A senior law enforcement official confirmed the investigation. A second senior law enforcement official says the FBI was looking at results of inquiries in several states, including a raid on ACORN's office in Las Vegas, for any evidence of a coordinated national effort.

The Associated Press State & Local Wire


October 17, 2008 Friday 12:43 AM GMT


Ads for Obama in Oklahoma surprise backers


BYLINE: By RON JENKINS, Associated Press Writer


SECTION: STATE AND REGIONAL


LENGTH: 355 words


DATELINE: OKLAHOMA CITY


State Democratic officials say they are a bit puzzled that television commercials in support of their party's presidential nominee, Barack Obama, have been popping up on Oklahoma television stations.

Despite his lead in national polls and in battleground states, Obama has little chance of defeating Republican nominee John McCain in Oklahoma, which makes expenditures on television advertising in the state surprising eveb to some Democrats.

Polls have generally given McCain a 2-1 advantage over the Illinois senator in Oklahoma, a conservative state that hasn't voted for a Democratic nominee for president since 1964.

State Democratic Party Chairman Ivan Holmes said he was surprised to see two television spots Wednesday on Oklahoma City television stations.

Holmes said the Obama campaign has "not spent a penny" for yard signs to help the party drum up support for the nominee and his fellow Democrats. "We appealed to them two or three times just to help us out with yard signs to help our down ticket candidates," he said.

Jed Green, Obama's state campaign director, said he assumed it was part of a 50-state strategy to increase Obama's popular vote and "hopefully provide a mandate from the populace."

He said he had no inside information, however, on the strategy behind the television ads.

The Obama campaign in Chicago did not immediately reply to an e-mail request for comment.

State Republican chairman Gary Jones suggested that since Obama was not coming to the state before the Nov. 4 general election, buying TV ads could be a way of appeasing Democrats who feel neglected by the Obama campaign.

Also, Jones said, Obama is flush with cash. "He has so much money he doesn't know how to spend it."

He said he does not think there has been much movement toward Obama in Oklahoma, such as has developed in other states won by George Bush in 2004 and now seen as possibilities for Obama.

Both Holmes and Green predicted Obama will do better in Oklahoma than polls suggest.

Holmes said the telephone polls are not capturing many enthusiastic young supporters of Obama who rely on cellular phones instead of traditional land lines.

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


October 16, 2008 Thursday
FIRST EDITION


IN CLOSE QUARTERS, IT GETS PERSONAL FINAL PRESIDENTIAL DEBATE TRUTH TEST o Candidates trade jabs on ads, policies and Joe the Plumber. AGAINST THE WALL o McCain plays aggressor early and often.


BYLINE: By Beth Fouhy The Associated Press


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A1


LENGTH: 1120 words


HEMPSTEAD, N.Y. - John McCain told Barack Obama to his face Wednesday night, "You didn't tell the American people the truth" about a key campaign pledge as the two presidential rivals slung accusations at close quarters in the final debate of their campaign for the White House.

"One hundred percent, John, of your ads - 100 percent of them - have been negative," Obama shot back a few moments later to his rival, seated only a few feet away at a round table.

"That's not true," McCain retorted.

"It is true," said Obama, seeking the last word.

McCain is currently running all negative ads, according to a study by the University of Wisconsin-Madison. But he has run a number of positive ads during the campaign.

Behind in the polls, McCain played the aggressor early and often.

He demanded to know the full extent of Obama's relationship with William Ayers, a 1960s-era terrorist, the Democrat's ties with ACORN, a liberal group accused of violating federal law as it seeks to register voters, and insisted Obama disavow last week's remarks by Rep. John Lewis, a Democrat, who accused the Republican ticket of playing racial politics along the same lines as segregationists of the past.

Struggling to escape the political drag of an unpopular Republican incumbent, McCain also said, "Sen. Obama, I am not President Bush. ... You wanted to run against President Bush, you should have run four years ago."

Obama returned each volley, and brushed aside McCain's claim to full political independence.

"If I've occasionally mistaken your policies for George Bush's policies, it's because on the core economic issues that matter to the American people - on tax policy, on energy policy, on spending priorities - you have been a vigorous supporter of President Bush," he said.

McCain's allegation that Obama had not leveled with the public involved the Democrat's decision to forgo public financing for his campaign in favor of raising his own funds. As a result, he has far outraised McCain, although the difference has been somewhat neutralized by an advantage the Republican National Committee holds over the Democratic Party.

"He signed a piece of paper" earlier in the campaign pledging to accept federal financing, McCain said. He added that Obama's campaign has spent more money than any since Watergate, a reference to President Richard Nixon's re-election, a campaign that later became synonymous with scandal.

Obama made no immediate response to McCain's assertion about having signed a pledge to accept federal campaign funds.

Asked about running mates, both presidential candidates said Democrat Joseph Biden was qualified to become president, although McCain qualified his judgment by adding the words "in many respects."

McCain passed up a chance to say his own running mate, Alaska Gov. Sarah Palin, was qualified to sit in the Oval Office, though he praised her performance as governor. Obama sidestepped when asked, saying it was up to the voters to decide.

McCain used the opening moments of the debate to accuse Obama of waging class warfare by advocating tax increases designed to "spread the wealth around." The Democrat denied it, and countered that he favors tax reductions for 95 percent of all Americans.

"Nobody likes taxes," Obama said in an exchange early in the third and final debate of a campaign nearing its end. "But ultimately we've got to pay for the core investments" necessary for the economy.

"If nobody likes taxes, let's not raise anybody's, OK?" McCain retorted with a laugh.

JOE THE PLUMBER

McCain's allegation stemmed from one of Obama's campaign appearances last weekend.

In Ohio on Sunday, Obama was approached by one man who said, "Your new tax plan's going to tax me more."

A video clip caught by Fox News shows Obama replying, "It's not that I want to punish your success. I just want to make sure that everybody who is behind you, that they've got a chance at success, too. And I think that when we spread the wealth around, it's good for everybody."

McCain referred repeatedly to that voter, Joe Wurzelbacher, a plumber from Toledo, Ohio.

Wurzelbacher watched Wednesday night's debate and said he still thinks Obama's plan would keep him from buying the small business that employs him.

About McCain: "He's got it right as far as I go."

Even so, Wurzelbacher declined to say who was getting his vote.

He said he was surprised that he was called "Joe the Plumber" repeatedly during the debate.

"It's pretty surreal, man, my name being mentioned in a presidential campaign."

McCain's reference to Ayers reprised campaign commercials he has run to try and raise doubts about Obama's fitness to serve.

Ayers, who was a member of the violent Weather Underground in the 1960s, hosted a meet-the-candidate event for Obama in an Illinois race many years later.

"The fact that this has become such an important part of your campaign, Sen. McCain, says more about your campaign than it says about me," Obama replied.

The two men debated at a round table they shared with Bob Schieffer of CBS, the moderator.

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


October 16, 2008 Thursday
FIRST EDITION


IN CLOSE QUARTERS, IT GETS PERSONAL FINAL PRESIDENTIAL DEBATE Where they strayed from the facts


BYLINE: By Calvin Woodward and Jim Kuhnhenn The Associated Press


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A1


LENGTH: 927 words


WASHINGTON - Facts went astray on tax cuts, negative campaign advertising and oil exports when Democrat Barack Obama and Republican John McCain engaged Wednesday in their third and final presidential debate.

Some examples:

THE DEFICIT

Obama: "Every dollar that I've proposed, I've proposed an additional cut, so that it matches."

The facts: The bipartisan Committee for a Responsible Federal Budget estimates that his programs would add $281 billion to the deficit at the end of his first term. The analysis includes Obama's proposals for saving money.

ENERGY

McCain: "We have to stop sending $700 billion a year to countries that don't like us very much."

The facts: This is a reference to U.S. spending on oil imports. McCain has repeatedly made this claim. But the figure is highly inflated and misleading. According to government agencies that track energy imports, the United States spent $246 billion in 2007 for all imported crude oil, a majority of it coming from friendly nations including neighboring Canada and Mexico. An additional $82 billion was spent on imported refined petroleum products such as gasoline, diesel and fuel oil. A majority of the refined products come from refineries in such friendly countries as the Netherlands, Canada, the United Kingdom, Trinidad-Tobago and the Virgin Islands.

ADS

Obama: "One hundred percent, John, of your ads - 100 percent of them - have been negative."

The facts: The statement is true when it comes to McCain's current commercial spots. But by saying McCain's ads "have been" 100 percent negative, Obama ventures into misleading territory. McCain is currently running all negative ads, according to a study by the University of Wisconsin-Madison. But he has run a number of positive ads during the campaign.

McCain: "Sen. Obama is spending unprecedented amounts of money in negative attack ads on me."

The facts: Obama is spending unprecedented amounts of money on ads, period - negative or otherwise. Obama is outspending McCain and the Republican Party by more than 2-to-1 in presidential ads. At one point in August, 90 percent of the ads Obama was airing were against McCain. A study conducted at the University of Wisconsin-Madison found that about 34 percent of Obama's ads are now negative.

TAX CUT

Obama: "I want to provide a tax cut for 95 percent of working Americans, 95 percent."

The facts: Obama constantly says this. But the independent Tax Policy Center says his plan cuts taxes for 81.3 percent of all households in 2009.

BIDEN

McCain: Said of Obama's running mate Sen. Joe Biden: "He had this cockamamie idea of dividing Iraq into three countries."

The facts: Biden actually proposed dividing Iraq into three semiautonomous regions, not separate countries. He was a prime sponsor of a nonbinding Senate resolution that called for Iraq to have federal regions under the control of Kurds, Shiites and Sunnis in a power-sharing agreement similar to the one that ended the 1990s war in Bosnia.

THE BAILOUT

McCain: "Now, we have allocated $750 billion. Let's take 300 of that billion and go in and buy those home loan mortgages and negotiate with those people in their homes, 11 million homes or more, so that they can afford to pay the mortgage, stay in their home."

The facts: Ordering the government to buy up bad mortgages to cut homeowners' monthly payments might sound good, but experts are skeptical. They say the plan McCain is promoting is unlikely to solve the housing crisis that's pushing the economy toward recession. One big problem: The vast majority of the toxic home loans that are clogging financial markets and freezing up credit have been repackaged into complex investments that the government would be hard-pressed to unravel and buy. And the government could end up paying far more than they would ever be worth. That could primarily help banks and lenders with taxpayer money.

---

- Obama, on McCain's advertising

"That's not true."

- McCain's answer

"It is true."

- Obama's response

"Sen. Obama is spending unprecedented amounts of money in negative attack ads on me."

- McCain

- McCain

- Obama's response

- Obama

- McCain's response

"Every dollar that I've proposed, I've proposed an additional cut, so that it matches."

- Obama

- McCain

- Obama

"We need to know the full extent of that relationship."

THIRD EDITION


DIGEST


BYLINE: FROM NEWS SERVICES


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A4


LENGTH: 511 words


WASHINGTON

Obama endorsement costs Buckley his job

A week after endorsing Democrat Barack Obama for president, Christopher Buckley is leaving National Review, the conservative magazine founded more than 50 years ago by his father, William F. Buckley Jr.

"Within hours of my endorsement appearing in The Daily Beast, it became clear that National Review had a serious problem on its hands," Buckley wrote in a blog at thedailybeast.com. "So the next morning, I thought the only decent thing to do would be to offer to resign my column there. This offer was accepted - rather briskly!"

NEW YORK

Minor-party candidates will debate on Sunday

Minor-party presidential candidates finally will have their own debate: Sunday at 7 p.m. St. Louis time at Columbia University in New York.

The debate will include independent Ralph Nader, the Green Party's Cynthia McKinney and the Constitution Party's Chuck Baldwin. Libertarian Party nominee Bob Barr said he had a scheduling conflict, but debate organizers say he wanted to appear only with Nader.

Barack Obama and John McCain are also invited.

GENEVA

Iran parliament speaker dismisses 'Bomb Iran'

An Iranian leader takes no offense at John McCain's jokingly turning the refrain of the song "Barbara Ann" into the phrase "Bomb Iran."

Reporters have questioned McCain about it. Barack Obama has criticized him for it.

But Ali Larijani, Iran's parliamentary speaker and former chief nuclear negotiator, said Tuesday in response to a reporter's question in Geneva, "We don't have any objection to them singing a song."

But a real bomb, he said, would be no laughing matter. "We are confident that if they make that mistake they will be awakened from their sleep and they will face a nightmare," Larijani said.

WASHINGTON

Obama ads turn up in video games

Barack Obama has become the first presidential candidate to buy ad space inside a video game.

Nine video games from Electronic Arts Inc., ranging from the extremely popular "Madden '09" football game to the street racing "Burnout: Paradise," feature in-game ads from the Obama campaign. The ads appear on billboards and other signage.

WASHINGTON

ACORN defends registration practices

The community organization ACORN defended its voter registration practices amid new allegations of voter fraud and a call from Republican lawmakers to investigate irregularities.

In Ohio, Barack Obama told reporters that the group's registration problems should not be used by the GOP as an excuse to keep voters from turning out on Election Day.

ACORN has registered 1.3 million young people, minorities and poor and working-class voters, the group says.

MIAMI

Doctors ask McCain for medical records

More than 90 doctors in Florida have signed a petition calling on McCain to fully release his medical records, suggesting that more about his bouts with skin cancer needs to be disclosed.

McCain, 72, would be the oldest president to take office if elected in November. His campaign dismissed the petition (which has nearly 2,900 signatures) as a stunt and noted it's driven by a group with ties to the liberal Moveon.org.

BOSTON

Independent group ads are fewer this campaign

Independent political groups, some of which made big splashes in the 2004 race, are playing reduced roles in this year's presidential campaign, the Boston Globe reports.

There's less money pouring into nasty negative television advertising from outside groups than in 2004.

Evan Tracey, president of the Campaign Media Analysis Group, said, "(Obama) doesn't need them, and (McCain) doesn't want them."

CHICAGO

Michelle Obama tries to stay near home

Michelle Obama tries to organize her campaign travel so that she's home on the weekends and gets home during the week before her daughters go to bed at 8:30 p.m.

During the day, the girls' grandmother helps out. When both parents are out of town, the girls stay with a fellow working mother, Yvonne Davila.

WASILLA, ALASKA

Palin's future son-in-law holds rare interview

Levi Johnston, 18, held a rare interview to say he is not being forced to marry Bristol Palin, 17.

Standing in the driveway of his family home, Johnston also said he dropped out of high school to take a job on the North Slope oil fields as an apprentice electrician.

He said the MySpace page on which he was said to claim, "I'm a ... redneck," and, "I don't want kids," was a joke his friends created years ago and that he had nothing to do with it.

WASHINGTON

Biden on 'Nightline'

In an interview scheduled to air Monday night on ABC-TV's "Nightline," Joe Biden said it was "fair enough" for Republicans to question Obama's relationship with 1960s antiwar radical Bill Ayers but attacked the way they did that.

Biden suggested it would be OK if Republicans noted that Obama was only 8 years old when Ayers co-founded a violent radical group but said they objected to Obama's serving on a nonprofit organization board with him a quarter-century later. "But to run an ad saying Barack Obama ... consorts with terrorists, and put this guy's picture up? Come on! That's over the top."

Briefly

- Negative study: A study of all presidential ads in the largest 186 TV markets by the University of Wisconsin says that nearly every John McCain ad from Sept. 28-Oct. 4 was negative, compared with 34 percent of Barack Obama's.

- Support study: Congressional Quarterly says John McCain supported President George W. Bush in Senate votes 90 percent of the time during Bush's eight years in office, though the figure rose to 95 percent in the most recent year.

- Endorsement: Barack Obama received the endorsement Monday of the Brady Campaign to Prevent Gun Violence. The group is named after Jim Brady, the press secretary to former President Reagan, who was wounded by a gunman who tried to assassinate Reagan in 1981.

- McCain on Lewis: John McCain said that it was unfair for Rep. John Lewis to compare the negative tone of the Republican presidential campaign to the atmosphere a segregationist fostered in the 1960s.

- Clinton report: Hillary Clinton is sending out press releases emphasizing that she is campaigning for Barack Obama. She was in Pennsylvania on the weekend and Monday. She also wants it known that by her count she has raised $10 million for the Obama campaign.

- Also in: Jonathan Allen, 45, announced he is a candidate for president of the United States. He is vice president and general manager of Galiso Inc., a provider of hydrostatic testing systems for compressed gas cylinder manufacturers and re-testers worldwide, but will make the time to run.

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


October 14, 2008 Tuesday
Correction Appended
THIRD EDITION


McCain: 'Got them where we want them' GOP candidate says he's the man to face enemies abroad and to restore economy.


BYLINE: By Rob Christensen and Ryan Teague Beckwith McClatchy Newspapers


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A3


LENGTH: 505 words


WILMINGTON, N.C. - John McCain, conceding he is behind in the presidential race, cast himself Monday as the fighter America needs to face its enemies abroad and to restore the economy at home.

"The national media has written us off," McCain told 2,500 people at Cape Fear Community College in Wilmington. "Senator Obama is measuring the drapes.

"But they forgot to let you decide. Friends, we got them just where we want them. What America needs in this hour is a fighter."

McCain's visit to Wilmington was his first public appearance in North Carolina in the general election - a state where his Democratic opponent, Barack Obama, has campaigned five times and sharply outspent him on advertising and staffing. Obama has aired a television ad specific to North Carolina, linking the closure of Carolina Mills factories in Maiden, N.C., to McCain's support for free-trade measures.

McCain is being forced to play defense in North Carolina and in Virginia - two upper-tier Southern states that have been reliably Republican in recent presidential elections. McCain sent running mate Sarah Palin into North Carolina last week, and she is scheduled to return for a fundraiser in Greensboro, N.C., and possibly other events Thursday. Plans are being laid for McCain to attend a rally in Charlotte on Saturday.

And after Monday's rally, McCain went to a residential area in Wilmington to film a campaign commercial.

The Wilmington event drew an enthusiastic crowd that chanted "U-S-A" and "McCain" but did not include the personal insults about Obama that recently prompted McCain to defend his Democratic opponent. About a dozen Barack Obama supporters outside the event got into a brief shouting match after the rally with McCain supporters on the grass. Shouts of "Nobama" were matched by "Yes We Can."

McCain compared Obama with President Herbert Hoover, who was in office when the country went into the Great Depression in 1929. He said Obama's proposal to raises taxes would be the wrong remedy for an economy in decline. (Obama has said he would raise taxes only on those who make more than $250,000 per year and would reduce taxes on the middle class.)

"The last president to raise taxes and restrict as Senator Obama proposes was Herbert Hoover," McCain said. "They say that those who won't learn the lessons from history are doomed to repeat them."

While casting Obama in the role of Hoover, McCain seemed to portraying himself as a sort of conservative Franklin Roosevelt. McCain said he had known fear and doubt - an allusion to his days as a POW in Vietnam.

"I know what fear feels like," McCain said.

But Paul Cox, Obama's North Carolina spokesman, said McCain is the one who hasn't learned history's lesson - at least recent history.

"Why does he continue to endorse the failed economic policies of George W. Bush?" Cox asked. "Folks are looking for answers and John McCain is not providing them."

LETTERS FROM READERS YOUR VIEWS

Clean energy, green jobs are a big part of the answer to Missouri's job problem

The story "Jobless rate here is higher than in U.S.; hard-hit manufacturing sector is a key" (Oct. 1) noted that our local unemployment rate is higher than the national average. An economic crisis, driven in part by skyrocketing energy costs, is pummeling our labor force and raising serious questions about how we can get the economy moving again and put people back to work.

But for all the recent attention to bailing out Wall Street, the real issue is how can we create jobs here in Missouri. How we can break our dependence on foreign oil, a dependence that has increased the cost of everything from a loaf of bread to a tank of gas, forced companies to lay off employees and sent billions of dollars overseas to oil-producing countries, pushing America deeper in debt?

Part of the solution should be investing in clean-energy technology and creating green jobs. A major investment in clean-energy technologies such as wind and solar energy would create more than 43,000 jobs in Missouri, according to a recent study from the Center for American Progress. These are jobs for which people already are trained. For example, the Sheet Metal Workers' International Association, in cooperation with the Sheet Metal and Air-Conditioning Contractors National Association, has been training energy management technicians for more than 20 years. These members are proficient in auditing energy-using systems and indoor air quality in homes, schools, hospitals and businesses.

Investment in green jobs will benefit many other skilled tradesmen in Missouri and around the country.

By investing in producing more energy here in America and increasing energy efficiency, we can break our dependence on foreign oil and create jobs here at home. That's a story that ought to be on the front page.

Dan Andrews | St. Louis Coordinator, St. Louis Sheet Metal Workers' JATC

Nothing less than free and open debate

Sylvester Brown is a good writer and I enjoy his columns, but I disagree with him about the seriousness of Democratic presidential nominee Sen. Barack Obama's alleged abuse of Missouri law enforcement ("Election cycle and spin cycle go hand in hand - on our dime," Sept. 5).

Here are the facts: On Sept. 23, KMOV-TV reported that two high-profile prosecutors were joining the Obama campaign's so-called "Truth Squad."

KMOV-TV's Russell Kinsaul reported that, "The Obama campaign is asking Missouri law enforcement to target anyone who lies or runs a misleading TV ad," and reporter John Mills said, "They also say they plan to respond immediately to any ads and statements that might violate Missouri ethics laws."

As soon as I saw this report, I issued a statement condemning the Obama campaign for using Missouri law enforcement to intimidate voters. I did so from my office because the issues are civic and public and not political in nature. It is fine to form a truth squad, but the group the Obama campaign has formed amounts to a police squad. If any campaign attempted to intimidate voters, I would condemn that campaign, including the McCain-Palin campaign.

KMOV-TV's executive news director Sean McLaughlin defended the station's report in "KMOV's report on Obama 'truth squad' stirs up controversy" (Sept. 30). St. Louis County Prosecutor Robert McCulloch claimed in a letter to the editor that the report was false.

I will leave it to Mr. McCulloch and the station to settle the dispute. In the meantime, I will continue to speak out against any attempt to intimidate voters or chill free and open debate.

Gov. Matt Blunt | Jefferson City

Bridge to somewhere

I am happy to hear that Wachovia Foundation is granting $2.5 million to the St. Louis Zoo for a new south entrance ("Makeover at zoo's south gate to feature pedestrian bridge," Oct. 7), but is this not the same Wachovia that initially agreed to a Citigroup buyout for $2.1 billion, backed by the federal government, as a result of its bad debt ratio?

Something just does not seem right. But at least it is a bridge to somewhere.

Mark Richardson | St. Louis

These machines still are in place in St. Louis and Kansas City. The secretary of state has done nothing about it.

This is appalling. I call on Secretary of State Robin Carnahan to decertify these machines immediately and do whatever she can to help these cities switch to optical-scan machines before the November presidential election.

Gail Michaels | University City

More than just transit

"Yes to Proposition M" (Oct. 5), an editorial supporting the Metro transit system, addressed urban and economic development issues but neglected to mention how support for the MetroLink affects environment and health issues in the St. Louis metro area.

The looming service reductions not only would result in diminished riding opportunities for the general public, but also would exacerbate the gridlock in which commuters languish daily during rush hour. This is on top of the fact that the entire state was designated recently as being in "non-attainment" of the 24-hour fine particle National Ambient Air Quality Standard and that the never-ending construction on U.S. Highway 40/Interstate 64 basically ensures area-wide traffic congestion and road construction pollution. These factors make respiratory health and summer ozone conditions in St. Louis a greater cause for concern for even the healthiest among us.

Support for MetroLink is crucial for the future prospects of an environmentally and economically sustainable St. Louis. Even if you could not care less about these incontrovertible warnings, the question remains: Who will St. Louis area residents blame when driving to work is too expensive and public transportation is a distant memory?

Ben Anderson | St. Louis

The new law requires health insurance companies to include these costs in their programs. Who will pay for the consultations for all the drug addicts and alcoholics? You and I soon will see our health insurance costs increase because of this. We all will all feel the pinch.

Our investments have tanked, and our savings are worth less every time the Federal Reserve lowers the interest rate, and it's all because the younger generation wants everything whether it can afford it or not.

It's OK to say, "Don't worry, everything will be back to normal in five or six years," but how about us who are living on 401(k)s, savings and (God forbid) the stock market?

John Schroeder | Wildwood

Politicians must stop quibbling like a bunch of old hens and tell the voters specifically what they would do if elected.

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


October 12, 2008 Sunday
FIRST EDITION


Obama holds to calm and careful approach


BYLINE: By Charles Babington THE ASSOCIATED PRESS


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A4


LENGTH: 779 words


COLUMBUS, Ohio - Barack Obama is playing it safe. Leading in polls with 25 days to Election Day, the Democratic nominee is offering careful proposals to address the economic crisis while letting allies respond to John McCain's sharpest charges.

Obama now reads his speeches from teleprompters, reducing the chance of gaffes. He has not held a news conference in two weeks, although he has done several one-on-one interviews with national and local reporters.

He now refers to Republican John McCain as "my opponent" more often than by name. And he offers carefully limited, comparatively noncontroversial remedies for the nation's financial crisis.

Publicly, Obama's aides say he keeps a calm demeanor and measured tone because he doesn't want to fuel the anguish and panic caused by the economic meltdown. Privately, they acknowledge there is no desire to shake up a campaign dynamic that is inching him closer to the White House.

"I don't like to yell," Obama told more than 10,000 people in Columbus on Friday, his fifth large rally in hotly contested Ohio in two days. He was referring to a sound-system glitch, but it could have been a metaphor for his home-stretch strategy.

"He's responding just right, and the polls are reflecting it," said Sen. Sherrod Brown, D-Ohio, who campaigned with Obama this week and helped lead the counterattacks against McCain. When GOP vice presidential nominee Sarah Palin spoke in Ohio on Thursday, Brown said, she spent too much time on issues such as Obama's ties to Vietnam War-era radical William Ayers, now a college professor in Chicago.

"People are saying, 'What about our jobs, what about the banking situation?'" Brown said.

On Friday as McCain rolled out a new TV ad with his sharpest language yet about Ayers, the sharpest Democratic response came from Obama's running mate, Joe Biden, who told an audience in Springfield, Mo., that McCain was trying to "take the lowest road to the highest office in America."

CAREFUL BALANCE

Obama is seeking a careful balance these days. He criticizes details of McCain's chief economic proposals, and he briefly and broadly disputes Republican attacks on his character, not getting into details. That seems to satisfy Democratic stalwarts who feel recent nominees were too slow to respond to character attacks.

Obama devotes more time to explaining his own long-standing proposals for tax cuts and energy investments. On Friday, he added a temporary program of tax breaks, low-interest government and government-backed private loans for small businesses having trouble borrowing to meet payrolls, maintain inventories or expand.

When this careful rhetoric threatened to bore crowds seeking rhetorical fireworks and when the economic problems turned into a crisis, he added more upbeat lines to his stump speech.

"Now is not the time for fear," Obama said at every Ohio stop this week. "Now is the time for resolve and steady leadership."

Unless asked, he does not mention McCain's and Palin's fiercest line of attack: that he has associated with Ayers, a former 1960s radical who helped found the violent Weather Underground.

Increasingly, high-profile supporters take the sharper jabs at McCain before Obama comes on stage. On a sunny street with a few thousand people in Chillicothe, Ohio, on Friday, Ohio Gov. Ted Strickland played the part.

"The McCain-Palin campaign and some of their followers unfortunately want you to be afraid of Barack Obama," he said, adding that "others" had spread untruths about the nominee.

Obama does not leave all the heavy lifting to surrogates. Referring to McCain and Palin, he told the Chillicothe audience, "it's not hard to rile up a crowd by stoking anger and division." He said Americans wanted "someone who can lead this country" in a time of economic crisis, not divide it.

Noting that McCain advisers have said their candidate will lose if the campaign's focus stays on the economy, Obama said: "So in the last couple of days, we've seen a barrage of nasty insinuations and attacks, and I'm sure we'll see much more over the next 25 days. We know what's coming. ... But it won't work."

Obama is playing it safe on policy, too, avoiding the far-reaching proposals that have caused McCain headaches. McCain's call this week for the government to buy bad home-loan mortgages at full face value and renegotiate them at a reduced price set off loud protests from conservatives and liberals alike.

Obama's proposals have been more limited. His proposal Friday to temporarily extend an expiring tax break that lets small businesses immediately write off investments would cover only investments up to $250,000. It would cost the Treasury $900 million, Obama aides said.

SPRINGFIELD, MO.

Biden accuses McCain of taking 'lowest road'

Democratic vice presidential candidate Joe Biden said Friday that Republican John McCain is trying to "take the lowest road to the highest office in America," with ads and rhetoric questioning Barack Obama's character and judgment.

"Every single false charge, every single baseless accusation is an attempt to stop you from paying attention to what is affecting your daily lives, to what is happening at your kitchen table," Biden said at a rally in Springfield, a Republican stronghold in Missouri.

WASHINGTON

Political watchdog calls for inquiry over ads

A campaign watchdog group filed a complaint Friday with federal regulators against two groups, alleging they violated the law by airing political ads during the presidential race.

Democracy 21 wants the Federal Election Commission to investigate the American Issues Project, which ran a $2.8 million ad campaign against Democratic presidential nominee Barack Obama, and the American Leadership Project, a group that backed Sen. Hillary Rodham Clinton in the Democratic primaries and ran about $4.3 million in ads supporting her or against Obama.

The complaint argues that both groups violated the law by not operating as a political action committee, which would have restricted their fundraising to capped donations. The American Leadership Project was largely financed by unions that supported Clinton. The American Issues Project ad against Obama was paid for by Texas billionaire Harold Simmons, a McCain fundraiser.

Both groups say they comply with federal requirements.

ST. JOSEPH, MO.

Biden blasts McCain plan

for troubled mortgages

Democratic vice presidential candidate Joe Biden said Thursday that Republican John McCain keeps switching responses to the economic crisis and now advocates a plan for troubled mortgages that would "reward banks and lenders for their greedy behavior."

Democrats have increasingly tried to paint McCain as lurching from one position to another in a bid to persuade voters that he would be capable as president of addressing the nation's economic woes.

"Now he's gone to the point of actually wanting to reward banks and lenders for their greedy behavior," Biden said. "Ladies and gentlemen, this is not a steady hand."

McCain has proposed to devote $300 billion - nearly half the recent financial rescue package passed by Congress - to buying troubled mortgages at face value from financial institutions. McCain promotes it as a way to help homeowners.

WASHINGTON

Obama buys half hour

in prime time on Oct. 29

Already advertising at record levels, Barack Obama has scheduled a half-hour commercial for prime time on Oct. 29, six days before Election Day.

Obama campaign officials said the campaign had secured a 30-minute block of time at 8 p.m. on CBS and NBC. CBS was already juggling its prime-time lineup to accommodate the Democratic presidential candidate, moving back an episode of "The New Adventures of Old Christine."

Such a vast purchase of commercial time is a multimillion-dollar expense, but Obama has been spending dramatically on ads, overshadowing rival John McCain and the Republican National Committee.

FIRST EDITION


McCain presses link to Ayers He questions Obama's candor; Democrat says opponent is seeking to 'score cheap political points.'


BYLINE: The Associated Press


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A4


LENGTH: 362 words


Waukesha, Wis. - Republican presidential candidate John McCain said Thursday that questions about Democratic rival Barack Obama's association with a former war protester linked to Vietnam-era bombings are part of a broader issue of honesty.

In his strongest personal criticism since his faltering campaign began casting Obama as an unknown and unacceptable candidate, McCain told supporters that Obama had not been truthful in describing his relationship with former radical William Ayers.

Loud cheers from 4,000 people gathered at a sports complex near Milwaukee greeted McCain's attacks over Ayers, who helped found the Weather Underground, a Vietnam protest group that bombed government buildings 40 years ago.

"We need to know the full extent of the relationship," McCain said. Later, McCain told ABC News: "It's a factor about Sen. Obama's candor and truthfulness with the American people."

Obama has denounced Ayers and his violent actions and views. He dismisses McCain's criticism as an effort to "score cheap political points."

The AP and other news organizations have reported that Obama and Ayers, now a college professor who lives in Obama's neighborhood, are not close but that they worked together on two nonprofit organizations from the mid-1990s to 2002. In addition, Ayers hosted a small meet-the-candidate event for Obama in 1995 as he first ran for the state Senate.

David Axelrod, a senior campaign adviser, says that Obama, who was a child living in Indonesia and Hawaii in the late 1960s and early 1970s, was not aware of Ayers' radical past at the time of that campaign event.

To press its argument, the McCain campaign also released a 90-second Internet ad about Obama and Ayers.

"Barack Obama and domestic terrorist Bill Ayers. Friends. They've worked together for years," the ad says. The ad also claimed that one of the nonprofits on which Obama and Ayers worked was a radical education foundation.

That educational foundation was The Annenberg Challenge; it was funded by the Annenberg Foundation, a charity set up by longtime Republican backer and newspaper publisher Walter Annenberg. Annenberg has since died, but his wife has endorsed McCain this year.

ATLANTA

25 pct. of teen girls have received cancer vaccine

One in four teen girls has rolled up her sleeve for the relatively new vaccine against cervical cancer, federal health officials said Thursday.

The Associated Press State & Local Wire


October 10, 2008 Friday 8:29 PM GMT


Biden: McCain trying to distract voters


BYLINE: By CHRIS BLANK, Associated Press Writer


SECTION: POLITICAL NEWS


LENGTH: 696 words


DATELINE: SPRINGFIELD Mo.


Vice presidential candidate Joe Biden asserted Friday that Republican John McCain's campaign is trying to distract voters from the nation's financial woes by attacking Democrat Barack Obama.

"Every single false charge, every single baseless accusation is an attempt to stop you from paying attention to what is affecting your daily lives, to what is happening at your kitchen table," Biden said at a campaign rally in Springfield, a traditional Republican stronghold in the swing state of Missouri.

McCain's campaign launched a TV ad Friday that raises questions about Obama's ties to an anti-Vietnam war radical. The ad states: "When convenient, he worked with terrorist Bill Ayers. When discovered, he lied."

The ad follows up on McCain's criticisms of Obama while speaking at a town hall forum in Wisconsin. McCain said Thursday that Obama hasn't been transparent in explaining links to Ayers and told ABC News that "it's a factor about Sen. Obama's candor and truthfulness with the American people."

GOP vice presidential candidate Sarah Palin has been even more blunt. She has accused Obama of "palling around with terrorists."

Ayers is a Chicago college professor who helped found the violent Weather Underground in 1969. The group is blamed for bombing government buildings in the early 1970s.

Obama and Ayers live in the same Chicago neighborhood and worked together on two nonprofit organization boards from the mid-1990s to 2002. Ayers also hosted a meet-the-candidate event for Obama in 1995 as he first ran for the Illinois state Senate.

Biden on Friday never specifically mentioned Ayers or the new ad. But he said McCain is trying to avoid dealing with the nation's financial woes and is instead trying to "take the lowest road to the highest office in America."

A spokeswoman for McCain's campaign said questions should be asked about Obama's connections to Ayers and the group Association of Community Organizations for Reform Now. Election officials in several states have accused ACORN of submitting false or duplicative voter registrations.

"Barack Obama continues to demonstrate through his relationships with Ayers, ACORN and others that he lacks the judgment and credibility we need in a president," spokeswoman Wendy Riemann said. "It's time Barack Obama was honest with the American people about these relationships."

Biden urged voters to force the presidential campaign to focus on the economy, health care and other issues that directly affect their lives.

Recent polls show McCain and Obama are running about even in Missouri, which has voted for the presidential winner in every election but one over the last century.

Attendees at the rally said that they have become increasingly worried about the nation's economy. Springfield resident Kenny Knauer, who said he's been a Democrat since Missouri picked Adlai Stevenson instead of Republican President Dwight Eisenhower in 1956, said that it really doesn't matter what Obama and McCain plan on doing unless they first fix the economy.

"You can't solve any other kinds of problems until you solve that," said Knauer, who has been working with local Democrats to put up campaign sings and canvass.

The nation's financial problems have been felt in Missouri, where the unemployment rate is at its highest mark in 17 years. Many of those lost jobs have been in the manufacturing sector.

While Biden spoke in Missouri, the stock market continued a weeklong slide. The Dow Jones industrials lost 128 points, while the Nasdaq composite index posted a modest gain.

Biden spoke on a sunny but windy day from a temporary stage erected in a city park near a minor league baseball stadium. The speech ended Biden's two-day swing through four Missouri cities, located in counties that supported President Bush in 2004. Springfield is the home of GOP Gov. Matt Blunt, and the southwest corner of the state has historically been Republican territory.

Biden committed a gaffe while introducing Springfield Democrats at the start of his Friday remarks. He referred to state Rep. Charlie Norr as "Claire." During a previous visit to Missouri, he asked state Sen. Chuck Graham to "stand up" before noticing the lawmaker's wheelchair.

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


October 9, 2008 Thursday
SECOND EDITION


Debate was a missed opportunity for voters POINT OF VIEW


BYLINE: COLLEEN CARROLL CAMPBELL


SECTION: EDITORIAL; Pg. B9


LENGTH: 689 words


There was no clear winner in Tuesday night's presidential debate, but there were clear losers: America's undecided voters.

It was their one chance to see Sen. John McCain and Sen. Barack Obama onstage together interacting directly with voters and spontaneously answering the questions of ordinary citizens. Instead, they heard mostly a rehash of stump speeches and claims and counter-claims already repeated ad nauseum on the campaign trail.

Billed as a town-hall-style debate, the event's stilted format yielded few memorable or illuminative moments. Scared-stiff audience members read their questions from note cards after those questions were screened and selected by moderator and former NBC News anchor Tom Brokaw. Brokaw inserted many of his own piercingly personal, straight-from-the-heartland queries, such as "Who do you have in mind for Treasury secretary?" and "Would you give Congress a date certain to reform Social Security and Medicare within two years after you take office?"

Instead of a free-wheeling, wide-ranging debate in which the candidates engaged directly with voters on a broad spectrum of issues, the gathering featured canned discussion of only a few topics, mostly confined to wonkish exchanges on economic and foreign policy.

The debate was the latest in a series of disappointments this electoral season. There were the early "Jeopardy"-style primary debates, in which a slew of candidates competed to see who could squeeze the most headline-grabbing phrases into 60-second sound-bites. Then there was the overhyped CNN/YouTube Republican debate, which fell short of its populist billing when it became clear that CNN staffers had picked the questions and turned the "people's debate" into a forum for Democratic partisans. The late-season primary debates between Sen. Hillary Clinton and Obama were punchier and more revealing, and Gov. Sarah Palin's outside-the-Beltway persona enlivened the recent vice-presidential debate, making it the second most-watched debate in history. But Tuesday's presidential debate marked a return to the disappointing status quo.

The moderators have been a big part of the problem, but the presidential campaigns also share the blame. They negotiated the straitjacket rules that have made direct engagement and rebuttal so scarce in these stage-managed, contrived affairs.

As a consequence, American voters miss out. We miss the chance to see the candidates offer unrehearsed responses to deeper questions about their governing philosophy, life experiences and personal values, including the cultural and character issues so often dismissed as a distraction by Beltway moderators.

Missing from our superficial debates are such questions as: What does this candidate think is the role of government in a citizen's life? Does he see government as the answer to our problems or as the problem itself? Does he worry, as I do, about our coarsened popular culture, the disintegration of our families, the violence on our streets and protecting the innocence of our children? Does he have a plan for bringing our country together amid such deep partisan divisions? What is that plan, and does his record show that he could carry it out? What does he believe is the root cause of the terrorist threat America faces today? Does he see the world the way I do? Can I trust him?

When we elect a president, we're not just choosing a set of policies; we're choosing the person we want to see on TV screens for the next four years, the person we want waking up to that 3 a.m. emergency call to respond to a national crisis, the person we want imposing his political will and values on government and, by extension, on our nation.

Choosing a president is a little like choosing a spouse, albeit for a shorter-term commitment: We'll be stuck with this person after Jan. 20, so we want someone we can live with long after the debates have ended, the ballots have been cast and counted and the hard work of governing has begun.

---

Colleen Carroll Campbell is an author, television and radio host and St. Louis-based fellow at the Ethics and Public Policy Center. Her website is www.colleen-campbell.com.

CAMPAIGN DIGEST


BYLINE: FROM NEWS SERVICES


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A3


LENGTH: 506 words


BETHLEHEM, PA.

McCain, Palin question Obama's record

Republicans John McCain and Sarah Palin questioned on Wednesday whether Barack Obama's record matched his rhetoric as they sought to sow doubt about the Democratic presidential candidate.

"He's not willing to drill for energy, but he's sure willing to drill for votes," Palin said, eliciting cheers of "Drill, baby, drill" from the crowd, which often interrupted the candidates during their joint appearance.

McCain's remarks about Obama were interrupted with shouts of "socialist," "terrorist" and "liar." At another time, a man in the bleaches shouted "No more ACORN," referring to a group that registers poor voters.

NASHVILLE, TENN.

CNN poll indicates strong showing by Obama

John McCain dismissively called rival Barack Obama "that one," Obama mocked McCain's "Straight Talk Express," and both left the debate stage to return to the campaign trail Wednesday.

CNN's national poll of debate watchers found that 54 percent said Obama did the best job, compared with 30 percent who said McCain performed better. While 51 percent of those polled said they had a favorable opinion of McCain, unchanged from before the debate started, 64 percent said they had a favorable opinion of Obama, up 4 percentage points from before the debate.

TAMPA, FLA.

Biden again links McCain to Bush

Democratic vice presidential candidate Joe Biden called Republican John McCain an angry man making ugly attacks against the Democratic ticket to cover up his support for President George W. Bush.

"You can't call yourself a maverick when all you've ever been is a sidekick," Biden said Wednesday of McCain. He credited Pennsylvania Sen. Bob Casey with first using the line.

WASHINGTON

NRA plans to use Clinton criticism of Obama

The National Rifle Association is turning to Hillary Rodham Clinton to bolster its criticism of Barack Obama's positions on gun issues.

The NRA's Political Victory Fund planned a national newspaper ad Thursday reviving a Clinton mailing that accused Obama of waffling on gun issues.

WASHINGTON

Contributions to Obama called into question

Democratic presidential candidate Barack Obama has raised about $3.3 million from contributors who did not list a home state or who designated their state with an abbreviation that did not match one of the 50 states or U.S. territories, according to records provided by the Federal Election Commission.

Most of those contributors idenitifed themselves as living in foreign cities. Under federal law, foreign citizens cannot make political contributions, but U.S. citizens living abroad can.

The Republican National Committee filed a complaint with the Federal Election Commission on Monday asking for an investigation of Obama's foreign contributions, among other things.

Briefly

- Palin's e-mail: David Kernell, the son of a Democratic Tennessee state lawmaker, pleaded not guilty Wednesday of hacking the e-mail account of Republican vice presidential candidate Sarah Palin last month. He is accused of posting some information from the account to a website.

Prosecutor clears the air about 'truth squad' reports

A recent report on KMOV-TV completely distorted what I and others said about potential campaign ads. We said that if ads were false, we would say so. Period. The statement immediately morphed into something sinister, starting with KMOV's lead-in to the story and continuing through recent letters to the editor with stops along the way at various blogs and radio talk shows. To clear the record, let me state unequivocally:

Russell Kinsaul of KMOV-TV said in his lead-in, "The Obama campaign is asking Missouri law enforcement to target anyone who lies or runs a misleading TV ad." That is absolutely false.

Matt Drudge and Rush Limbaugh said (by way of Gateway Pundit), "[Bob McCulloch and St. Louis Circuit Attorney Jennifer Joyce] are threatening to bring criminal libel charges against anyone who levels what turns out to be false criticisms of their chosen candidate for president." That is absolutely false, and never mind that there is no such animal as criminal libel.

Gov. Matt Blunt said, "What Obama and his helpers are doing is ... abusing the justice system and offices of public trust to silence political criticism with threats of prosecution and criminal punishment." Absolutely false.

Local radio hosts continue to spread these falsehoods without checking the facts or asking for clarification from me.

Neither I nor any other person made any of the above statements, nor did I or any other person say anything from which a responsible journalist could infer that we said or even intended the above. The mere fact that my statement of "... if they're not going to tell the truth then somebody's got to step up and say, 'wait a minute that's not true' ..." becomes "... threatened to file criminal libel charges" clearly demonstrates the need for someone to step up and call out those who intentionally distort or ignore the truth.

For 25 years as an assistant prosecuting attorney and the prosecuting attorney, I have worked diligently to protect the freedoms of all people in our community. I always have done my very best to earn and retain the trust of the people of St. Louis County and the metropolitan area I serve. I never have been, nor will I ever be, afraid or hesitant to stand up and speak out on issues important to the people of my community.

What the letter writers and their cohorts - Mr. Blunt, Mr. Drudge and Mr. Limbaugh, et al - must realize is that they are not the only people in this country who enjoy the freedoms and rights of the First Amendment. The rights apply to all people, including me.

Robert P. McCulloch | St. Louis County Prosecuting Attorney, St. Louis County

Unions, high wages go hand in hand

The article "Group seeks more minorities on construction jobs" (Oct. 1) noted the Missouri Department of Transportation's success in placing minorities on construction jobs on the U.S. Highway 40/Interstate I-64 project. What the article failed to note is that a report published the same day by the Transportation Equity Network found that construction wages in St. Louis, controlled for the cost of living, are the highest in the nation. St. Louis also has highest rate of unionization in construction in the nation.

The report, by the University of Missouri-St. Louis' Public Policy Research Center, found that unionization is highly correlated with construction wages. Construction jobs offer great opportunities for disadvantaged individuals who have not gone to college - especially if they can get into a union apprenticeship training program in St. Louis.

Todd Swanstrom | University City Des Lee Professor of Community Collaboration and Public Policy Administration, University of Missouri - St. Louis

Courage

Z. Dwight Billingsly's column "This is no time for naïvete and immaturity" (Oct. 2) reads as it it were a joke - a tragic joke. From the headline, I thought it was about Alaska Gov. Sarah Palin, the Republican vice presidential candidate. But no. The thrust was that Sen. John McCain, the Republican presidential nominee, showed incredible courage to pick Ms. Palin as his running mate. Mr. Billingsly does not mention issues, causes or facts.

Some issues he missed: The occupation of Iraq and Afghanistan, the deregulation that caused the economy to tank, the "war on terror" that is nothing but a money pit for Vice President Dick Cheney's cronies and the CIA that was operating better before all the Sept. 11, 2001-related changes.

It was despicable that Mr. Billingsly was branding Sen. Barack Obama, the Democratic nominee for president, as naïve when Mr. Billingsly defines courage as approving of death and watching the Bill of Rights, the economy and our respect in world go down the toilet.

Jon Jung Echols | St. Louis

No more deregulation

Sen. John McCain's health care plan would give a $5,000 tax credit to American families to spend on health insurance and tax employer-provided health care as income to employees. This tax would cause employers to cut employer-provided health care and leave Americans searching for private policies.

My husband and I are students who work part-time. We had to find independent health insurance. We couldn't afford the policy offered by my university. We found health insurance through a national company for $220 a month. We have dental coverage, but we couldn't afford maternity insurance now. We pay $15 a month for generic medicine, but if we require a prescription for which a generic is not available, our insurance pays about $3 on a $60 prescription. The company that provides our health care does not provide vision insurance. We spent $600 on our eye exams and contacts this spring. We pay $25 co-pays on office visits. We do our best not to get sick. Our yearly expenses for just our health insurance coverage, for two healthy 20-somethings, is $2,640.

Recently, a woman in our family died unexpectedly. She had worked part-time and provided health insurance for her husband, a farmer. When she died, her employer offered to continue the husband's coverage at $440 a month. That's $5,280 per year for one person. Mr. McCain's $2,500 tax credit wouldn't even cover that.

Deregulation has proven to be a failure on Wall Street; I can't imagine the effects it will have on health care.

Meggie Fox Biesenthal | Wentzville

Three legs of culpability

The indoor sport du jour seems to be assigning blame for the current economic crisis. In my view, blame for the financial morass rests squarely on the support of a three-legged stool of culpability.

The first leg, of course, is the financial institutions that created investment instruments out of thin air, assigned them a value and marketed them to a legion of greedy suckers. Their motivation was short-term gain with no regard for predictable future damage.

The second leg is the U.S. government that failed to write rules and assign referees to govern and control this economic free-for-all. Was something sinister going on here, or were our public servants simply blinded by the illusion of prosperity?

The third leg of culpability in the financial disaster is the person in the mirror. We have become a society addicted to easy credit. We have buried our noses in the free-lunch trough for so long that we have come to believe in it.

So, here we are. What do we do now?

We don't bail out the people who created the toxic financial game; we should throw them out. The financial system must be saved, but the people who gamed the system must be gone.

We insist on rules and enforcement mechanisms necessary to mete out swift and appropriate punishment to any future transgressors.

And we take stock of ourselves and the naievete that led us to believe in and demand a free lunch. In the final analysis, our financial institutions and government are a reflection of us.

David A. Baetz | Innsbrook

Serving their own

David Brooks is correct in saying that American leadership is scarcer than credit ("A leadership crisis makes things even worse," Oct. 1). Unfortunately, however, he misses the primary cause of the crisis: the failure of House Speaker Nancy Pelosi to lead. Ms. Pelosi and her associates have been so thoroughly engulfed in hatred for President George W. Bush that crucial issues of the American people have been ignored.

Ms. Pelosi and U.S. Rep. Barney Frank, D-Mass., have been incompetent. They insisted that Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac were doing fine - right up to the point of near-failure of both institutions. They accepted political donations from these institutions and pressured banks to approve loans to people who had neither jobs nor money to pay the mortgage. Mr. Frank should resign in disgrace.

Our representatives in Washington need to start representing the people, not serving their own needs.

G. Iverson | St. Louis County

Fritz Korte | Pacific
