October 6, 2008 Monday
THIRD EDITION


Obama says McCain camp stooping to smear tactics


BYLINE: By Charles Babington The Associated Press


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A12


LENGTH: 485 words


ASHEVILLE, N.C. - Barack Obama's allies warn that John McCain's attacks on the Democrat's character will lead to the political equivalent of mutual assured destruction: Fire your big weapon at your own peril.

Several Obama surrogates said his supporters may start reminding voters of McCain's ties to Charles Keating, a convicted savings and loan owner whose actions two decades ago triggered a Senate ethics investigation that involved McCain as one of the "Keating Five."

The warnings of massive retaliation came as McCain's running mate, Alaska Gov. Sarah Palin, took on the role of attacker and said that Obama sees America as so imperfect "that he's palling around with terrorists who would target their own country." She was referring to an early Obama supporter, 1960s radical Bill Ayers, a founder of the Weather Underground whose members were blamed for several bombings when Obama was a child.

Obama has denounced Ayer's radical views and activities. But he's not above questioning McCain's character with loaded words.

On Sunday, Obama unveiled a TV ad on the economy that paints McCain was "erratic in a crisis." Some see that as a reminder of McCain's age, 72.

Obama also alluded to harsher tactics in a speech Sunday to thousands of people in Asheville, N.C.

McCain and his aides, Obama said, "are gambling that he can distract you with smears rather than talk to you about substance. They'd rather try to tear our campaign down than lift this country up. It's what you do when you're out of touch, out of ideas and running out of time."

Obama has denounced Ayers' radical views and activities. Ayers hosted a small gathering for Obama in 1995. Obama and Ayers live in the same Chicago neighborhood and served on a charity board together.

Just months into his Senate career, in the late 1980s, McCain made what he has called "the worst mistake of my life." He participated in two meetings with banking regulators on behalf of Keating, a friend, campaign contributor and S&L financier who was later convicted of securities fraud. The Senate ethics committee investigated five senators' relationships with Keating. It faulted McCain for "poor judgment."

In the new Obama ad, an announcer says: "Our financial system in turmoil. And John McCain? Erratic in a crisis. Out of touch on the economy."

The ad, slated to start running today on national cable, alludes to McCain's response to the nation's financial crisis. He briefly suspended his campaign, called for a White House summit meeting that ended chaotically and showed varying degrees of support for the massive rescue bill Congress passed Friday.

Palin repeats claim

Meanwhile, in Burlingame, Calif., Palin defended her claim that Barack Obama "pals around with terrorists," saying the Democratic presidential nominee's association with a 1960s radical is an issue that is "fair to talk about."

Palin launched the attack Saturday and repeated it Sunday.

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


October 5, 2008 Sunday
FOURTH EDITION


Election cycle and spin cycle go hand in hand - on our dime


BYLINE: SYLVESTER BROWN JR.


SECTION: METRO; Pg. E1


LENGTH: 984 words


Like a reliable Maytag, politicians are known for spinning the truth.

Gov. Matt Blunt recently wrote an opinion piece schooling the Springfield News-Leader on "free speech," "constitutional rights and the integrity of law enforcement."

Great principles but, it turns out, a tad off-topic.

Blunt's letter appeared the same day (and in the same newspaper) as an editorial accusing him of using taxpayer dollars to release press statements that support the Republican presidential ticket. Describing Blunt as a "political assassin," the editorial charged the governor with "stinking up his Web site with partisanship and propaganda."

Ouch!

To comprehend all this, let's analyze the cycle of spin.

On Sept. 23, KMOV (Channel 4) reported that Barack Obama was getting help from local prosecutors to clarify misleading TV ads. Obama's Missouri "Truth Squad," the report said, included U.S. Sen. Claire McCaskill, St. Louis Circuit Attorney Jennifer Joyce, St. Louis County Prosecuting Attorney Bob McCulloch and Jefferson County Sheriff Glenn Boyer.

Republicans, both locally and nationally, hit the roof. Obama enlisted prosecutors to intimidate voters and squelch free speech, they claimed. Conservative pundit Rush Limbaugh's website reported that the Truth Squad threatened "to prosecute anti-Obama speech." The state GOP campaign accused Obama's campaign of creating a "police state in the Show-Me state."

The outrage seemed feigned.

Since the primaries, both Obama and McCain have employed such tactics to counter negative attacks. McCain has enlisted the aid of powerful Republican officials to serve on his Truth Squads, including New Hampshire's attorney general.

In the KMOV report, no Democrat interviewed said anything about using prosecutorial power on Obama's behalf. McCulloch and Joyce explained that their goal is only to get truthful information to voters.

Jefferson County's Sheriff Boyer was infuriated by insinuations that he'd arrest voters for their political views. The Vietnam veteran said the accusations were nothing but "spin for political purposes."

Even KMOV's John Mills, who reported the story, said the GOP twisted his report.

Heaven forbid a little thing like "truth" get in the way of a juicy allegation that might fire up the base.

Shortly after the firestorm, Blunt issued a statement on the state's website accusing Obama, McCaskill, Joyce, Boyer and McCulloch of "abusing the justice system ... to silence political criticism with threats of prosecution and criminal punishment."

Nine days after the KMOV report aired and the accusations had been largely refuted, Blunt still maintained that the Democrats used "the language of enforcement" and attached the "stench of a police state to Missouri's civic life."

In his News-Leader op-ed piece, the governor said the newspaper ignored "the assault on free speech."

Let's give the governor the benefit of the doubt. The Obama campaign did in fact stress the involvement of prosecutors.

Justin Hamilton, spokesman for Obama's Missouri campaign, explained: "We reached out to respected members of the community who would donate their personal time to help keep the campaign focused on the issues."

Still, when introducing the KMOV report, an anchor said, "The Barack Obama campaign is asking Missouri law enforcement to target anyone who lies or runs a misleading TV ad during the presidential campaign."

It seems naive for a governor, but Blunt did write that he based his statement on "news reports" that "exposed" Obama's plans.

But didn't he in fact commit the very same infraction he's putting on his political opponents - blending politics and a public office? Blunt used the state website to name Democratic names and support a word war launched by his party. Taxpayer dollars support government offices, including websites.

Blunt's statements were "fact-driven," Rich Chrismer, Blunt's spokesman, countered in response to my questions.

"In his op-ed, the governor goes into detail about why he condemned the actions of the Obama campaign because the issues are civic and public and not political in nature," Chrismer said.

Sounds like a little dual-action spin to me.

Surely, there's a rule prohibiting an elected official from using his power and office for partisan favors.

"I can't give you an accurate answer to that question," said Stacey Heislen, interim executive director of the Missouri Ethics Commission.

Heislen said she couldn't recall any similar violations investigated by the commission. And even if she could, she'd be hesitant to discuss them - it might influence someone interested in filing a claim on the matter, she explained.

I followed Heislen's advice and called the Missouri attorney general's office. One would think that the attorney general, Jay Nixon, a Democrat running for governor, would have something to say on this issue. But a representative declined to address the subject. It should fall under the purview of the Missouri Ethics Commission, he told me.

David C. Kimball, associate professor of political science at UMSL, chuckled when I told him about my runaround for a definitive answer.

Kimball said he was no ethics lawyer, but he couldn't recall any hard-and-fast rules. He did, however, seem to find a measure of delight in Blunt's position.

"There's certainly a high amount of irony in the governor using his letterhead to accuse others of interjecting partisanship into their official duties, isn't it? Unfortunately, irony is lost on politicians, particularly during election times."

Elected officials, be they Democrat or Republican, play partisan games. The only real recourse is voting offending politicos out of office, Kimball said.

But I still think there ought to be a rule banning partisan politicking on the taxpayers' dime and time but, hey, what do I know? I'm just a voter.

I guess on Election Day, we'll see how Blunt's Maytag worked. We'll also see if the stains of politics have permanently set.

ST. LOUIS

Polls give Biden edge in debate with Palin

Joe Biden won Thursday night's vice presidential debate at Washington University, according to two national polls.

A CBS News/Knowledge Networks survey of 500 uncommitted voters taken after the debate reported that 46 percent thought Biden won, while 21 percent gave Republican Sarah Palin the victory. A separate CNN/Opinion Research Corp. survey reported similar views, with 51 percent saying Biden did better, to 36 percent favoring Palin. The CNN survey reported that 87 percent thought the Delaware senator was qualified to be president, while 42 percent saw Palin that way.

Far more people watched Thursday's vice presidential debate on television than watched the first presidential debate. Nielsen Media Research says preliminary ratings in the nation's 55 biggest markets were up 42 percent from the same measurement of John McCain and Barack Obama's first encounter last Friday.

LAS VEGAS

Jury begins deliberating in O.J. Simpson trial

A jury began deliberating Friday on whether O.J. Simpson and his co-defendant robbed two sports memorabilia dealers at gunpoint in a casino hotel room.

The 61-year-old former football star and a golfing buddy, Clarence "C.J." Stewart, each face five years to life in prison if convicted of kidnapping, or mandatory prison time if convicted of armed robbery. They've pleaded not guilty to 12 charges, including conspiracy, coercion and assault with a deadly weapon.

WASHINGTON

Experts play down danger in food scare

Tiny traces of melamine, the chemical that has set off a global food safety scare, are not harmful in most foods, except baby formula, government experts said Friday.

The Food and Drug Administration said Friday its safety experts have concluded that eating a minuscule amount of melamine - 2.5 parts per million - would not raise health concerns, even if a person ate food every day that was tainted with the chemical.

"It would be like if you had a million grains of sand and they were all white, and you had two or three that were black, that's kind of the magnitude," said Stephen Sundlof, director of the FDA's food safety program.

The FDA guideline is meant to help federal and state investigators checking for contaminated foods from China at ports of entry and in Asian community groceries around the country.

THIRD EDITION


President appears on behalf of Hulshof


BYLINE: By Jo Mannies Post-Dispatch Political Correspondent


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A15


LENGTH: 572 words


President George W. Bush made a short stop in St. Louis County on Friday to help raise $1.5 million for fellow Republican Kenny Hulshof, one of several signs that the national GOP is paying closer attention to the state.

Republican National Committee Chairman Robert M. "Mike" Duncan also was in the area Friday to attend the fundraiser and to encourage volunteers at the party's joint campaign headquarters in Maryland Heights.

"Missouri is a target state," Duncan said in a brief telephone interview. "You're going to see an RNC presence here until Election Day. We're doing very well here."

He discounted recent polls, including several for the Post-Dispatch, that show Hulshof trailing Democratic opponent Jay Nixon and Democratic presidential candidate Barack Obama about even against Republican John McCain.

Analysts and many activists in both major parties say McCain must carry Missouri to win the White House.

McCain's campaign indicated as much earlier this week, when leaders announced that his campaign was withdrawing workers, money and ads from Michigan and shifting them to a handful of other key states, including Missouri.

As of last week, Obama was outspending McCain by a huge margin for TV ads in Missouri. The latest figures by TNSMI-Campaign Media Analysis Group, a nonpartisan firm monitoring TV ad spending for the Post-Dispatch, showed Obama spending at least $369,035 on ads airing in Missouri last week, compared with $115,752 for McCain. The Republican was running ads only in St. Louis and Kansas City, while Obama's ads were airing statewide.

Duncan said he was heartened by the thousands who turned out Thursday night for a rally featuring Republican vice presidential nominee Sarah Palin at St. Louis University's Chaifetz Arena. The rally followed Palin's 90-minute debate with the Democratic running mate, Joe Biden, at Washington University. Duncan said the rally turnout underscored his confidence that Missouri will go for McCain.

"The people of Missouri match up with the values of the McCain-Palin ticket," Duncan said.

Missouri Democratics disagree. On Friday, the party and labor launched the first of several events in the coming days aimed at highlighting the plight of area workers who have lost jobs.

"They're shipping our jobs to Canada, and we've heard nothing from the Bush administration," said Joe Shields, president of United Auto Workers Local 110. Shields expects to lose his job soon as a result of Chrysler's decision to curb production at one Fenton plant and close another by Oct. 31.

Hulshof was accompanying Bush on Air Force One. Hulshof spokesman Scott Baker said the congressman was thrilled to get the president's help, but noted that the two had their differences. Earlier Friday, Hulshof had voted against the $700 billion bailout package for financial institutions that Bush had supported.

---

St. Louisan Gets Honor

President George W. Bush presented the President's Volunteer Service Award to Eric Greitens during his visit to St. Louis Friday.

Greitens, a lieutenant in the Navy Reserve and a Navy SEAL, started the Center for Citizen Leadership after returning from active duty that took him to Iraq, Afghanistan, Africa and Southeast Asia. The St. Louis-based nonprofit, which Greitens started with his combat pay and help from two friends, helps wounded and disabled soldiers pursue careers in public service.

Greitens, a Parkway North High School graduate, lives in St. Louis.

LETTERS FROM READERS


SECTION: EDITORIAL; Pg. C12


LENGTH: 1380 words


Truth squad blurs lines between personal belief, professional duties

Democrats, Republicans and independents alike should be concerned that the Obama campaign launched a truth squad, composed in part of Sen. Claire McCaskill, St. Louis Circuit Attorney Jennifer Joyce and St. Louis County Prosecutor Bob McCulloch, which plans to challenge those people who allegedly say anything erroneous about Sen. Barack Obama, the Democratic nominee for president ("KMOV's report on Obama 'truth squad' stirs up controversy," Sept. 30).

A truth squad like this dangerously blurs the lines between the personal beliefs and professional obligations of elected officials, further complicated because some "truths" are subjective.

In a television interview, Mr. McCulloch shrugged off concerns, saying it was unlikely that anyone would be prosecuted. That makes the issue of a crusading partisan squad no less serious. Its ultimate effect is to dampen free speech, engender distrust of neighbors and frighten the general population, which isn't in a position to sort through the technicalities.

Gov. Matt Blunt, talk show host Rush Limbaugh and the Drudge Report have raised concerns about affect of these tactics on freedom of speech.

If there should be a necessity for a truth squad, it should be bipartisan and composed of people from a range of disciplines and experiences, such as political scientists, ethicists, religious leaders and educators without ties to one party or campaign. If anything, we need to encourage more people to talk openly and freely, not less.

Joan Lipkin | St. Louis

Censure

I hope that the Post-Dispatch upholds our constitutional right of freedom of speech by censuring the threats of the St. Louis County prosecuting attorney and the St. Louis circuit attorney to challenge advertisements that mischaracterize Sen. Barack Obama, the Democratic nominee for president. This is outrageous!

Ed Will | St. Charles

Voters decide

I wish the St. Louis Democratic "truth squad" would spend some time rooting out voter fraud, rather than trying to determine if a candidate's ad is truthful or not. Aren't the voters suppose to decide who is telling the truth - at the polls?

Don Pixley | Ballwin

Intent of letter

"No super sound bites - and no knockout blow either" (Sept. 27), an analysis of the first presidential debate, included Republican presidential nominee Sen. John McCain's story about General Dwight D. Eisenhower's supposed draft letter of resignation to be used in case the Normandy invasion did not succeed. Mr. McCain used it as "an effective way to bring up the issue of responsibility in Washington." Effective, perhaps, but also historically inaccurate.

As a former archivist at the Eisenhower Presidential Library, I have seen the actual "in case of failure message." You can see it, too, on the library's website. After lauding the bravery and devotion to duty of the troops, Eisenhower did take responsibility: "If any blame or fault attaches to the attempt it is mine alone." But there was no mention of any intention to resign. He would have, I surmise, seen resignation as a shirking of his responsibility to continue what he called the Great Crusade.

This is not the first time Mr. McCain has used this story. Why, if it was going to be used in major speeches and the debate, was this story not fact-checked? Is he more interested in "effect" than "truth"?

Karen Holzhausen | New Melle

Better representation

Regarding the economic bailout: Why are banks not to be held responsible for managing their own liquidity? How can it be that the taxpayer has the responsibility to attempt restoration of banks' capacity to borrow or lend to each other? The notion that banks' refusal or inability to lend or borrow has created the calamity we face is ludicrous. What self-respecting company needs to borrow to make current payroll or stock the shelves? Where do profits go if not to fund continuing operations?

It is apparent to ordinary Americans that massive fraud must be involved to create such a rapidly deteriorating financial crisis, and before any bailout is approved, the crooks, legislators included, should be identified, prosecuted and jailed. We deserve better representation from our elected officials and more honest operations from the management of our banks and other companies.

Clyde Bollinger | Festus

Better ideas

Many people are against the bailout because the public has better ideas. The philosophy of smaller government, less regulation and diminished oversight cannot be sustained. The people in charge cannot be trusted. Left to their own devices, they will find a way to line their own pockets and those of the politicians who enable their greed.

For the mortgage debacle, we can form a new temporary department. The government could purchase bad loans and renegotiate them at reasonable rates and terms. All loans 60 or more days in arrears would be transferred. All foreclosure proceedings would be frozen. This would keep owners in their homes at payments they could afford. It would free up money for spending and stabilize neighborhoods.

The banks who made the bad choices would suffer losses, as they should. They would become smaller, but with good assets and cash flow and capable of making new, ethical loans. The "spread" should be regulated, and lending institutions should pass along decreases in the prime rate instead of using them for higher profits. That way the "Fed" truly can have an impact on the availability and cost of credit and stimulate the economy.

To restore trust in the system, everyone involved should be investigated and any wrongdoing should be prosecuted. Corporate reform is needed, too. Wages and benefits for the highest positions should be limited to 50 times that of the lowest-paid employee.

The wealth needs to be spread down - all the way down. No one believes in anything "trickling down" anymore.

Mike Byington | Spanish Lake

Someone else's money

It appears that the $700 billion fleecing of the good ol' taxpayer soon will be passed. It is a sad time in our country when the many people who caused this problem - from borrowers to lenders to government hacks - are not taken to the woodshed for punishment. Instead, we're getting this bailout shoved down our throats to reward more failures of business and government, which is supposed to look out for our interests.

The only thing that the government did well in the last 100 years was to win World War II.

What bailout is next? You know there will be another one because it is easy to spend someone else's money.

Fred Daly | St. Charles

Wrong man for the job

Treasury Secretary Henry Paulson is the wrong man for the job. How can he possibly be qualified to get us out of this mess? Either he was not smart enough to see the problems coming or he was smart enough but chose to allow the problems to continue. There have been warnings from others for at least two years about the crisis. If he didn't see it coming, why would we think he suddenly has become smart enough to figure out how to get us out of the mess?

If he did see it coming and allowed it continue, was it because he and his friends would benefit personally while he kept telling us the economy and our finances were stable and strong? If this is the case, how could we possibly trust him to suddenly do the right thing now?

Mary Ann Ford | Florissant

Intestinal fortitude

Our government's $700 billion boondoggle is financially unsound, but the alterative is a depression. Sen. Judd Gregg, R-N.H., said, "This is the bottom line: If we do not do this, the trauma, the chaos and the disruption to everyday Americans' lives will be overwhelming, and that is a price we cannot afford to risk paying."

At age 85, I read his statement to mean that baby boomers and their children who have enjoyed the eras of materialism and self-indulgence the past 50 years of prosperity simply don't have the psychological stamina and intestinal fortitude to endure the suffering and hardship I saw handled by my parents' generation during the Great Depression. Only we over 75 can recall those horrors; no book or movie can come close.

This action indicates that our nation is on the edge of financial and social collapse, and no one wants to admit it.

Are we headed to national and political bankruptcy?

Bill Bandle | Manchester

Missing from campaigns: Discussion of education

The article "VP presidential face-offs have produced memorable moments" (Sept. 29) was a great read. I hope the debate produces what's been long overdue this election season: a detailed and serious discussion of public education.

A meaningful discussion about our nation's public schools is even more important now that we're grappling with the financial crisis. As the economy continues its downward spiral, states will be tempted to decrease their investment in public schools, and local schools will feel the woes of Wall Street when parents, teachers and students need real solutions for fixing our public schools, not just stale debate.

I've seen how White House and congressional emphasis on one-size-fits-all, high-stakes standardized tests has stigmatized students and sapped the enthusiasm of many educators who never envisioned teaching would become more about test-taking strategies than the actual subjects our students need to know.

A discussion about the federal role for education and how the bailout plans will affect our nation's public schools may not produce the memorable moments the media craves, but they're something parents and teachers deserve to hear.

Chris Guinther | Jefferson City President, Missouri National Education Association

Stay on track

I have read or heard this week that Alaska Gov. Sarah Palin, the Republican vice presidential candidate, is being urged to use the vice presidential debate time as a 90-minute negative ad against Sen. Barack Obama, the Democratic presidential nominee.

Fine enough, but we already know how she and the Republican Party feel about the Democrats. What we need is to hear her views, her opinions, her take on the vice presidency, her experience.

An appeal to vice presidential debate moderator Gwen Ifill: Can you please keep her on track so that we can all get a fair view of who and what this woman really is.

Barbara Brunetto | Lake Saint Louis

Know before you go

The editorial "Bracing for Election Day" (Sept. 29) reminded voters that they can help speed the polling place process by being sure that their name and address, if either has changed, are correct on the voter list.

The voting process also will go faster if voters have read and decided how to vote on the many propositions before they get to the polling place. I hope the Post-Dispatch will print all the ballot information and also have articles discussing the propositions well before Election Day.

Finally, people should watch for the postcard from the Election Board that should arrive a few days before the election. As a poll worker, I hate to tell a person five minutes before the polls close that he is at the wrong polling site. You have to be at the right place to vote. So check those cards. If one didn't come, call the Election Board before Election Day. You can even use that postcard as your required identification when checking in at your polling place.

Although it will be a very busy day, with informed voters and poll workers, the election process should go smoothly.

Gloria Bratkowski | St. Louis

We need ballot issue info

The national election coverage by the Post-Dispatch does a good job of providing information about both parties running for president and vice president. However, those of us who have chosen to vote absentee in the upcoming election need to decide soon on more than the next president and vice president.

The November ballot has a number of issues that will have people spending a great deal of time reading. The issues as written on the ballot are topics about which we are not reading in the newspaper. People will walk away from the polls wondering why judges and bonds didn't get the same coverage as national issues.

When are we going to hear about the upcoming bond issues that will affect taxes in the region? Many of these bonds would have a long-term affects on the city. When are we going to hear about the judges up for retention? Those judges will affect Missouri voters.

We must start getting information on the ballot issues in Missouri.

Ed Wagenaar | Maryland Heights

Prevention education

"For many kids, violence trumps learning" (Sept. 12), an online follow-up to "The ABC's of violence" (March 9), was another example of the growing need to incorporate prevention education into our schools. Every day our children are faced with an extremely violent world, and not all are lucky to have caring parents to help them navigate it safely.

Now that a study from Washington University shows a correlation between children witnessing violence and having difficulties in school, we must work together to give our kids the tools to handle this. With access to a solid prevention education, any child can learn how to not only keep themselves safe, but also how to make his or her world a safer place, which benefits us all.

Educating our children about how to handle life situations such as bullying, anger and discrimination is key to healthy development. When children have the knowledge and confidence to deal peacefully with these situations, they can enjoy a safer and happier childhood, which will lay the foundation to help them grow into healthy, productive adults.

There are agencies and groups in St. Louis dedicated to bringing this education to our kids. Nonprofits such as Progressive Youth Connection have been delivering violence prevention education to local schools for more than 36 years. Unfortunately, these programs are not widespread or available to many of our youth. We owe it to our children and our community to give our kids the chance for a bright, happy future. Prevention starts now.

Annie Meagle | Crestwood Progressive Youth Connection

The uninsurables

Republican presidential candidate Sen. John McCain's health insurance plan may force people with pre-existing illnesses to purchase Missouri Health Insurance Pool health insurance. If one is accepted, the monthly payments for MHIP insurance are close to $1,000 per person per month at age 63 and close to $585 per person per month at age 43. Many people with pre-existing illnesses are underemployed. Those with mental illnesses often are not employed. Other states have similar "bargains" for those who are refused by private insurers.

In Missouri, if a person with a mental illness in his medical records called a leading health insurance company, he would be told immediately that, "We won't insure anyone with a mental illness."

Mr. McCain is not aware that between MHIP and employer-provided insurance lies a no man's land for "uninsurables," those with any pre-existing illness, mental or physical. Mr. McCain, with his pre-existing diagnosis of cancer, probably could not get insurance in Missouri.

As a long-time registered nurse, I know that no health insurance leads to no preventive medical care, followed by poor health and more emergency room visits and finally to the taxpayer, who picks up the tab for those with no health insurance. Mr. McCain's plan is lacking.

Nancy R. Cooksey | Kirkwood

Reckless abandon

I recently had to sell my house to relocate for family reasons, and it cost me the equity from two previous homes, my 401(k), my personal savings and my pension to cover my negative equity position. I pleaded with my mortgage company to consider an equitable adjustment, not a bailout. It seemed that our interests were aligned. But I was treated poorly and rudely. Ultimately, I honored my obligation with nothing but lip service from a mortgage company that suggested I find a charity to help with food so that I could devote more income to covering my mortgage.

Now, our interests are closely aligned again. But, this time, I am being asked to loan money to cover the very same assets that the banks are causing to be non-performing because of their own belligerence.

These financial institutions have acted with reckless abandon and have done this country a tremendous disservice. While I recognize that our interests are aligned, I suggest that the management at the financial institutions that would become the custodians of this mammoth expenditure either are unwilling to behave in a constructive manner or are incapable of pursuing a less modest but honest return on their investments.

Perhaps I lack objectivity because I have been so affected, but maybe, just maybe, I havea perspective that recognizes that entrusting assets that amount to national treasure to this existing group of petulant adolescents that managed the collection of financial companies into crisis is worse than wasteful and would serve only to enable further misguided behavior by these management teams.

Tony Raymond | St. Charles

St. Louis Daily Record/St. Louis Countian (St. Louis, MO)


October 1, 2008 Wednesday


Missouri expecting large voter turnout for November election


BYLINE: Donna Walter


SECTION: NEWS


LENGTH: 823 words


Expecting an 80 percent voter turnout for the November election, Missouri officials say they've been working long hours to ensure that the election runs smoothly.

The hotly contested presidential race between Republican John McCain and Democrat Barack Obama is likely to spawn charges of voter fraud and voter intimidation.

Both political parties will have observers at the polling places. "We are going to do everything we can to ensure it is a fair election," said Tina Hervey, spokeswoman for the Missouri Republican Party. Hervey said the party plans to have poll watchers at the sites "that have had a history of fraud occurring. " She declined to say where those sites are and how many poll watchers the party will send out. Messages left for Obama campaign workers were not returned by press time.

To be prepared for voter challenges, Missouri Secretary of State Robin Carnahan announced Tuesday morning that $2 million in grant funding is available for local election officials to hire additional poll workers. That includes $500,000 for student poll workers, too.

Carnahan was speaking at a news conference held at the St. Louis Board of Election Commissioners in downtown St. Louis. With her were Carol A. Wilson, chairwoman of the St. Louis election board, and John Fox Arnold, chairman of the St. Louis County election board.

Missouri is used to close elections, Carnahan said, adding that she welcomes the additional scrutiny by the campaigns. "We want to make sure that they understand the laws in Missouri, and the best way to do that is to make sure poll workers are well-trained," she said.

Election officials are still recruiting poll workers. Carnahan said she wants to have about 7,000 more poll workers at the polling places on Election Day.

"If you don't have enough poll workers there, and they're not well-trained, you're going to have chaos, and you're going to have long lines whenever there is an issue," Carnahan's spokeswoman," Laura Egerdal, said in a telephone interview.

Long lines may be inevitable due to the record turnout expected, but the idea is to enable some poll workers to help voters resolve their issues while allowing the lines to move forward, according to Carnahan.

Arnold said he expects long lines to be a problem in the county, and he pointed out that Missouri law says everyone in line when the polls close at 7 p.m. has the right to vote.

Wilson said the city election board is on track to getting 2,100 poll workers, and Arnold said the county board expects to have 5,400 poll workers, including 100 workers to handle the technical issues that arise. Currently, about 208,000 voters are registered in the city and 670,000 in the county. Voter registrations are still coming in for both jurisdictions. Wilson said the city board will remain open until 7 p.m. next Monday, Tuesday and Wednesday.

The anticipated voter turnout is roughly 500,000 more than voted in the 2004 election, when 65 percent of registered voters participated, Carnahan said. About 230,000 new voter reg-istration forms have been processed, she said, although it's unclear how many of those are first-time voters and how many are name or address changes.

To guard against potential voter-registration challenges at the polls, Carnahan urged voters to verify that they are registered to vote before the Oct. 8 voter registration deadline. Missouri residents can look up that information on the secretary of state's Web site, www.sos.mo.gov. Voters who get tagged as not registered by the "Voter Information Lookup" should follow up with phone call to their local election officials.

(When I input my name and address to test the Lookup, the system said that I am not a registered voter in the city of St. Louis. My local election workers, however, checked the database and reassured me that, yes, I am properly registered. The secretary of state's office later figured out that city election workers typed my street's directional prefix in the street name field.)

"If it can't pull you up, it's just because the software doesn't like your address or the way you typed in your name. That's why it says to call," Egerdal said.

Rumors have been circulating that voters who lose their homes through foreclosure will face challenges to their right to vote.

But "that's not the law in Missouri," Carnahan said. In Missouri, people vote where they live, she said.

Carnahan's office plans to get the word out about who's eligible to vote. Egerdal said in a follow-up e-mail that the law allows voters who move within the voting jurisdiction to make ad-dress changes on Election Day and to vote either at a central location or at the polling place for the new address. Voters who move to another voting jurisdiction before the Oct. 8 deadline are to complete a new voter registration application. Those who move to another jurisdiction after Oct. 8 may vote absentee for federal and statewide races by contacting their local election authorities.

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


September 30, 2008 Tuesday
THIRD EDITION


KMOV's report on Obama 'truth squad' stirs up controversy Democrats say the group, which includes law enforcement officials, is being mischaracterized.


BYLINE: By Jo Mannies Post-Dispatch Political Correspondent


SECTION: METRO; Pg. B1


LENGTH: 647 words


A local television station's coverage of a Missouri campaign "truth squad" working on behalf of Democratic presidential nominee Barack Obama has touched off a national Internet frenzy.

Two prominent political websites - the conservative "Drudge Report" and the liberal "Daily Kos" - are battling over the purpose of the squad, which is among dozens that Obama and Republican rival John McCain have set up in various states.

Some conservative groups also are distributing video of the report that KMOV-TV (Channel 4) aired last week. And commentator Rush Limbaugh is talking about it.

What has prompted all the furor is that several members of Obama's "truth squad" in Missouri are prosecutors or members of law enforcement. They include St. Louis Circuit Attorney Jennifer Joyce, Jefferson County Sheriff Glenn Boyer and St. Louis County Prosecuting Attorney Bob McCulloch. All are Democrats.

Joyce and McCulloch are featured in a KMOV report by John Mills, in which both say their aim was to refute any false information spread about Obama.

But when KMOV anchor Russell Kinsaul introduced the report, he said: "The Barack Obama campaign is asking Missouri law enforcement to target anyone who lies or runs a misleading TV ad during the presidential campaign."

That comment seems to have sparked the controversy.

Gov. Matt Blunt, a Republican, accused the Obama camp of planning "to use Missouri law enforcement to threaten and intimidate his critics."

Blunt sent out a statement from his office in which he decried what he called "the stench of police-state tactics."

The governor repeated those accusations on national TV news.

Republican National Committee Deputy Chairman Frank Donatelli held a conference call over the weekend to call for the law enforcement officials to step down. And conservative blogs have accused the Democratic Party of trying to intimidate voters and squelch free speech.

Democrats contend that the squad is being mischaracterized. They note that several of McCain's "truth squads" in other states, including New Hampshire, include Republican officials with prosecutorial powers, including the state's attorney general.

(McCain's watchdog groups in Missouri don't have any law enforcement officials, a GOP spokeswoman said.)

Joyce said in a statement over the weekend that "my sole purpose in participating in this initiative is about getting truthful information to the voters. This has never been or never will be about prosecuting people."

Missouri Obama communications director Debbie Mesloh says the accusations are "the height of absurdity" and reflect a Republican effort "to deceive voters about the role of respected Missourians across the state who want to see this election be about the truth."

McCulloch, who could not be reached for comment Monday, told a local television station that critics have "very sinister motives." McCulloch has been a prominent political player this year. In the Democratic contest for attorney general this summer, he narrated a TV ad on behalf of state Sen. Chris Koster that is widely credited with aiding Koster's narrow primary victory.

KMOV executive news director Sean McLaughlin defended his station's report and emphasized that "nothing in the story said anything about prosecution."

McLaughlin said the report was "misinterpreted," perhaps because the public isn't used to seeing law enforcement officials talk politics in public.

The furor prompted Mills, the KMOV reporter, to post a follow-up on the station's website in which he discounted the Republican critics' claims and noted that McCain has truth squads, too.

McLaughlin attributes the uproar to politics, and recent polls showing a neck-and-neck contest in Missouri between Obama and McCain.

"We're a significant state in an important election," said McLaughlin. "That's probably what's driving it."

The upside for the station: a huge surge in traffic to its website.

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


September 29, 2008 Monday
THIRD EDITION


The big squeeze OUR VIEW | INSURANCE


SECTION: EDITORIAL; Pg. A18


LENGTH: 451 words


With the meltdown of the American financial system dominating news reports, another long-running crisis continues unchecked, squeezing American businesses and workers as never before.

Last week, the nonpartisan Kaiser Family Foundation reported that family health insurance coverage provided by an employer now costs an average of $12,680 a year. The foundation's authoritative report, based on a nationally representative survey of employers, found coverage for a single worker costs an average of $4,704 a year.

As recently as 2000, the average cost of family coverage was $6,348, while the cost of covering a single employee was $2,424.

Growth in the cost of health insurance premiums slowed this year to just 5 percent. Such good news may, in turn, slow the trend of businesses discontinuing employee health benefits. But the reduced rate of growth is the result of companies shifting an increasing proportion of insurance costs to their workers, who in many cases are even less able to afford them.

Workers will pay an average of $3,354 in premiums for family coverage this year. That's more than double the $1,543 they paid as recently as 1999. And that doesn't even count higher deductibles, co-insurance fees and co-pays.

With workers' incomes stagnating as food and energy costs soar, more American families are being torpedoed by medical costs.

The nonpartisan Center for Studying Health System Change reported last week that one in five American families - 57 million people, most of them in families that have insurance coverage - had problems paying medical bills last year. That's up from 15 percent in a 2003 survey.

More than half of these families said they had to borrow money to meet their medical expenses. About 20 percent said they considered declaring bankruptcy because of their difficulties.

Like the Kaiser report, the center's findings were based on a national survey, and both were completed before the recent turmoil in American financial markets. Today, the impact of medical bills on families could be even more severe.

It's becoming increasingly clear that the American health care system as currently organized - wasteful, inefficient and poorly designed - is unsustainable. The solution isn't more money; it's a completely redesigned system.

Health care reform has been a hot topic in political campaigns at all levels over the past year. Both Republican Sen. John McCain and Democratic Sen. Barack Obama have offered different reform plans, although neither of them calls for the fundamental transformation that many experts believe is needed.

As the debate continues, so does the squeeze on families and businesses. And the casualties, both commercial and personal, continue to mount.

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


September 29, 2008 Monday
THIRD EDITION


Passage is predicted


BYLINE: By Julie Hirschfeld Davis The Associated Press


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A1


LENGTH: 1066 words


Signs to watch o If the plan works, interest rates on short-term Treasury securities should rise, signaling banks' willingness to take on riskier types of debt - including new mortgages and student loans.

No quick fix o Rising unemployment and falling housing prices are not expected to vanish overnight, and a recession is still possible. Economists say it could be late 2009 before home prices stop falling.

---

WASHINGTON - Key lawmakers who struck a postmidnight deal on a $700 billion bailout for the financial industry predicted Sunday that it would pass Congress, putting in place the largest government intervention in markets since the Great Depression.

Flexing its political muscle, Congress insisted on a package that gives lawmakers a stronger hand in controlling the money than President George W. Bush's administration had wanted. The rescue plan casts Washington's long shadow over Wall Street, with the federal government taking over huge amounts of devalued assets from beleaguered financial firms in exchange for more oversight.

Under the plan, lawmakers could block half the money and force the president to jump through some hoops before using it all. The government could get at $250 billion immediately, $100 billion more if the president certified it was necessary and the last $350 billion with a separate certification - and subject to a congressional resolution of disapproval.

Still, the resolution could be vetoed by the president, meaning it would take extra-large congressional majorities to stop it.

The proposal is designed to end a vicious downward spiral that has battered all levels of the economy, in which hundreds of billions of dollars in investments based on mortgages gone bad have cramped banks' willingness to lend.

Lawmakers had to navigate between angry voters - many of whom view the plan as a rescue package for the wealthy on Wall Street - and Bush administration officials who warned that inaction would cause the economy to seize up and spiral into recession.

Negotiators sought Sunday to iron out the final shape of the legislation, which House Republicans still had to review. It was their fierce opposition to a federal rescue that nearly torpedoed an emerging bipartisan pact late in the week.

But officials in both parties hoped for a House vote today, and the two presidential candidates said they probably would support it.

"This is the bottom line: If we do not do this, the trauma, the chaos and the disruption to everyday Americans' lives will be overwhelming, and that's a price we can't afford to risk paying," Sen. Judd Gregg of New Hampshire, the chief Senate Republican in the talks, said Sunday. "I do think we'll be able to pass it, and it will be a bipartisan vote."

A breakthrough came when Democrats agreed to incorporate a GOP demand - letting the government insure some bad home loans rather than buy them - designed to limit the amount of federal money used in the rescue.

Another important bargain, vital to attracting support from centrist Democrats and Republicans who are fiscal hawks, would require that the government, after five years, submit a plan to Congress on how to recoup any losses.

The presidential nominees came behind the outlines of the bailout.

"This is something that all of us will swallow hard and go forward with," said Sen. John McCain, D-Ariz. "The option of doing nothing is simply not an acceptable option."

His Democratic rival, Illinois Sen. Barack Obama, sought credit for taxpayer safeguards added to the initial proposal from the Bush administration. "I was pushing very hard and involved in shaping those provisions," he said.

House Republicans said they were still reviewing the plan.

"We are not ready to say that a deal is done," said Rep. Eric Cantor, R-Va.

Congressional leaders announced a tentative deal in the early hours of Sunday morning after lengthy negotiations at the Capitol.

"We've still got more to do to finalize it, but I think we're there," said Treasury Secretary Henry Paulson, who also participated in the negotiations at the Capitol.

Executives whose companies benefit from the rescue could not get "golden parachutes" and would see their pay packages limited.

The government would receive stock warrants in return for the bailout relief, giving taxpayers a chance to share in financial companies' future profits.

To help struggling homeowners, the plan requires the government to try renegotiating the bad mortgages it acquires with the aim of lowering borrowers' monthly payments so they can keep their homes.

"Nobody got everything they wanted," said Democratic Rep. Barney Frank of Massachusetts, chairman of the House Financial Services Committee. He predicted it would pass, though not by a large majority.

Gregg said he thought taxpayers would come out as financial winners.

"I don't think we're going to lose money, myself. We may, it's possible, but I doubt it in the long run," he said.

---

Specifics of the plan

- A $700 billion taxpayer rescue of Wall Street, with $250 billion available immediately, another $100 billion upon report to Congress and the final $350 billion available only upon action by Congress.

The money would be used to buy mortgage-backed securities and other troubled assets from investment banks, commercial banks, smaller community banks, pension plans and even local governments.

- Government could use its power as the owner of the troubled mortgage bonds to facilitate modifications for the mortgages themselves.

A bipartisan oversight commission would monitor the program. If after five years there was a net loss to taxpayers, the president would have to submit legislative proposals to recoup funds from beneficiaries.

- Democrats won new, unspecified restrictions on CEO pay and executive compensations for participating companies.

Republicans won language creating a parallel insurance program that companies could choose instead of giving up their bad assets.

- Creation of warrants, which would allow any windfall coming to participating companies to be shared with the government, thus the taxpayers.

- Actions by the Treasury would be posted online in real time.

- Judicial review of the Treasury's actions.

MCT

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


September 27, 2008 Saturday
FIRST EDITION


President to attend fundraiser here for Hulshof next week Bush will appear at a private event at Hunter Farms scheduled for Friday.


BYLINE: By Jo Mannies and Mark Schlinkmann St. Louis Post-Dispatch


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A9


LENGTH: 498 words


President George W. Bush will be returning to Missouri next week to help raise money for the Republican nominee for governor, U.S. Rep. Kenny Hulshof, R-Mo.

The president's visit will be Friday, the day after the vice presidential debate at Washington University between Republican Sarah Palin and Democrat Joe Biden.

Hulshof's private fundraiser is to be at Hunter Farms, an estate in St. Louis County owned by Stephen and Kimmy Brauer and a favorite site for GOP events.

Bush's stop is among several signs that the GOP is paying attention to the state's tightening contests.

The campaign of Republican presidential nominee Sen. John McCain is "upping the number" of paid workers in Missouri, said Republican National Committee deputy chairman Frank Donatelli during a campaign stop Friday in St. Peters.

Donatelli said the additional staff reflected stronger fundraising for the GOP and its presidential ticket.

"We've gone from a Cadillac to a Rolls Royce. We had a good plan. We're top of the line now,'' Donatelli said.

The latest poll for the Post-Dispatch/KMOV-TV shows a dead heat in Missouri between McCain and the Democratic nominee, Sen. Barack Obama.

The poll of 800 likely voters, conducted Monday through Wednesday, showed 47 percent supported McCain, compared to 46 percent for Obama. The rest were undecided or supporting one of three minority-party candidates.

The poll also showed that Hulshof trailed his Democratic rival, Missouri Attorney General Jay Nixon, by nine percentage points.

Donatelli played down the poll results, as did Obama state spokesman Justin Hamilton.

But Hamilton said that the Obama operation has had a much larger field operation in Missouri than McCain for some time. "We think you definitely have to be in the state to win it,'' Hamilton said. "We've always been under the impression that this is a very competitive race in Missouri.''

Obama's campaign ad spending in Missouri has been almost twice that of McCain for the past two weeks, according to a nonpartisan ad-tracking firm.

TNSMI-Campaign Media Analysis Group, which is monitoring ad buys for the Post-Dispatch, reported Friday that Obama has spent at least $703,000 over the past two weeks for 2,646 ad spots aired on urban and rural TV stations around the state. In contrast, McCain spent $348,000 for 828 ads - which were airing only on St. Louis and Kansas City stations, the firm said.

Overall this year, Obama has spent at least $5.3 million to air TV ads in Missouri, compared to $4.6 million for McCain, the ad firm's figures show.

Obama has 40 field offices in the state, and at least 100 paid workers in the state. The campaign says it intends to knock on 100,000 doors today in a statewide effort.

The McCain campaign and the state GOP are sharing 10 offices around the states, and jointly have close to 40 paid workers, a spokeswoman said.

---

President Bush's planned visit is among several signs that the GOP is paying attention to Missouri's tightening contests.

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


September 26, 2008 Friday
THIRD EDITION


McCain stem cell ad irks conservative Christians Ad never uses the term 'embryonic,' which is at the heart of dispute about stem cells in Missouri.


BYLINE: By Tim Townsend ST. LOUIS POST-DISPATCH


SECTION: METRO; Pg. C1


LENGTH: 1302 words


A radio ad for Sen. John McCain trumpeting the Republican presidential candidate's support for stem cell research is upsetting some conservative Christians he recently won over by choosing one of their own - Alaska Gov. Sarah Palin - as a running mate.

McCain "is running the risk of alienating the people (he's) just pulled into the fold," said Glynn Young, an anti-abortion conservative from Kirkwood. "I'm mystified."

Memories of a narrow-but-devastating 2006 defeat in the battle over state constitutional protection of embryonic stem cell research still are fresh in the minds of Young and other conservative evangelicals and Catholics in Missouri.

With the new stem cell ads, McCain is tapping into ethical nuances Missouri voters are more familiar with than other voters, said Larry Weber, executive director of the Missouri Catholic Conference. "I think he does this at some risk," Weber said.

The one-minute ad - which also is airing in Colorado, Florida, Michigan, Nevada, New Mexico, Ohio, Pennsylvania and Wisconsin - never uses the word "embryonic," but the term "stem cell research" comes up four times.

"John McCain has been a champion of stem cell research and does not hide from his record," Wendy Riemann, a spokeswoman for the McCain campaign, wrote in an e-mail message to the Post-Dispatch. "Stem cell research offers tremendous hope for those that are suffering, however, John McCain feels the compassion to relieve suffering and to cure deadly disease should not erode moral and ethical principles."

Proponents of the research say that because embryonic stem cells can grow into any tissue, they hold the promise of cures for a variety of diseases.

Those who oppose the research on moral grounds say that because human embryos are destroyed when extracting the stem cells, the process is akin to abortion. They promote so-called adult stem cell research, which does not involve the destruction of embryos.

Most scientists say adult stem cell research does not hold the same promise as embryonic stem cell research. However, some scientists recently have attempted to circumvent the moral issue posed by embryonic stem cell research, including Harvard researchers who on Thursday announced a possible alternative that would not involve destroying human embryos.

"Stem cell research, to unlock the mystery of cancer, diabetes, heart disease," says a narrator in the McCain ad. "Stem cell research to help free families from the fear and devastation of illness. Stem cell research to help doctors repair spinal cord damage, knee injuries, serious burns. Stem cell research to help stroke victims."

Alta Charo, a professor of law and bioethics at the University of Wisconsin, said the ad was "designed to confuse voters."

She said that because the ad does not designate what kind of research is being discussed, it would remind champions of embryonic stem cell research of McCain's support for the science, and suggest to its critics that McCain is leaning toward continuing President George W. Bush's current policy of opposing it.

Helen Hitchcock, director of the St. Louis Catholic organization Women for Faith & Family, said the ad is confusing. "Particularly in the context of controversy in Missouri over funding embryonic stem cell research, it is most unfortunate that in this ad no distinction is made," she said.

In 2001, Bush announced that federal funds would pay only for studies on stem cell lines already in existence. Scientists have said those lines have become tainted and unusable for research. Last June, Bush vetoed legislation, for the second year in a row, that would have expanded the use of federal funding for the research.

Both McCain and Sen. Barack Obama, the Democratic presidential candidate, have said they favor relaxing Bush's restrictions on federal funds for the research. But while Obama also favors abortion rights, McCain has a long record of opposing them.

McCain also opposed embryonic stem cell research until 2001, when he changed his mind, saying he had "looked at the issue more carefully" and that he had been "educated" on it.

Riemann said the genesis of the ad was a speech by Sen. Joe Biden, the Democratic vice presidential candidate, in a Mehlville gymnasium on Sept. 9. Biden referenced Palin's pledge to advocate for disabled children if elected to the White House.

"Well, guess what folks?" Biden said. "If you care about it, why don't you support stem cell research?"

Three days later, nine St. Louis radio stations began airing the McCain ad. Voters in 16 markets across the country, including Kansas City, began hearing the ad at the same time. The McCain campaign would not say if the ad was airing elsewhere in Missouri.

Last week, radio stations in Florida, Missouri, New Mexico, Pennsylvania and Wisconsin received a stem cell ad from the Obama campaign, including multiple stations in St. Louis and Kansas City, but none in between.

Like McCain's, Obama's ad does not mention the word "embryonic," and it claims the research could "unlock" cures for diabetes, cancer and other diseases.

But Obama's ad says McCain "has stood in the way" and "opposed" stem cell research. That claim has been called misleading in the mainstream press and by the McCain campaign.

"The appeal of the McCain-Palin ticket crosses party lines and has Barack Obama so scared that he is lying about John McCain's record on stem cells," Riemann said.

A press release put out by McCain's campaign this week refuting the Obama ad heralded McCain's voting record and quoted letters the senator has written and signed in favor of embryonic stem cell research.

"John McCain, as early as this year, has indicated he's open to changing his position on stem cell research," Justin Hamilton, a spokesman for the Obama campaign, said referring to meetings McCain has had in the past year with opponents of embryonic stem cell research.

That embryonic stem cell research is even being debated in this presidential campaign at all is surprising given that McCain's position is more similar to his opponent's than it is to his own party's 2008 platform, which bans all embryonic stem cell research, public and private.

Of the four politicians atop the major party tickets, only Palin has consistently opposed embryonic stem cell research. In the 2006 Alaska gubernatorial debate, Palin said, "Stem cell research would ultimately end in the destruction of life. I couldn't support it."

Several denominations in Missouri joined hands against the 2006 ballot proposal to amend the state constitution to protect all forms of embryonic stem cell research allowable under federal law. The coalition almost pulled off an upset against the well-financed supporters of the research, and the battle scars from that fight have not healed. Some of those who took part in the Amendment 2 fight believe the McCain campaign has misunderstood the depth of anger over the issue among conservative Christians in Missouri.

"Why is he talking about this here when it's been such a hot-button issue?" asked Cynthia Haehnel, an attorney who assisted the Archdiocese of St. Louis during the Amendment 2 battle and who called the ad "upsetting."

Luke Bobo, executive board president of the Center for Bioethics and Culture Missouri, said McCain's strategy to air the ads here is "risky, quite insensitive and shows a general lack of knowledge of issues here in Missouri."

Not all opponents of embryonic stem cell research in the state thought the McCain ads were a mistake.

"I would caution everyone from over-reacting just because the words 'stem cell' were used," said Kerry Messer, a lobbyist for the Missouri Baptist Convention. "There's no issue here other than you can be sure Obama's followers will use this as a wedge issue."

To lower crime, focus on education

As kids start a new school year, we know that many of them are learning to be productive citizens. But far too many will drop out before getting diplomas. As police chief, I know that this makes the job of law enforcement harder; there is a clear link between high school graduation and the likelihood that a person will commit violent crime.

High school dropouts are three and a half times more likely than graduates to be arrested and eight times more likely to be incarcerated. The anti-crime group Fight Crime: Invest in Kids Illinois recently released a report showing that increasing graduation rates by just 10 percentage points would prevent 150 murders and 8,100 aggravated assaults every year in Illinois.

Fortunately, we know what will help boost graduation rates. Getting kids started on the right track early in quality preschool programs makes them more likely to graduate and less likely to end up in our jails. A study of the High/Scope Perry Preschool program in Michigan showed that at-risk kids who participated were 44 percent more likely to graduate, compared to those who did not attend.

We need to reach kids early. Giving them the skills to succeed in school will make our streets safer in the long run.

Col. Richard E. Miller | Granite City Chief of Police, Granite City Police Department

Playing with our future

Regarding "Missouri board seeks big boost in funding for higher education" (Sept. 16): Missourians need to speak up and tell the Legislature to stop playing politics with our future. Being ranked 47th in the United States in state appropriations per capita is an embarrassment. The one thing that can be said about the track record of funding of education in our state is that we are consistent: consistently bad.

Too many times have Missourians or the Legislature used the magic wand of "formulas" to calculate school appropriations. We have many excuses for not funding our schools and universities. Some of the excuses could be racial, religious or vouchers, taxes (I no longer have children in the schools or universities), it doesn't benefit me, etc.

What we have to understand is that if we do not invest in our intellectual future, we will have nothing. Having a strong educational system from First Steps through the university level is paramount to Missouri's future.

Allowing individuals to become certified to become an educator in Missouri by taking a few online courses indicates the priority of education in our state. Would we allow this for a someone who wanted to become a lawyer or engineer?

The question we should ask is not "what high school did you attend?" but "from what university did you graduate?"

J. Douglas Hippler | Ladue

John Czarnecki | Bonne Terre, Mo.

Shutting it down

It's not a big surprise that the Post-Dispatch came out in support of the very, very misleading bill that just passed the House of Representatives regarding offshore drilling ("A hiccup in time," Sept. 18). The bill supposedly allows expansion of offshore drilling when, in truth, it shuts it down.

The nuts and bolts are this: Drilling within 50 miles of the coast is banned. In the Gulf of Mexico, this is where the majority of the drilling is done. Between 50 and 100 miles, where there is a good amount done in the Gulf, an adjacent state can block drilling. More than 100 miles offshore, where there is only a smattering of drilling done in the Gulf, drilling would be allowed. The idea and effect here is to kill any real move to increase offshore drilling.

Gary Duff | Granite City

A recent poll found that the issues of most concern to voters were the economy, terrorism and health care. It's amazing that the cause of all these problems was not mentioned. The Iraq war, which is an ill-advised attempt to steal another country's oil, not only is responsible for our economic problems, including lack of funding for health care and for repair of our infrastructure, but it also has nurtured the growth of terrorism from local fringe movements to a world-wide "holy war" against Western civilization.

It is time we bring our troops home and concentrate on our internal national issues. We can fight the war on terrorism in ways much better than direct military action.

William A. Joern | Sunset Hills

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


September 25, 2008 Thursday
THIRD EDITION


Straight-ticket voting is no longer option in Missouri


BYLINE: JO MANNIES


SECTION: METRO; Pg. B3


LENGTH: 762 words


As Missourians begin absentee voting this week, leading up to the Nov. 4 general election, they'll notice something different with the ballot.

For the first time in a presidential election, Missouri voters no longer can simply check or punch one box to vote for all candidates in their preferred party all the way down the ballot.

Known as "straight-ticket voting," the vote-once-vote-for-all option had been standard in Missouri for decades until the Legislature outlawed it in 2006.

Ken Warren, a political science professor at St. Louis University who has studied the effects of the straight-ticket option, says there's no doubt that its abolition will be noticed in Missouri's election-night tallies this year.

"It's going to take a lot more time for people to vote," Warren said. "Some will simply quit and go home."

Some political activists in both major parties also privately agree that the lack of the straight-ticket option this fall could shorten the coattails of the two major-party candidates for president, Republican John McCain and Democrat Barack Obama.

The upshot: The party with the victorious presidential candidate might not see its down-ballot candidates benefit.

First, a little history:

Of the 2,765,067 ballots cast in Missouri in the last presidential contest in November 2004, almost half - at least 1,101,488, according to an incomplete tally - used the straight-ticket box.

The partisan breakdown of those ballots: roughly 54 percent Democrat, 46 percent Republican.

Although the straight-ticket option has been credited with presidential coattails (Republican Ronald Reagan in 1980 and Democrat Bill Clinton in 1992), 2004 was the first year state officials kept records on its use. That also was the year when prominent Republicans began publicly calling for its elimination.

State Sen. John Loudon, R-Ballwin, was among the leaders of that drive. Among other things, he asserted that the straight-ticket voting option had become a particularly powerful political tool for Democrats in St. Louis County, the state's largest bloc of swing votes.

Roughly 60 percent (172,520) of the county voters who favored Democrats in the 2004 presidential election cast straight-ticket ballots. That compared to about 45 percent (111,343) of the county voters who cast pro-Republican ballots.

The Legislature outlawed the straight-ticket voting option in 2006, a move widely seen as an attempt to hurt down-ballot Democratic candidates in a pro-Democratic year.

The ban didn't appear to harm the victorious Democrats at the top of the 2006 statewide ballot: now-U.S. Sen. Claire McCaskill and now-state Auditor Susan Montee.

However, there was noticeable voter drop-off: 66,000 Missouri voters cast a vote for U.S. Senate, but not for state auditor. The decline continued farther down the ballot.

This year, with polls now showing a slight edge in Missouri for McCain, there's talk that the lack of a straight-ticket option will hurt the GOP candidate for governor, U.S. Rep. Kenny Hulshof, who continues to trail his Democratic opponent, Attorney General Jay Nixon, in recent polls.

Hulshof spokesman Scott Baker disagrees, saying that the lack of a straight-ticket option on the ballot could help Hulshof peel off Democratic voters. "Kenny got a number of crossover votes in 2006," Baker said.

State Democratic Party spokesman Zac Wright sees no impact. "Regardless of the layout of the ballot, people are hungry for positive change," he said.

SLU professor Warren, for one, predicts that the voter drop-off could be dramatic.

The question is: Which down-ballot races do they ignore?

A lot of political futures will hinge on the answers.

China on their minds

For weeks, Obama has been hammering McCain in Missouri with a TV ad that ties McCain to the outsourcing of U.S. jobs to China.

Now, Nixon has followed suit with an ad that hits Hulshof over congressional votes deemed pro-China.

The state Democratic Party points to the loss of at least 45,400 Missouri jobs to China since 2001.

Hulshof spokesman Baker countered that China is Missouri's fourth-largest export market, and asserted that Nixon is putting at risk $9.6 billion in related economic activity in the St. Louis area.

Nixon spokesman Oren Shur said the issue is the loss of Missouri jobs to what he calls unfair trade deals Hulshof backed in Congress.

Such attacks on trade with China could collide with the local bipartisan effort to persuade that nation to consider Lambert-St. Louis International Airport for a cargo hub to transport goods.

But until Nov. 4, expect China to continue to be a popular political target.

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


September 21, 2008 Sunday
FOURTH EDITION


The Ides of September


SECTION: EDITORIAL; Pg. B2


LENGTH: 718 words


The Ides of September 2008 someday may be recalled as the time when the American economy faced its greatest challenge since the stock market crash of 1929 ushered in the Great Depression.

The nation's response will test us in ways that 9/11 hasn't, at least not so far. We can't outsource this one to the military. As America reorganizes its financial markets, every American will feel the pain, some far more than others. Jobs may be harder to come by, loans will be harder to obtain, prices may be higher and paychecks may be smaller.

For every "master of the universe" bond trader who won't get a seven-figure bonus this December, there will be a dozen or more people considerably farther down the food chain who won't be able to pay the rent.

That's why it's crucial that Congress, the Bush administration, the banking industry, federal regulators and even presidential campaigns get this one right.

The response must be prompt but also fair. It must be immediate but also thorough and thoughtful. The usual politics of self-interest must go by the board: No special deals for special friends.

Congress and the administration, Democrats and Republicans, must come together in a bipartisan - indeed, non-partisan - effort to remake the financial markets in a way that rewards capital but doesn't punish those who work for a salary. The system must be transparent, risks must be known and regulation must be tough.

The remedy should come in at least two stages. The first would create, by month's end, a federal entity to take bad mortgage loans off the books of banks at a fair price. This entity would negotiate with debtors with the goal of allowing them to keep their homes, and then sell the strengthened loans back into the commercial market. Such a move should help restore confidence in the credit markets.

This step should be followed early next year by new enforcement laws and strengthened regulatory agencies. This effort would be led by the new president, either Republican John McCain or Democrat Barack Obama, which means voters must weight carefully each man's ideas about the economic challenges and remedies.

Mr. McCain is an awkward position. A longtime foe of government regulation, he tried to repackage himself as an economic populist last week, even as the storm surge of events swept over him. On Tuesday, he said he was opposed to a government takeover of American International Group. The next day, after the government took over the huge insurance firm, Mr. McCain allowed as how it might have been a good idea.

Mr. Obama, after a halting start, was back on his game Friday in Miami. Surrounded by his economic advisers and appearing determinedly presidential, Mr. Obama exuded a kind of calm confidence. To his left, as if to remind people of the boom economic times of the 1990s, stood former Clinton administration Treasury Secretary Robert Rubin.

We'd feel better about that if we didn't know that Mr. Rubin had made his pile as an arbitrageur at Goldman Sachs and if he'd done more to rein in Wall Street excesses when he headed the Treasury Department. To be sure, very few people either on Wall Street or in Washington have acknowledged their responsibility for this mess. Nor, for that matter, did many of us in the news media pay much attention as problems grew.

That's why we'd suggest that Warren Buffett be asked to help reorganize the financial markets - even though he's 78 years old and hardly needs the job. The Oracle of Omaha, the chairman and CEO of Berkshire Hathaway, Inc., not only is the richest man in the world, but he also predicted the current debacle in 2002.

In his letter to shareholders that year, Mr. Buffett warned of Wall Street's dangerous infatuation with financial derivatives, those contracts that add risk upon risk. Derivatives based on mortgage-based securities are what crashed the financial system.

"I view derivatives as time bombs, both for the parties that deal in them and the economic system," Mr. Buffett wrote, predicting, "these instruments will almost certainly multiply in variety and number until some event makes their toxicity clear. Central banks and governments have so far found no effective way to control, or even monitor, the risks posed by these contracts. In my view, derivatives are financial weapons of mass destruction. . . ."

WASHINGTON

Obama leads McCain ... in August fundraising

Democratic presidential nominee Barack Obama outraised and outspent Republican rival John McCain in August, according to reports filed Saturday with the Federal Election Commission.

McCain reports raising $47 million to Obama's $65 million. McCain spent $41 million in August, and Obama $53 million.

Sen. Hillary Rodham Clinton's report showed only $1.8 million in donations in August. She still owed about $9 million to her campaign vendors.

WASHINGTON

Obama ad defends his record on abortion

Democrat Barack Obama is airing a new ad that defends his abortion-rights record and says Republican rival John McCain backs a party platform that would deny abortions to victims of rape and incest.

Obama's ad answers an independent ad campaign that singles out Obama's efforts in the Illinois Senate to defeat the Born Alive Infants Protection Act.

"Now votes taken out of context, accusing Obama of letting infants die? It's a despicable lie," Obama's ad states.

As an Illinois state senator, Obama opposed three legislative efforts in 2001, 2002 and 2003 to give legal protections to any aborted fetus that showed signs of life. Obama and other opponents of the Illinois bill have noted the state already had a law to protect aborted fetuses born alive and considered able to survive. Moreover, they said, the proposed legislation would have undermined the landmark Supreme Court case on abortion, Roe v. Wade.

CASTLEWOOD, VA.

Biden assails McCain on labor front

Democratic vice presidential candidate Joe Biden says Republican John McCain is helping President George W. Bush wage war against organized labor.

Biden told a union picnic Saturday that McCain has been an accomplice in dismantling protections that safeguarded the economy and workers' rights. Hundreds of coal mining families in southwestern Virginia cheered as Biden accused Bush of putting anti-labor, corporate interests in charge of the U.S. Labor Department and the National Labor Relations Board.

WASHINGTON

Records indicate Palin's pay dropped, but rose too

On the campaign trail, GOP vice presidential nominee Sarah Palin has declared at least four times that she cut her own pay as mayor of Wasilla, Alaska.

Just-released records from the Wasilla clerk's office show Palin's pay did drop from $64,200 in October 1996 to $61,200 in January 1997. But six months later, in June 1998, it jumped to $68,000. Palin's pay dipped once more in July 1999 to $66,000, according to the records, but it went back to $68,000 three months later and stayed at that level until Palin left office in October 2002.

Palin spokeswoman Maria Comella did not have an immediate comment.
