Hidden Valley already makes wide range of public contributions

Regarding "Wildwood officials can't budge ski resort owner on plan to close" (Sept. 11): As a skier and long-time winter public information specialist for Hidden Valley Ski Area, I personally stand to lose if the ski area closes. But it's not just skiers and seasonal employees who will lose. This is a business that makes a wide range of public contributions.

A few that maybe Wildwood's administration has overlooked:

- Hidden Valley already donates public parking. The ski area allows AmerenUE to park a large fleet of vehicles used in tree-trimming on a side lot at the ski facility. That helps keep the power on for all of us.

- Hidden Valley provides the largest winter field school trip in the area. More than 5,000 kids in grades five to eight visit during winter from more than 100 schools from eastern Missouri and Southern Illinois. Many kids from rural school districts - Sullivan, Mo., to Waterloo - otherwise never would experience skiing. Most will not become skiers. The program is about building confidence and getting out of doors in winter.

- Hidden Valley is a friend to wildlife. Hidden Valley has been helping me with a personal project: keeping alive the tradition of providing homes for purple martins, a declining bird species. Hidden Valley has spent more than $1,000 in two years erecting purple martin housing, joining Forest Park and the Missouri Botanical Garden in this effort.

That Wildwood feels a need to make demands of a public payment before approving a modest expansion is absurd.

John Miller | Brentwood

Leave the deer alone

Would you take someone's life just because there might be too many of us? I am a seventh-grader at Westminster Christian Academy. I was shocked to hear that people want to kill innocent deer in Town and Country. The deer lived here way before we did. They should not be murdered.

In Genesis, God told us to take care of the creatures he gave us. For many years, I have enjoyed the presence of deer in my own yard. Maren Osterholt | Town and County

Account for all POWs, MIAs

Sept. 19 is POW-MIA Recognition Day. In World War II, 20,000 American prisoners of war liberated from German camps by our Soviet allies were taken to the Soviet Union. Live sightings were reported until the late 1950s. In Korea, 8,000 prisoners of war were taken to China and the Soviet Union. Live sightings were reported into the 1960s. In Vietnam, 3,331 people held by the communist government as bargaining chips were betrayed by President Richard M. Nixon, who, after accepting a token 300 or so, declared that all prisoners of war were home. We had live sightings as late as 1989. The administrations of Presidents Gerald Ford, Jimmy Carter, Ronald Reagan, George H.W. Bush, Bill Clinton and George W. Bush have aided in the cover-up.

Today, federal buildings and post offices will fly the POW flag. A commemorative postage stamp was designed. The American Legion has an empty chair at all meetings for the prisoners of war and those missing in action. None of these has brought a single accounting of a single prisoner of war.

In this election cycle, we should ask all candidates what they will do to bring a full accounting of prisoners of war and those missing in action from World War II, Korea and Vietnam.

John P. Fitts | Noel, Mo.

Barbara O. Barnes | Sappington

Shirley F. Giebel | St. Louis

Friends and family plan

The article "In Alaska, Palin kept politics personal" (Sept. 14) had much to say about how Republican vice presidential candidate Alaska Gov. Sarah Palin appointed friends to help her in her job as governor. This must make the Democrats feel a real kinship to her, as their own John F. Kennedy surrounded himself with friends and family to help him in his cabinet (and other areas) as president.

And don't forget that former President William Jefferson Clinton appointed his wife, Hillary, to bring national health insurance to Americans.

It just shows we all need friends and family around us.

Carole Edwards | South St. Louis County

Vote accordingly

The 40th annual Phi Delta Kappa/Gallup "Public's Attitudes Toward the Public Schools" poll (published in the September issue of Phi Delta Kappan: The Journal for Education) includes this finding: "Americans view Democratic presidential candidate Barack Obama as much more supportive of public schools than Republican presidential candidate John McCain."

Those of us who support the public schools ought to take note and vote accordingly in November.

A.K. Trautwein | Overland

Fairy tales

The McCain/Palin plan to drill, particularly in Alaska, is the height of irresponsibility. There is strong evidence for global warming. There are more natural disasters all around the world, and they are increasing. Scientists have explained the reasons for the phenomenon, but Republicans prefer to ignore them. "Drill, baby, drill" won't solve our problem, but it could create additional ones.

The technology exists for us to start giving up oil - foreign and domestic - but vested interests won't allow it. Drilling for new Alaskan oil will provide only minimal, if any, relief from dependence on foreign oil, and nuclear power plants - which still have concerns about waste - will take many years to build and get up and running.

Solar, wind and thermal energy and other sources are here and are being used successfully in many places in this country and around the world. So why are Republican presidential nominee Sen. John McCain and vice presidential candidate Alaska Gov. Sarah Palin feeding us irresponsible fairy tales? Perhaps to protect our dependence on the Arabian American Oil Company in Saudi Arabia - the homeland of Osama bin Laden and most of his Sept. 11, 2001, cohorts.

Rita Warren | Olivette

Marvels of science

Scientists warmed up the largest machine ever built by man, a gigantic atom smasher, hoping to shed light on the so-called dark matter that makes up about a quarter of the universe ("Huge atom smasher, which some fear, is set to start up this week," Sept. 8). Some scientists fear that there is a slight chance that this would create a black hole that would set in motion the end of the world.

Remember when they were experimenting with the atom bomb? Many thought the bomb itself would set off a chain reaction that would blow up the world. What nonsense.

True, it obliterated two large cities in Japan, killing 200,000 or so people, but the entire world? Balderdash.

So, while the scientists fiddle with the secrets of the atom, I am fiddling with a way to travel to the safety of Mars.

Harvey Meyer | Green Park

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


September 18, 2008 Thursday
THIRD EDITION


Groups take on a new shape 'SWIFT BOAT' FALLOUT New organizations push agendas with even less oversight than in 2004.


BYLINE: By Deirdre Shesgreen Post-Dispatch Washington Bureau


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A1


LENGTH: 1177 words


DATELINE: WASHINGTON


By this time in 2004, the Swift Boat Veterans for Truth and other political advocacy groups had made a major splash in the presidential race, spending millions of dollars to sway voters' views of John Kerry and George W. Bush.

This year, the rules of the game have changed.

Federal election officials cracked down on the Swift Boat group and similar outfits after they swamped the airwaves in 2004. As a result, some "soft money" groups have stumbled out of the 2008 election gate, shutting down before the presidential election even switched into high gear.

But a new breed of political advocacy could be gaining steam.

Enter Ed Martin, the energetic and ambitious former aide to Missouri Gov. Matt Blunt and now a St. Louis lawyer who is president of the American Issues Project.

The AIP was set up earlier this year as a nonprofit, "social welfare" organization that is subject to even less disclosure than the Swift Boat-style groups.

Martin says he expects that in the coming weeks, outside groups - including the AIP and an array of other conservative and liberal organizations - will unleash a torrent of advocacy spending. "We're in the calm before the storm," Martin said.

Calm, of course, is a relative term. There's no shortage of independent political organizations already engaged in influencing the 2008 elections - including multimillion-dollar campaigns by business and labor groups.

Established interest groups, such as the Club for Growth on the right and the Service Employees International Union on the left, have launched big efforts to rev up key constituencies.

But so far at least, the soft money groups, called 527s - named for the section of the tax code they fall under - don't appear poised to match 2004.

FEC CRACKDOWN

In the Bush-Kerry contest, groups stepped into a void created by campaign finance reform, using 527s to launch withering attacks funded by unlimited contributions and subject to little public disclosure.

In all, 527s, from the Swift Boat Veterans to the left-leaning Media Fund and America Coming Together, poured more than $420 million into the race.

But after the election, the Federal Election Commission determined that four groups - the GOP-aligned Swift Boat Veterans and Progress for America Voter Fund and the Democratic-leaning Media Fund and America Coming Together - had flouted election laws. The agency levied more than $2 million in fines.

The FEC's move seems to have dampened the enthusiasm for 527s this year, campaign experts say. Plus, the two major party candidates, Sens. John McCain, R-Ariz., and Barack Obama, D-Ill., have been critical of the independent attack groups.

"It appears to have substantially reduced the role (of 527s) so far," said Fred Wertheimer, president of Democracy 21, a campaign watchdog group. Several soft money outfits that were expected to play a major role in the election have either fizzled or shifted their focus.

"The legal environment is much tougher in 2008 than it was in 2004," said Tom Matzzie, who runs Accountable America, a left-leaning website set up to monitor conservative advocacy groups. "The big story is truthfully that there's not a lot of activity going on."

'CONSERVATIVE VALUES'

Soft money hasn't disappeared from the political scene. In all, federal 527s have raised about $132 million in the first 18 months of this election cycle, about $30 million less than such groups raised in 2004.

And with the new rules on 527s, some political strategists turned to a different section of the tax code - 501(c)(4) - for social welfare organizations.

"All these groups are doing basically the same thing," said Steve Weissman, of the Campaign Finance Institute, a nonpartisan organization affiliated with George Washington University. "They're electioneering in various ways, and we don't know what the cumulative total is going to be."

The American Issues Project is among those set up as a social welfare group. Created earlier this year to champion what the group's website calls "conservative values," the AIP focuses on smaller government, strong national defense and lower taxes.

Martin, 38, is a solo-practice lawyer who resigned in November amid a controversy over the destruction of e-mails in Blunt's office. He said he "jumped at the chance" to get involved with the group because it offered a new avenue to press a conservative agenda as the presidential race played out.

Martin said he got involved with the group, based in Tampa, Fla., at the urging of Cleta Mitchell, a well-known Republican lawyer based in Washington.

He said he and others have been worried that McCain would be vastly outspent, because Obama opted out of public financing and its accompanying spending restrictions. And he noted that Democratic soft money groups have traditionally had an edge on their GOP counterparts, so this was a chance to even out a "lopsided debate."

"The liberal-leaning groups have made very clear ... that they're going to spend tens of millions of dollars on advocacy for their issues and to influence the discussion in the fall," Martin said, citing campaigns by the Service Employees International Union and Planned Parenthood, among others. "So our goal is to be pushing into the public discourse some conservative ideas."

'WE MAY NEVER KNOW'

Indeed, the AIP created a firestorm when it debuted on the political scene a few weeks ago with a controversial ad linking Obama with William Ayers, a one-time member of the Weather Underground, a radical group that bombed government buildings in the early 1970s. Obama was 8 years old when the bombings occurred; it was many years later when he met Ayers, a longtime resident of the Chicago neighborhood where Obama lives.

The ad was paid for by a $2.9 million donation from Texas billionaire Harold Simmons, a major fundraiser for McCain who also helped bankroll the Swift Boat group in 2004.

Obama's campaign denounced the ad and asked the Justice Department to investigate the AIP, saying the group appeared to be more like a political committee than a nonprofit group.

Martin shrugged off the charges, saying the ad buy was a "drop in the bucket" compared with what some liberal groups were spending.

The AIP is now gearing up for its next move, he said. The group will likely air new TV ads and launch other "issue advocacy" efforts in the coming weeks, but no firm decisions have been made, Martin said.

"We're looking at everything to figure out what's possible," he said.

Campaign finance watchdogs and election lawyers for both presidential candidates are watching warily as they enter the crucial home stretch of the contest.

"A lot of these groups don't get started until now, and they don't report until October, so it's hard to know exactly what they're doing," said Weissman, of the Campaign Finance Institute. And because the 501(c)(4)s are subject to fewer disclosures, "we may never know" the full extent of their role.

September 18, 2008 Thursday
THIRD EDITION


Obama's campaign stays active in Missouri


BYLINE: JO MANNIES


SECTION: METRO; Pg. B3


LENGTH: 793 words


Democratic presidential nominee Barack Obama launched two new campaign ads in Missouri on Wednesday, a move that state Democrats say is the latest proof that he's not abandoning the state.

Another piece of evidence: the 1,500 or so new voter registrations that the state Obama operation has submitted in the past week to election officials in the city of St. Louis and St. Louis County.

The new registrations are among 75,000 that Obama's Missouri campaign say they hope to add to the state's voter rolls before voter registration ends Oct. 8.

State Obama spokesman Justin Hamilton declined to say how many the campaign has turned in so far this election season. "We're on track," he said Wednesday.

In any case, Democrats say they don't have to offer any proof to discount what they see as unfounded speculation pushed by Missouri Republicans to deflate enthusiasm among Obama loyalists in Missouri.

The state Republican Party touched off the talk of a Democratic defection late last week, when state GOP executive director Jared Craighead held a conference call to assert that the Obama campaign would soon pull up stakes after the latest round of public polls showed a slight edge for the Republican presidential nominee, John McCain.

"I think they're out of here," Craighead said. "They're just not getting traction here."

Craighead provided no proof of an eminent Obama defection, beyond third-hand talk from an unnamed rural mail carrier who allegedly told Republicans of outgoing shipments of equipment from an outstate Obama office.

Still, the state GOP continued its mantra this week, issuing a release entitled "So Long, Farewell, auf Wiedersehen, Good Night."

In fact, both presidential campaigns seem to be testing Missouri's political waters lately. McCain and his running mate, Sarah Palin, headlined two high-profile rallies in the state in recent weeks. Obama's vice presidential nominee, Joe Biden, conducted two campaign stops - in Columbia and south St. Louis - about 10 days ago.

The two presidential contenders also have spent more than $4 million apiece on TV ads in Missouri so far this year, according to the latest figures compiled by TNSMI-Campaign Media Analysis Group, a nonpartisan ad-tracking firm that is monitoring campaign ad spending for the Post-Dispatch.

But in recent weeks, both presidential camps have dramatically curbed their Missouri ad buys. The latest weekly report showed combined ad spending of less than $120,000 in the state.

But what's particularly interesting about the Republican claim that Obama is about to pull up stakes is the state GOP's acknowledgement that Obama has a much larger ground operation in the state: 40 campaign offices to 10 offices for the combined McCain-state GOP operation.

While Obama has at least 100 paid staffers in the state and a Missouri press operation, McCain's state staff is believed to be far less. McCain's press spokesmen appear to be stationed in other states.

Craighead and state GOP spokeswoman Tina Hervey say their point is that Obama is wasting time and money on Missouri, and that polls prove it.

But Obama spokesman Hamilton said the GOP was sidestepping what really appears to be happening.

"It's not a question of whether we're leaving, but whether the McCain campaign ever plans to show up," Hamilton said. "We're committed to winning the state by being here on the ground. The McCain campaign wants to phone it in."

St. peters mayor backs Nixon

Meanwhile, St. Peters Mayor Len Pagano, a Republican, touched off a stir with his announcement Wednesday that he's endorsing a Democrat for governor: Attorney General Jay Nixon.

Pagano joined Nixon on Wednesday for a tour of the St. Charles County Economic Development Center. "Jay Nixon is definitely the person I feel will ... target our economic problems that we're having in Missouri," Pagano said.

Nixon said in a statement that he welcomed Pagano's support and will focus on creating "a better-educated, healthier work force to compete in the 21st-century economy."

Pagano's support is significant in that St. Peters is in St. Charles County, generally considered a Republican stronghold.

Pagano emphasized in an interview that his decision shouldn't be seen as a slap at the Republican candidate for governor, U.S. Rep. Kenny Hulshof of Columbia. "This isn't anti-Hulshof, it's pro-Nixon," he said.

But fellow Republicans aren't expected to see it that way. Hulshof campaign manager John Hancock said there's no evidence Pagano contacted anybody in state government to air concerns about jobs. Hancock also noted that the St. Peters post is nonpartisan and added, "It's not credible to call him a Republican."

September 18, 2008 Thursday
THIRD EDITION


Wall Street is big donor to McCain, Obama Executives of troubled firms have been major fundraisers.


BYLINE: By Pete Yost THE ASSOCIATED PRESS


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A3


LENGTH: 734 words


DATELINE: WASHINGTON


Some of John McCain's and Barack Obama's biggest fundraisers are executives from the stricken financial services industry, which will need all the help it can get from whichever candidate wins the White House.

Merrill Lynch & Co.'s chief executive, for example, has raised more than $500,000 for McCain's campaign. Obama has received at least $1.5 million collected by three senior executives at Lehman Brothers.

McCain and Obama each are considering how to avoid future collapses and the need for further costly government bailouts, steps that may include tougher banking and investment regulations. But executives from the same companies in the crosshairs of such decisions are helping these candidates get elected.

Tough talk is coming from candidates who have fueled their campaigns with Wall Street money. Securities and investment firms gave $9.9 million to Obama and $6.9 million to McCain through July, according to the Center for Responsive Politics, a private group that tracks money spent in politics. The industry is McCain's No. 3 contributor and Obama's No. 4 contributor, according to the center.

Three executives from Goldman Sachs Group Inc. have raised at least $500,000 for Obama. That firm is Obama's top source of campaign money overall; its employees have contributed more than $690,000 to his campaign, according to the center.

Merrill Lynch's chief executive, John Thain, has raised more than $500,000 for McCain. Merrill's work force likewise is McCain's top donor, giving nearly $300,000.

Separately, employees from the commercial bank and insurance sectors gave McCain's campaign $3.6 million and Obama's campaign $3.4 million.

Congressional candidates collect considerable money from Wall Street, too. Democratic candidates have accepted nearly $37 million from securities and investment firms in the current election, and Republicans have accepted nearly $29 million.

The industry's contributions to all federal candidates and political parties: $101 million so far in this two-year election cycle.

Merrill Lynch and its new owner both will be looking for help from the White House. Merrill Lynch jumped into the arms of Bank of America Corp. over the weekend to avoid becoming the next Lehman Brothers, which filed for bankruptcy protection Monday.

Following a weekend that reshaped Wall Street, Goldman Sachs is the larger of the nation's two remaining major independent investment banks. The other is Morgan Stanley, where employees have contributed $300,000 to Obama and $217,000 to McCain.

It's still early, but Wall Street's institutions may have good reason to worry about a new president, once they get past the issue of financial survival.

In March, before the latest round of financial carnage, Treasury Secretary Henry Paulson said he expected the next president and the next Congress to address Paulson's blueprint for the biggest overhaul of government financial regulation since the Depression.

Campaigns Respond

Meanwhile, the candidates scrambled Wednesday to adjust their messages to connect more directly with financially struggling voters.

Obama talks directly into the camera in a new, two-minute television ad on how he'll fix an economy in which "paychecks are flat and home values are falling." McCain and running mate Sarah Palin softened opposition to government bailouts, accepting the U.S. takeover of the nation's largest insurer as unfortunate but necessary to protect ordinary Americans.

"The shot that has been called by the Feds - it's understandable but very, very disappointing that taxpayers are called upon for another one," Palin told reporters during a visit to a delicatessen in Cleveland.

Both McCain and Obama advocated cracking down on freewheeling Wall Street practices and for tough new regulations on financial institutions.

Obama ridiculed McCain's calls for more regulation as an eleventh-hour conversion for one who has long championed deregulation.

Too many in Washington and on Wall Street "weren't minding the store. They sat on their hands until it was too late," Obama told a rally in Elko, Nev. He challenged McCain's vow to take on the "old boys' network. ... He hasn't taken them on for the last 26 years."

A day earlier, McCain opposed additional government relief and praised the government's decision not to rescue Lehman Brothers after it had intervened to help investment bank Bear Stearns and mortgage giants Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac.

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


September 17, 2008 Wednesday
THIRD EDITION


Let's remember who's actually running for president


BYLINE: ERIC MINK


SECTION: EDITORIAL; Pg. D11


LENGTH: 887 words


After two weeks' vacation and more than 1,700 Midwestern highway miles, I came home looking for some wisdom. I found it on the editorial page of Sunday's Post-Dispatch - in a letter to the editor by Dorothy Anderson of Spanish Lake:

"I have a question," she wrote, setting us up for her rhetorical punch line. "This year, are we having a presidential election or a vice-presidential election?"

Thank you.

Enough about Sarah Palin already. Enough, even, about Joe Biden, as paltry as coverage of the long-time Delaware senator has been in comparison to the frenzied attention paid to the formerly unknown governor of Alaska.

I'll grant that their respective life stories, family situations and opinions on issues are interesting. I'll also note that, except for an immense disparity in length, the political careers of both are essentially routine when you bore down into them. Both of those careers now are the subjects of inevitable campaign exaggerations and, in Palin's case especially, campaign deceptions.

But none of this really matters. The truly relevant questions when it comes to them are: If Obama were elected and, God forbid a million times, were to be struck down by violence or illness, would you feel comfortable with Biden becoming president? Likewise, if McCain were elected and, God forbid a million times, were to be struck down by illness or violence, would you feel comfortable with Palin becoming president?

People's opinions obviously differ. I'd be comfortable with Biden becoming president. And I'd be as uncomfortable with Palin becoming president as I'd be with University City's Joe Adams, a decent man and mayor of my hometown, becoming president. By any reasonable comparative assessment, Biden is qualified for the job, and Palin isn't.

But neither Biden nor Palin is running for president, as reader Dorothy Anderson gently chided us on Sunday, and I'm not about to base my vote for president on either one. The candidates for president are Barack Obama and John McCain.

By the time I vote on Nov. 4, I expect to have a good sense of which man comes closer to sharing my values and my priorities about issues; which one is more genuine, given the realities of politics; and which one I can trust better to keep faith with me and the rest of the American people - including those who don't vote for him.

What does this amount to?

- Well, I know that millions of Americans are struggling to pay bills that aren't optional: mortgages and rent, food, transportation, medical expenses. People close to me have lost their jobs and suddenly find themselves working to stay afloat financially. And although I make a good living and have managed to keep debts to a minimum, my wages haven't kept pace with inflation, my retirement funds have taken a huge hit and my profession is undergoing a wrenching transformation that could put me on the street tomorrow with barely a blink.

- I read about crises in the financial and securities industries and reflect on the last 30 years or so, during which The Market was elevated to the status of god - as if asset and liability balance sheets and profit and loss statements said more about the goodness of American society than the way people live and treat each other.

I hear politicians express confidence in the marketplace and wonder if they are ignorant or if they secretly realize how many crises have resulted from the failure of government to do its job and protect ordinary Americans from the abuses of the very marketplace they pretend to revere.

- I've seen the American health care system left to the mercy of those same market forces and watched costs rise, health decline and millions forced into bankruptcy. I'm mystified that anyone can keep a straight face when he claims that the marketplace can solve these problems when what we need is the political will to create a new, uniquely American system that will spare people from needless suffering and anguish when they are most vulnerable.

- I'm offended by scam artists who prey on the real fears of real people and pretend that getting more oil to market, even in 10 years, will do anything but prolong our sick and destructive dependence on petroleum products, further erode the American economy and hurt working people more than anybody else.

- I keep hearing that education is the key to conquering poverty, and then I read about higher and higher tuition costs, limitations on grants to bright qualified students who lack only money and abusive commercial loan practices that pump young people into the job market carrying crushing debts.

- I see my country's fundamental values of justice, fairness and individual rights debased in a fight against punks and thugs who exploited simple security flaws we knew about but hadn't bothered to fix. I see a war waged because weak, frightened people needed to feel and appear strong - a war that, no matter how it concludes, will leave our country more vulnerable than it was before and our American values pointlessly and shamefully compromised. I wonder if there is such a thing any more as accountability.

It's six weeks and six days until the presidential election, and I'm going to spend that time figuring out whether to place my trust in Barack Obama or John McCain. I've already answered the only question that matters about Joe Biden and Sarah Palin.

September 16, 2008 Tuesday
THIRD EDITION


Obama and McCain grapple over economy Both candidates seek to win over voters by speaking of the financial crisis, but neither poses new ideas for solutions.


BYLINE: By Liz Sidoti THE ASSOCIATED PRESS


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A2


LENGTH: 657 words


With chaos rocking financial markets, John McCain assailed "greed and corruption" on Wall Street and promised to clean it up, while Barack Obama blamed White House policies and said his opponent would only deliver more of the same.

The candidates struggled on Monday to seize control of the issue voters say is most important - the economy - with Republicans and Democrats alike saying the man who succeeds may well win the election.

However, in a dizzying day of speeches and statements, neither White House hopeful offered any fresh ideas for turning things around. Instead, each relied on the same vague, though vastly different pitches he has sounded over the last few months for fixing what ails the country.

And they didn't emphasize that they are part of the Congress that has done little to head off the crisis. McCain is a four-term Arizona senator, Obama a first-termer from Illinois.

Bemoaning "the most serious financial crisis since the Great Depression," Democrat Obama faulted Republican McCain's domestic policy agenda as the same as President George W. Bush's - "one that says we should just stick our heads in the sand and ignore economic problems until they spiral into crises."

McCain declared in a new TV ad, "Our economy is in crisis. Only proven reformers John McCain and Sarah Palin can fix it" - though he also told voters in Jacksonville, Fla., "The fundamentals of our economy are strong."

With some seven weeks left in the campaign, both Obama and McCain are trying to find a message that resonates with anxious voters who are fretting about their retirement nest eggs, home mortgages and job security.

As different as their policies are, they were united in their message to voters: It's not your fault.

Courting working-class voters who gave him grief in the Democratic primary, Obama sounded an I-feel-your-pain note.

Obama lamented Republican policies over eight years that he said "encouraged outsized bonuses to CEOs while ignoring middle-class Americans" and said: "Instead of prosperity trickling down, the pain has trickled up - from the struggles of hardworking Americans on Main Street to the largest firms of Wall Street."

McCain's words were sympathetic as well.

"America is in a crisis today," he said - then added: "The economic crisis is not the fault of the American people. Our workers are the most innovative, the hardest working, the best skilled, the most productive, the most competitive in the world. ... But they are being threatened today ... because of greed and corruption that some engaged in on Wall Street." Some in the markets, he said, "have treated Wall Street like a casino."

In line with historical positions of Democrats and Republicans, Obama generally supports stronger consumer protections, better regulatory oversight and more government intervention, while McCain broadly prefers a market system of less federal involvement and red tape.

Both advocate tax cuts, though to different degrees and different ends. Obama seeks to cut into inequality between rich and poor by raising taxes on the wealthiest Americans and giving breaks to the middle class and lower-income people. McCain wants to spur the economy and create jobs by keeping tax rates low for higher-income taxpayers and slashing rates for corporations.

While they focused on the topic that voters say matters most, the two campaigns also continued to assail each other Monday on character issues. The Democratic vice presidential nominee, Joe Biden, said McCain was "launching a low blow a day." McCain's campaign, in turn, accused Obama of spouting "false talk about change" and hurling insults to cover up his record.

Obama has led for months on the question of who would best handle the economy, but some polls show that his advantage has dwindled. He had a slight advantage over McCain on the economy - 47 percent to 42 percent - in an ABC News/Washington Post poll last week, with the Democrat's edge cut in half since spring.

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


September 15, 2008 Monday
THIRD EDITION


Big banks pool $70 billion to head off global panic Merrill Lynch joins Lehman Brothers on shaky ground.


BYLINE: THE ASSOCIATED PRESS


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A1


LENGTH: 1715 words


DATELINE: NEW YORK


A failed plan to rescue Lehman Brothers was followed Sunday by more seismic shocks from Wall Street, including an apparent government-brokered takeover of Merrill Lynch by the Bank of America.

A forced restructuring of the world's largest insurance company, American International Group Inc., also weighed heavily on global markets as the effects of the 14-month-old credit crisis intensified.

A global consortium of banks, working with government officials in New York, announced late Sunday a $70 billion pool of funds to lend to troubled financial companies. The aim, according to participants who spoke to The Associated Press, was to prevent a worldwide panic on stock and other financial exchanges.

Ten banks - Bank of America, Barclays, Citibank, Credit Suisse, Deutsche Bank, Goldman Sachs, JP Morgan, Merrill Lynch, Morgan Stanley and UBS - each agreed to provide $7 billion "to help enhance liquidity and mitigate the unprecedented volatility and other challenges affecting global equity and debt markets."

The Federal Reserve also chipped in with more largesse in its emergency lending program for investment banks. The central bank announced late Sunday that it was broadening the types of collateral that financial institutions can use to obtain loans from the Fed.

Federal Reserve Chairman Ben Bernanke said the discussions had been aimed at identifying "potential market vulnerabilities in the wake of an unwinding of a major financial institution and to consider appropriate official sector and private sector responses."

Futures pegged to the Dow Jones industrial average fell more than 300 points in electronic trading Sunday evening, pointing to a sharply lower open for the blue chip index this morning. Asian stock markets were also falling.

In a statement early today, Lehman Brothers said that it would file for Chapter 11 bankruptcy.

The investment bank said it is exploring the sale of its broker-dealer operations and is in "advanced discussions" to sell its investment management unit.

All potential buyers walked away from Lehman on Sunday after the U.S. Treasury refused to budge on its refusal to provide any takeover aid, as it had done six months ago when Bear Stearns faltered and earlier this month when it seized Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac.

Expectations that the 158-year-old Lehman would survive dimmed after Barclays PLC withdrew its bid to buy the investment bank. Lehman is foundering under the weight of $60 billion in soured real estate holdings.

Insurer AIG, hit hard by deterioration in the credit markets, said Sunday it is reviewing its operations and discussing possible options with outside parties to improve its business after a week when its stock dropped 45 percent amid concerns about the company's financial underpinnings. It was working with New York Insurance Superintendent Eric Dinallo and a representative of the governor's office through the weekend to craft a solution that protects policyholders, according to Dinallo's spokesman David Neustadt.

Bank of America

buying Merrill?

Merrill Lynch, another investment bank laid low by the crisis that was triggered by rising mortgage defaults and plunging home values in the U.S., agreed to be acquired by Bank of America for $29 a share, according to the Wall Street Journal. That's a premium to its closing price on Friday of $17.05 but only a fraction of its price of almost $100 a share early in 2007.

Charlotte, N.C.-based Bank of America has the most deposits of any U.S. bank, while Merrill Lynch is the world's largest brokerage. A combination of the two would create a global financial giant to rival Citigroup Inc., the biggest U.S. bank in terms of assets.

The deal would not come without risks, however. Merrill Lynch, like many of its Wall Street peers, has been struggling with tight credit markets and billions of dollars in assets tied to mortgages that have plunged in value. Merrill has reported four straight quarterly losses.

And Bank of America's own finances are far from robust. As consumer credit deteriorates, the bank has seen its profits decline, and the company is still in the midst of absorbing the embattled mortgage lender Countrywide Financial, which it acquired in January.

Election affected

The stunning weekend developments took place as voters, who rank the economy as their top concern, prepare to elect a new president in seven weeks.

The weekend's developments will likely spur a much greater focus by presidential candidates - Republican John McCain and Democrat Barack Obama - and members of Congress on the need for stricter financial regulation.

Samuel Hayes, finance professor emeritus at Harvard Business School, said President George W. Bush's administration may get a lot of blame for the situation, which could benefit Obama.

"Just the psychological impact of this kind of failure is going to be significant," he said. "It will color people's feelings about their well-being and the integrity of the financial system."

Paulson was huddled through the weekend at the New York Federal Reserve's fortress-like building in downtown Manhattan with executives from major banks and investment houses to hash out the fate of Lehman Brothers and to stanch the bleeding on Wall Street that threatened to shatter investor confidence around the globe.

"It's clear we're one step away from a financial meltdown," said Nouriel Roubini, chairman of the consulting firm RGE Monitor.

The meetings that began Friday night were a who's who of financial heavyweights: Paulson, Timothy Geithner, president of the New York Fed, Securities and Exchange Commission Chairman Christopher Cox, and a host of CEOs, including Vikram Pandit of Citigroup Inc., Jamie Dimon of JPMorgan Chase & Co., John Mack of Morgan Stanley, Lloyd Blankfein of Goldman Sachs Group Inc., and Merrill Lynch & Co.'s John Thain.

For all their efforts, Lehman appeared ready to file for bankruptcy.

More banks falter

The end of Lehman may not stop the financial crisis that has gripped Wall Street for months, analysts said. More investment banks could disappear soon.

The independent broker-dealers "are going the way of the dodo bird," said Bert Ely, an Alexandria, Va.-based banking consultant.

That's partly because some of the firms, particularly Merrill, made bad bets on real estate. But several analysts said that investment companies will need the deep pockets of commercial banks to survive the next few years.

Roubini said with no deal for Lehman, Merrill and the other investment firms would have been hit with a "run on the bank," as hedge funds and other clients withdraw funds and banks become reluctant to lend to them. Many of the investment banks rely on short-term loans to finance day-to-day operations.

The cost of insuring financial firms' debt from default has been soaring.

A rise in the cost of the insurance, known as credit default swaps, indicates debt holders believe there is a greater chance of default by the financial companies. Especially over the last week, those insurance costs have been increasing rapidly as more debt holders fear companies like Lehman Brothers and Washington Mutual Inc. could collapse and not be able to repay their debt.

Swaps on most financial firms are likely to get even worse during the upcoming week, analysts said.

On Sunday, there was also an emergency trading session being held at the International Swaps and Derivatives Association to "reduce risk associated with a potential Lehman Brothers Holdings Inc. bankruptcy." The ISDA, which arranges trades for derivatives, said it was allowing customers to make trades and unwind positions linked to Lehman - but that those trades would be

Roubini said it's difficult to accurately gauge the health of companies like Merrill because their financial health depends on how they value complex securities. As a result, their finances aren't very transparent, he said.

That can lead to a loss of confidence in the financial markets, he said, which can overwhelm an investment bank even if it is financially healthy by some measures.

Ely said similar shake-outs have happened in other parts of the financial industry, such as credit cards and thrifts. Bank of America acquired independent credit card issuer MBNA in 2005, for example, while credit card company Capital One Financial Corp. has diversified itself by purchasing regional banks in Louisiana, Texas and New York.

The common denominator of the financial crisis, analysts said, is the bursting of the housing bubble. Home prices have dropped on average 25 percent so far. Roubini predicted they could drop another 15 percent.

The crisis has begun to slow the broader economy as banks make fewer loans and consumers have begun cutting spending. Many economists are now forecasting that the economy could slip into recession by the end of this year and early next year.

That, in turn, could cause additional losses for commercial banks on credit cards, auto loans and student loans.

The Fed is widely expected to keep interest rates steady at 2 percent when it meets Tuesday. It was possible, however, that the central bank might decide in coming weeks to cut rates if such a move is seen as needed to calm turbulent financial markets.

The International Monetary Fund predicted earlier this year that total losses from the credit crisis could reach almost $1 trillion. So far, banks have only taken about $350 billion in losses.

Commercial banks are also starting to feel the pinch. Eleven have closed so far this year, including Pasadena, Calif.-based IndyMac Bank, which had $32 billion in assets and $19 billion in deposits.

Christopher Whalen, managing director of Institutional Risk Analytics, a research firm, predicts that about 110 banks with $850 billion in assets could close by next July. That's out of 8,400 federally insured institutions, he said, which together hold $13 trillion in assets.

Individual customers are starting to get nervous about the financial health of their banks for the first time in generations, he said. Whalen's firm analyzes the safety and soundness of banks for business clients, but began receiving inquiries from individuals in the past two months for the first time, he said.

"If we don't get ahead of this, we are going to face a run on the retail banks by election day," he said.

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


September 15, 2008 Monday
SECOND EDITION


Big banks pool $70 billion to head off global panic Firms Founder Attempt to rescue Lehman Brothers fails. Deal for sale of Merrill Lynch to Bank of America is reported.


BYLINE: THE ASSOCIATED PRESS


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A1


LENGTH: 1703 words


NEW York - A failed plan to rescue Lehman Brothers was followed Sunday by more seismic shocks from Wall Street, including an apparent government-brokered takeover of Merrill Lynch by the Bank of America.

A forced restructuring of the world's largest insurance company, American International Group Inc., also weighed heavily on global markets as the effects of the 14-month-old credit crisis intensified.

A global consortium of banks, working with government officials in New York, announced late Sunday a $70 billion pool of funds to lend to troubled financial companies. The aim, according to participants who spoke to The Associated Press, was to prevent a worldwide panic on stock and other financial exchanges.

Ten banks - Bank of America, Barclays, Citibank, Credit Suisse, Deutsche Bank, Goldman Sachs, JP Morgan, Merrill Lynch, Morgan Stanley and UBS - each agreed to provide $7 billion "to help enhance liquidity and mitigate the unprecedented volatility and other challenges affecting global equity and debt markets."

The Federal Reserve also chipped in with more largesse in its emergency lending program for investment banks. The central bank announced late Sunday that it was broadening the types of collateral that financial institutions can use to obtain loans from the Fed.

Federal Reserve Chairman Ben Bernanke said the discussions had been aimed at identifying "potential market vulnerabilities in the wake of an unwinding of a major financial institution and to consider appropriate official sector and private sector responses."

Futures pegged to the Dow Jones industrial average fell more than 300 points in electronic trading Sunday evening, pointing to a sharply lower open for the blue chip index this morning. Asian stock markets were also falling.

Lehman Brothers may be forced to seek an orderly unwinding of its businesses. All potential buyers walked away after the U.S. Treasury refused to budge on its refusal to provide any takeover aid, as it had done six months ago when Bear Stearns faltered and earlier this month when it seized Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac.

Expectations that the 158-year-old Lehman would survive dimmed after Barclays PLC withdrew its bid to buy the investment bank. Barclays and Bank of America were considered front-runners to buy Lehman, which is foundering under the weight of $60 billion in soured real estate holdings.

Insurer AIG, hit hard by deterioration in the credit markets, said Sunday it is reviewing its operations and discussing possible options with outside parties to improve its business after a week when its stock dropped 45 percent amid concerns about the company's financial underpinnings. It was working with New York Insurance Superintendent Eric Dinallo and a representative of the governor's office through the weekend to craft a solution that protects policyholders, according to Dinallo's spokesman David Neustadt.

Bank of America

buying Merrill?

Merrill Lynch, another investment bank laid low by the crisis that was triggered by rising mortgage defaults and plunging home values in the U.S., agreed to be acquired by Bank of America for $29 a share, according to the Wall Street Journal. That's a premium to its closing price on Friday of $17.05 but only a fraction of its price of almost $100 a share early in 2007.

Charlotte, N.C.-based Bank of America has the most deposits of any U.S. bank, while Merrill Lynch is the world's largest brokerage. A combination of the two would create a global financial giant to rival Citigroup Inc., the biggest U.S. bank in terms of assets.

The deal would not come without risks, however. Merrill Lynch, like many of its Wall Street peers, has been struggling with tight credit markets and billions of dollars in assets tied to mortgages that have plunged in value. Merrill has reported four straight quarterly losses.

And Bank of America's own finances are far from robust. As consumer credit deteriorates, the bank has seen its profits decline, and the company is still in the midst of absorbing the embattled mortgage lender Countrywide Financial, which it acquired in January.

Election affected

The stunning weekend developments took place as voters, who rank the economy as their top concern, prepare to elect a new president in seven weeks.

The weekend's developments will likely spur a much greater focus by presidential candidates - Republican John McCain and Democrat Barack Obama - and members of Congress on the need for stricter financial regulation.

"Just the psychological impact of this kind of failure is going to be significant," he said. "It will color people's feelings about their well-being and the integrity of the financial system."

"It's clear we're one step away from a financial meltdown," said Nouriel Roubini, chairman of the consulting firm RGE Monitor.

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


September 14, 2008 Sunday
FOURTH EDITION


McCain camp is drawing fire over veracity Even when statements are shown to be inaccurate, the campaign has stood by them.


BYLINE: By Charles Babington THE ASSOCIATED PRESS


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A12


LENGTH: 826 words


DATELINE: WASHINGTON


John McCain's campaign keeps telling voters that Sarah Palin opposed a federally funded Bridge to Nowhere when the facts are otherwise. It accuses Democrat Barack Obama of calling Palin a pig, which did not happen.

Even in a political culture accustomed to truth-stretching, McCain's skirting of facts has stood out this week. It has infuriated and flustered Barack Obama's campaign, and campaign pros are watching to see how much voters disregard news reports noting factual holes in the claims.

That voter reaction could help determine who wins this presidential election.

Politicians usually modify or drop claims when a string of newspaper and TV news accounts concludes they are untrue or greatly exaggerated. Sen. Hillary Rodham Clinton, for example, conceded she had not come under sniper fire in Bosnia after a batch of debunking articles subjected her to ridicule in her primary contest against Obama.

McCain's persistence in pushing dubious claims is all the more notable because many political insiders consider him one of the greatest living victims of underhanded campaigning. Locked in a tight race with George W. Bush for the Republican presidential nomination in 2000, McCain was rocked in South Carolina by a whisper campaign claiming he had fathered an illegitimate black child and was mentally unstable.

Shaken by the experience, McCain denounced less-than-truthful campaigning. He even apologized to journalists for his own reluctance to criticize the flying of the Confederate flag at South Carolina's state Capitol in a bid for votes. When the so-called Swift Boat Veterans for Truth attacked the military record of Democrat and fellow Navy officer John Kerry in 2004, McCain called the ads "dishonest and dishonorable."

Now, top aides to McCain include Steve Schmidt, who has close ties to Karl Rove, Bush's political adviser in 2000.

McCain and his running mate, Palin, the Alaska governor, were defiant this week in the face of fact-checking news reports. Day after day, she said she had told Congress "no thanks" to the so-called Bridge to Nowhere, a rural Alaska project that was abandoned when critics challenged its costs and usefulness. For nearly a week, major news outlets had documented that Palin supported the bridge when running for governor in 2006, and she turned against it only after it became an embarrassment to the state and federal dollars had been pulled back.

The McCain-Palin campaign made at least three other aggressive claims last week that omitted key details or made dubious assumptions to criticize Obama. It equated lawmakers' requests for money for special projects with corruption, even though Palin has sought nearly $200 million in such "earmarks" this year.

It produced an Internet ad implying that Obama had called Palin a pig when he used a familiar phrase, which McCain also has used, about putting "lipstick on a pig" to try to make a bad situation look better. McCain supporters said Obama was slyly alluding to Palin's description of herself as a pit bull in lipstick, but there was nothing in his remarks to support the claim. Obama accused the GOP campaign of "lies and phony outrage."

The lipstick wars were fully engaged when the McCain campaign produced another ad saying Obama favored "comprehensive sex education" for kindergartners. The charge triggered the sort of headlines becoming increasingly common in major newspapers and wire services monitoring the factual content of political ads and speeches.

"Ad on Sex Education Distorts Obama Policy," was the headline on a New York Times article Thursday. "McCain's 'Education' Spot is Dishonest, Deceptive," said The Washington Post.

Major news outlets have written such fact-checking articles for years. "But in the last two election cycles, the very notion that the facts matter seems to be under assault," said Michael X. Delli Carpini, an authority on political ads at the University of Pennsylvania's Annenberg School for Communication.

Surveys already show that most people believe Obama would raise their taxes - a regular McCain claim - even though independent groups such as the Tax Policy Center concluded that four out of five U.S. households would receive tax cuts under his proposals.

McCain spokesman Tucker Bounds defended the campaign's statements. "We include factual back-up in every one of our TV spots," he said Thursday.

Obama, of course, has made exaggerated or questionable assertions as well. Earlier this year, for instance, he repeated a claim that more black men are in prison than in college, after news accounts refuted it. He also used a McCain remark about having troops in Iraq for "100 years" to exaggerate McCain's proposals for being fully engaged militarily in that country.

In general, however, Obama has been quicker to react to news accounts challenging his accuracy. Faced with skeptical reports this year, he stopped saying he "worked his way" through college, and instead credited hard work and scholarships.

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


September 13, 2008 Saturday
FIRST EDITION


New Obama TV ad plays up McCain's age


BYLINE: By Margaret Talev and Steven Thomma McClatchy Newspapers The Associated Press contributed to this report.


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A18


LENGTH: 392 words


DATELINE: WASHINGTON


Thrown off stride by a hard-charging campaign from Republican rivals John McCain and Sarah Palin, Barack Obama moved Friday to reclaim the spotlight and assure worried supporters that he can and will fight back.

"We will respond with speed and ferocity to John McCain's attacks and we will take the fight to him," Obama campaign manager David Plouffe said in a memo Friday.

"But we will do it on the big issues that matter to the American people. "

Obama immediately launched a new ad that notably omitted any mention of Palin - an abrupt switch after days of criticizing her.

The makes a none-too-subtle dig at McCain's age. It shows McCain at a hearing in the early 1980s, wearing giant glasses and a now-out-of-style suit. Other images include a disco ball, clunky phone, outdated computer and Rubik's Cube. "Things have changed in the last 26 years," the announcer says, "but McCain hasn't."

"He admits he still doesn't know how to use a computer, can't send an e-mail, still doesn't understand the economy, and favors $200 billion in new tax cuts for corporations, but almost nothing for the middle class," the commercial says.

McCain has said he relies on his wife and staff to work the computer for him and that he doesn't use e-mail.

At the same time, Obama supporters tried to seize on a comment McCain made the night before at a forum on service in which he defended Palin's experience as a small-town mayor by saying she was closer to real people than he was in the Senate.

"It's easy for me to go to Washington and frankly be somewhat divorced from the day-to-day challenges people have," McCain said.

Obama pounced on Friday in New Hampshire, saying, "From where he and George Bush sit, maybe they just can't see. Maybe they are just that out of touch. "

Obama hoped the steps would calm donors and a base that's been agitating for more fight from him. Although the race is neck and neck, many Democrats think that Obama should be ahead given broad public dissatisfaction with President George W. Bush and the Republican Party.

McCain responded with a new ad of his own, aimed at simultaneously ridiculing Obama and keeping the focus on Palin.

"They lashed out at Sarah Palin," the new ad says. "Dismissed her as good-looking. That backfired, so they said she was doing what she was told. Then desperately called Sarah Palin a liar. How disrespectful."

LETTERS FROM READERS


SECTION: EDITORIAL; Pg. B6


LENGTH: 1423 words


No new taxes until all sales - even online - are taxed

If St. Louis and St. Louis County would collect the taxes already on the books, there would be no need for additional taxes. Sales taxes in some areas, such as Eureka, are now almost 8 percent.

Many naive voters don't realize what these sales taxes amount to. If one or more of these taxes are approved in November, many communities will have sales taxes of more than 8 percent. On the purchase of your average new car that is about $2,000. Now calculate the amount of other purchases, and you will see these that taxes are much more than the naive voter understands.

Sales of items sold on eBay, Overstock, Amazon and a host of other websites are not taxed. Some people will drive 10 miles out of their way to save a nickel on a gallon of gas. Think how much they save when they avoid sales taxes for everyday purchases. Some websites offer discounts on bulk items, much like warehouse clubs. Websites such as these hurt the local economy because sales taxes are not collected.

Until sales taxes are collected on all sales, includingonline sales, I don't think anyone should vote for additional sales taxes.

So get out and vote your opinion in November. I suggest that you vote no on all sales taxes.

Tom Tenholder | Eureka

Too much change

According to "Chicago's public schools face boycott threat today" (Sept. 2), the Chicago public schools are a mess. Amazingly, the "ailing" district spent more than $11,000 per student last year, placing it well above the national average. Even more amazing is the suggested tonic for their ailment: a $120 million pilot program that would give the state legislature the "chance to prove that money does make a difference in school performance."

While the state's junior U.S. senator is otherwise engaged, his former colleagues are proposing an all-too-familiar solution: pouring more taxpayer dollars into a broken system. One hundred and twenty million dollars is a bit too much pocket change for me to believe in.

Margret Graham | St. Louis County

Silent epidemic

Regarding "Death of girl is linked to aerosol hair spray" (Aug. 29): The huffing epidemic is killing many of our young men and women. I lost my oldest son to huffing on Christmas Eve in 2006. Until my son died, I had never heard of huffing. When I talked to the DARE officer at my son's school, I was told that even DARE doesn't go into detail about huffing. They more or less just say, "Don't do it." But after talking to police officers, I learned that this epidemic has been going on for many years.

My son was 17. You have to be 18 to buy the product that killed him, but the clerk at the store where the product was purchased did not verify anyone's age. The company that makes the product has known for years that its product is harmful but refuses to put a bittering agent in it because it makes the product "smell bad." I tried sued the company and the store, and I was slapped with a gag order. I can talk about my son and what happened to him, but I can't talk about the companies that made or sold the product.

These aerosol products continue to be sold, even though they are harmful. The companies refuse to look for safer products because the existing products are profitable. I commend those companies that make non-aerosol products.

Until Dec. 24, 2006, I was blind to what drugs are out there. I fought the illegal drugs and turned in drug dealers many times over, but to whom do we turn in the legal drug dealers?

GeorgAnn Kays | Sullivan, Mo.

No hot dog is a safe hot dog

As a dietitian with the nonprofit organization that produced the provocative "Protect Our Kids" anti-hot dog TV commercial, I think Americans deserve to know about the compelling scientific evidence linking processed meat consumption to increased risk of colorectal cancer ("Deadly 'dogs'," Aug. 29).

A landmark report released last year by the American Institute for Cancer Research and the World Cancer Research Fund concluded that no amount of processed meat is considered safe to eat; ideally, it should be avoided completely. After reviewing 58 large scientific studies, researchers with the two cancer organizations concluded that processed meats increase the risk of colorectal cancer by 21 percent for every 50 grams of processed meat consumed daily (a 50-gram serving is about the size of a typical hot dog). As with all carcinogens, longer exposure probably means greater risk. That's why it's so important to get hot dogs and other processed meats out of the school lunch line and help kids develop healthy eating habits that can protect them from cancer later in life.

Susan Levin | Washington, D.C. Staff Dietitian, The Cancer Project

Slice of Americana

We were glad to see the Post-Dispatch cover "Deadly 'dogs'" (Aug. 29). And we couldn't agree more with the paper's opinion that eating hot dogs is OK, as long as you save them for an occasional treat. We feel that eating hot dogs is a heavenly slice of Americana, a pastime worth savoring and saving.

The hot dog has a storied history in St. Louis. Wasn't it at the 1904 Louisiana Purchase Exposition (World's Fair) that some ingenious food vendor had the idea to put the wiener and the bun together and created the original hot dog? And St. Louis is home to many long-time restaurants and establishments that regale and revere the hot dog to cult status.

The advice that Virginia should ask that Mom, Dad and the lunch lady "serve good-quality dogs, not cheapos" is right on the money. We invite Virginia to join us at ToastedRav.com's "Tour de Frankfurter," a quest to eat 24 hot dogs in 24 places within 24 hours on Sept. 11. We will share the joy and pleasure of eating hot dogs. We'll even spring for a hot dog for Virginia's lunch lady.

Chris Files | St. Louis County ToastedRav.com Videographer

Big enough to lose

"If you're not big enough to lose, you're not big enough to run," are words I remember my dad saying when he ran for a second term in Congress. He never sold out. I'm deeply saddened to see national political aspirants who are willing to twist in any direction in order to win.

Elizabeth Sublette | Richmond Heights

Whiners and empathizers

In his column "Is it as simple as: Democrats care and Republicans don't?" (Sept. 2), Paul Krugman hit the ball out of the park. A great difference exists between Democratic and Republican leaders of today. The Democrats have compassion and empathy for those who desperately need help, such as those without health insurance. Republicans believe that those people who need help are just a bunch of whiners. If they need health care, just let them go to an emergency room.

Does America want four more years of President George W. Bush's policies of astronomical tax cuts for the rich and to heck with everybody else? Hope is the mother of all men and women. Democrats bring us hope for a compassionate, competent and equitable America.

Dick Reeves | Kirkwood

Informed decisions

One of Republican presidential nominee Sen. John McCain's central arguments is that his experience helps inform better decision-making and judgment. His hurried choice of Alaska Gov. Sarah Palin to be his vice presidential running mate is a clear indicator of his decision-making ability. Embarrassing revelations are coming by the minute. She's not been truthful so far (her statements about opposing the "bridge to nowhere are discredited by 2006 reports and quotes). And it looks as if Mr. McCain's team never really looked into Ms. Palin's record. Reports from the Anchorage, Alaska, newspapers indicate the Republicans arrived after her selection to look into Ms. Palin's record.

This "shoot from the hip" decision-making is consistent with Mr. McCain's past, and it proves fairly conclusively that he lacks the temperament and judgment needed for the presidency.

Chris Osborn | Olivette

Odious comparison

How can R.J. Matson continue to get it so completely wrong? His Aug. 28 editorial cartoon, "Backstage at the DNC Convention," is the latest example. Comparing Sen. Barack Obama, the Democratic nominee for president, to Abraham Lincoln and the Rev. Dr. Martin Luther King Jr. is laughable.

The most obvious gaffe is that both Lincoln and King were Republicans. Why would they be celebrating at the Democratic National Convention? Lincoln used U.S. blood and treasure to unite the country and abolish slavery; King used his faith in Jesus and his moral strength to abolish the Democratic party's Jim Crow laws. Mr. Obama wants to take away our freedoms with high taxes and odious regulations.

Gary Goulet | Kirkwood

September 6, 2008 Saturday
THIRD EDITION


GOP TV audience tops Democrats; McCain, Obama virtually tied Readout ehre ehre


BYLINE: By David Bauder AP Television Writer


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A19


LENGTH: 428 words


DATELINE: NEW YORK


As a television draw, John McCain was every bit the equal of Barack Obama.

The GOP presidential candidate attracted roughly the same number of viewers to his convention acceptance speech Thursday as Obama did before the Democrats last week, according to Nielsen Media Research.

The Republican convention was the most-watched convention on television ever, beating a standard set by the Democrats a week earlier.

This week's ratings, with an average of 34.5 million viewers watching the GOP convention over three days, proved people are becoming more interested in what the Republicans have to say. The Democrats had an average audience of 30.2 million over four days, Nielsen said.

"No one really thought they had it in them in terms of pulling off this amazing convention," said Jay Wallace, vice president for news and editorial at Fox News Channel. But Hurricane Gustav pulled people into the news networks over the weekend, he said. People also were intensely curious about McCain's pick of Sarah Palin as a running mate, he said.

Nielsen said that 38.9 million people watched McCain accept the GOP nomination Thursday on either ABC, CBS, NBC, CNN, Fox News Channel or MSNBC. PBS, which has a more imprecise estimate based on samples in a few big cities, said 3.5 million watched on its network.

Last week, Obama's speech in Denver was seen by 38.4 million on 10 commercial networks, and an additional 4 million on PBS.

That's McCain, 42.4 million, to Obama, 42.4 million.

No one can really tell who truly had the biggest audience, because C-SPAN also showed the speeches, and Nielsen doesn't measure the channel's viewers.

McCain caught a break Thursday when the opening NFL game between the New York Giants and Washington Redskins finished a few minutes before he took the stage, Wallace said.

More men (17.9 million) watched McCain speak than watched Obama (16.2 million), Nielsen said.

Fox was the most-watched network all three nights of the GOP convention, duplicating its feat from the 2004 GOP convention. CNN was the most popular destination for Obama's speech.

Fox had 9.2 million viewers for McCain's speech, followed by NBC - which aired the football game - at 8.7 million. ABC, CBS, CNN and MSNBC followed in order, Nielsen said.

Thursday's edition of Fox's "The O'Reilly Factor," featuring the first part of Bill O'Reilly's interview with Obama, was the second-highest rated edition of his show ever, topped by a night in March 2003 when the Iraq war was breaking out.

DIGEST


BYLINE: FROM STAFF AND WIRE REPORTS


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A4


LENGTH: 478 words


ST. PAUL, MINN.

Danforth decries attacks on Palin

Former Sen. John C. Danforth, R-Mo., said attacks lobbed at Republican vice presidential nominee Sarah Palin remind him of the criticism hurled at Clarence Thomas after he was nominated to the U.S. Supreme Court in 1991.

Danforth, who used to be Thomas' boss in the Missouri attorney general's office, led the contentious Senate fight that won Thomas' confirmation.

"This is very reminiscent of what was done to Clarence - off-the-wall attacks,'' said Danforth, referring to left-wing bloggers, some women's groups and media inquiries into Palin's personal life.

"They want to smash her as a person,'' he said, adding: "She's tough ... she won't be smashed."

MINNEAPOLIS

Palin points finger at Obama's supporters

In a fundraising letter Thursday, Palin blamed supporters of the Democratic presidential ticket for spreading "misinformation and flat-out lies" about her and her family. "The Obama-Biden Democrats have been vicious in their attacks directed toward me, my family and John McCain," the letter said.

But Palin's spokeswoman later said Barack Obama's campaign was not responsible. That campaign has raised questions about Palin's qualifications, but Obama himself has said her family should be off-limits. He also said he would fire any staffer who talks about it.

Asked who was to blame, the spokeswoman said, "You want me to tick through all the stuff that's been ticking through all the blogs the past few days? "

SPRINGFIELD, ILL.

Community organizers defend themselves

Angry community organizers defended their work, and that of former organizer Obama, as they fought back Thursday against speakers at the Republican National Convention.

Organizers described their jobs as helping powerless people join forces to demand better schools and safer streets, often by working through churches.

On Wednesday night, former New York Mayor Rudy Giuliani referred to Obama's organizing work as "the first problem on the résumé." Palin, a former mayor, said: "I guess a small-town mayor is sort of like a community organizer, except that you have actual responsibilities."

NEW YORK

Palin's TV audience on par with Obama's

More than 41 million people tuned in to the Republican convention Wednesday to hear vice presidential candidate Sarah Palin.

That audience rivaled the one for Democratic presidential candidate Barack Obama last week. Nielsen Media Research estimated 37.2 million people watched Palin on either ABC, CBS, NBC, CNN, Fox News Channel or MSNBC. PBS estimated it had four million viewers.

Obama had 38.4 million viewers on commercial networks, and 40 million with PBS and C-SPAN included.

Those are bigger audiences than the "American Idol" finale or the Academy Awards.

Viewers were far more interested in Palin than Democratic vice presidential candidate Joe Biden, whose speech was seen by an estimated 24 million people.

SECOND EDITION


DIGEST


BYLINE: from staff and wire reports


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A4


LENGTH: 555 words


ST. PAUL

Independents will be key in vote, poll finds

A National Journal poll released Tuesday showed something remarkable for this stage of the campaign: 85 percent of Republicans are firmly for John McCain and 84 percent of Democrats are squarely behind Barack Obama.

That commitment leads pollsters to conclude that this election will be decided by independents and a relatively small number of voters in the middle.

In Missouri and other battleground states, the candidates' best route to those voters - and perhaps to victory in November - is connecting on the cluster of economic issues that are worrying the public, analysts said.

PHILADELPHIA

Vetting of Palin was thorough, McCain says

Republican John McCain said Tuesday he's satisfied that Sarah Palin's background was properly checked out before the Alaska governor joined the Republican ticket. He predicted that public excitement about her candidacy will increase after her address to the GOP convention tonight.

McCain visited firehouses in Ohio and Pennsylvania on Tuesday, and was due to arrive at the Republican National Convention in St. Paul, Minn., today.

Asked about whether Palin's background was thoroughly checked out before he selected her, McCain told reporters in Philadelphia: "The vetting process was completely thorough, and I'm grateful for the results."

WASILLA, ALASKA

Bristol's boyfriend heads to the GOP convention

Bristol Palin's boyfriend plans to join the family of the Republican vice presidential candidate at the GOP convention.

Levi Johnston's mother said her 18-year-old son left Alaska on Tuesday morning to join the Palin family in St. Paul, Minn.

Sherry Johnston also said there has been no pressure put on her son to marry Bristol Palin, the pregnant daughter of vice presidential candidate Sarah Palin.

"Absolutely not," Sherry Johnston told reporters. Johnston said the two teens already had plans to marry before they knew she was pregnant.

ST. PAUL, MINN.

Latest Obama, McCain ads highlight familiar themes

Barack Obama and John McCain punched up the themes that will drive the fall presidential campaign in new and expanded commercial spots airing Tuesday.

Obama linked McCain to President George W. Bush. McCain portrayed Obama and congressional Democrats as heralds of higher taxes and increased spending.

BLOOMINGTON, MINN.

Romney says he's 'not disappointed at all'

Mitt Romney said Tuesday he was "not disappointed at all" at being passed over for the Republican vice presidential nomination, and also said he wouldn't be interested in a Cabinet post in a McCain administration.

"I wish I were the Republican presidential nominee," Romney said before addressing Massachusetts delegates to the Republican National Convention. "That's what I fought hard for, but you know, John McCain won fair and square. I salute him for that, congratulate him for that."

Briefly

- Missouri's role: Missouri will be among five states to formally nominate Sen. John McCain for president this evening. That vote initially had been planned for Monday, then Tuesday due to Hurricane Gustav but was moved again when the hurricane damage was less than feared.

- Biden in Florida: Sen. Joe Biden assured older Jewish voters in Florida Tuesday that Barack Obama will be strong on protecting Israel, which he said is less secure now than when President George W. Bush took office.
