August 31, 2008 Sunday
THIRD EDITION


Sixty-five days


SECTION: EDITORIAL; Pg. B2


LENGTH: 1185 words


Over the next 65 days, Americans will be presented with two very different visions of the role of government, one anchored in the verities of the past, the other urging something that hasn't been tried for a while.

The first will be articulated by Sen. John McCain of Arizona, an old warrior who embodies duty, honor and country. At his side will Sarah Palin, moose-hunting mom and daughter of the Alaskan frontier.

Over the next four days at their convention in St. Paul, Minn., Republicans will nominate Mr. McCain and Ms. Palin as president and vice president. They'll offer a kind of post-feminist John Wayne vision of America as a place where rugged individuals will continue to unleash the forces of creative capitalism as they've always done.

The second vision will be articulated by Sen. Barack Obama of Illinois, the personification of a new polyglot, transracial America, and Sen. Joe Biden of Delaware, who's been agitating for change for most of his 65 years.

Last week in Denver, the Democrats nominated the Obama-Biden ticket as ready, indeed eager, to remake America - in Mr. Obama's words - as "one American family," where the highest goal is the common good.

The race is on.

The messages are shorthand, and the whole truth is far more complicated and nuanced than the message makers would have us believe. America is 300 million people with almost that many sets of interests. The electorate will be sliced, diced and microtargeted in a million ways between now and Nov. 4, the messages amplified and distorted by hundreds of millions of dollars worth of television commercials. Much of the message won't stand up to scrutiny.

Consider Mr. McCain's choice of Ms. Palin as his running mate, Calamity Jane to his Wild Bill Hickok. Here's the standard bearer of the party whose base is old white men choosing a young woman as his running mate, a bold attempt to capitalize on cracks inside that happy Democratic family. Sen. Hillary Clinton of New York, in perhaps the best speech of her political career, did her best last week to spackle over those cracks, but it remains to be seen whether the patches will hold.

It is insulting to think that Mrs. Clinton's only attraction to voters was her gender. And aside from their paired X chromosomes, Mrs. Clinton and Ms. Palin appear to have little in common. The former has been in politics most of her life, the latter only since 1992, when she joined the Wasilla, Alaska, City Council the same year Mrs. Clinton was helping her husband become president of the United States.

One is pro-choice; the other pro-life. One is anathema to family-values voters; the other is a champion. One was a feminist lawyer; the other was elected Miss Congeniality in the 1984 Miss Alaska contest.

Ms. Palin's elective experience is limited to town politics in Wasilla, an Anchorage suburb with a population of 8,471, and 20 months as governor of a state with two-thirds the population of St. Louis County. So in his first presidential-level decision, John McCain placed her in position to be within one beat of a 72-year-old heart from the presidency.

And to think, the motto of the Republican Convention is "Country First."

Mr. McCain may have done the Democrats a favor in selecting Ms. Palin. By comparison, with eight years in the Illinois Senate and four in the United States Senate, Mr. Obama now looks like a grizzled veteran. His choice of Mr. Biden, a 36-year Senate veteran, looks ... dare we say it ... presidential.

Thanks to Mrs. Clinton's graciousness, however belated, the Democrats managed to get out of Denver looking unified. Their convention was carefully choreographed to allow all the fractious elements of the party a moment in the sun, though plenty of sunscreen was applied. When Rep. Jose Serrano from the Bronx submitted the text for the speech he delivered at Monday's session for approval, the Obama campaign scrubbed it of the line, "let us build bridges of friendship and cooperation with our Southern neighbors."

No sense raising the sticky issue of immigration reform, particularly as Mr. McCain himself supports (or at least he used to) giving undocumented immigrants a pathway to U.S. citizenship.

A bigger problem for the Democrats this fall is the very audaciousness of what Mr. Obama represents. Not only is he the first African-American to be nominated for president by a major party, but also he is challenging Americans to confront change and embrace it.

His speech Thursday night was not soaring rhetoric but a litany of the dislocation that Americans feel: "[O]ur nation is at war, our economy is in turmoil and the American promise has been threatened once more. Tonight, more Americans are out of work and more are working harder for less. More of you have lost your homes and even more are watching your home values plummet. More of you have cars you can't afford to drive, credit card bills you can't afford to pay and tuition that's beyond your reach."

Mr. Obama dealt forthrightly with the fact that fundamental assumptions of the last 50 years have been shaken: Gasoline no longer is cheap and plentiful, good jobs are disappearing and higher education may be out of reach for many families. Economic models aren't holding, social boundaries are falling and African-Americans, Hispanic Americans and Asian-Americans are crowding the table, which feels threatening to those whose own place at the table no longer feels secure.

His message was that America has to change and adapt and build a bigger table to contain what he called "the promise of America."

Mr. Obama and Mr. Biden will spend the fall laying the blame for America's failure to adapt on President George W. Bush, and by extension, on Mr. McCain. They will try to sell the notion, embodied in Mr. Obama himself, that Americans of every race share common goals and aspirations and that government has a role in helping us achieve those goals.

"Our government should work for us, not against us," he said. "It should help us, not hurt us. It should ensure opportunity not just for those with the most money and influence, but for every American who is willing to work.

"That's the promise of America, the idea that we are responsible for ourselves, but that we also rise or fall as one nation; the fundamental belief that I am my brother's keeper; I am my sister's keeper."

That's not a new vision for America, but it is one that has fallen on hard times in the last 40 years. The "Me Generation" did damage to it in the 1960s and 1970s; the Reagan revolution undermined it in the 1980s; the booming economic times in the 1990s made it less pressing; and the Bush administration of this decade all but killed it.

Mr. McCain and Ms. Palin have the easier job, selling the idea that we can have change without changing very much, that the market will cure our ills with just a tweak here and there, that if government would just stay out the way, the good ship America will right itself.

These two threads - the common good and individual rights - have run through American history since the Pilgrims hit our shores. Balancing them always has been the trick. It remains so this fall.

August 31, 2008 Sunday
THIRD EDITION


10,000 turn out for McCain and running mate


BYLINE: By William Douglas and Margaret Talev McClatchy Newspapers The Associated Press contributed to this report.


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A14


LENGTH: 549 words


DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Pa.


Republican John McCain showed off his vice presidential running mate, Alaska Gov. Sarah Palin, in the key battleground state of Pennsylvania Saturday as Democratic rival Barack Obama's campaign aired a new TV ad urging voters not be distracted by McCain putting a woman on the GOP ticket.

Ten thousand people filled the stands and infield of Southwestern Pennsylvania's 3,200-seat Consol Energy Park - home of independent baseball's Washington Wild Things - to see McCain introduce his running mate. Palin is only the second woman in American history to be a vice presidential candidate from a major party.

"I had a lot of good people to choose from," McCain told the enthusiastic crowd. "I have found the right partner. ... I found someone who has accomplished reform, who has accomplished and managed a multibillion-dollar budget and fought against corruption and the failed politics of the past."

Palin told the crowd she's not interested in being vice president to maintain the status quo.

"I didn't get into government to do the safe and easy thing," she said. "It's like the that old adage 'A ship in harbor is safe, but that's not why the ship is built.'"

Palin repeated many of the lines she delivered in her introductory speech Friday in Dayton, Ohio, including remarks praising Sen. Hillary Clinton for breaking the political glass ceiling and collecting 18 million votes in the Democratic presidential primaries.

But where the Clinton line drew cheers in Dayton, it was lustily booed in this town, located about 30 miles southwest of Pittsburgh.

McCain and Palin were headed to St. Louis later Saturday and were to appear at a rally Sunday in O'Fallon, Mo. Later Sunday, the candidates planned to fly to Mississippi to check on people preparing for Hurricane Gustav.

Democrats' ad

Obama and running mate Joe Biden, meanwhile, stumped in Ohio Saturday. At the same time, their campaign unveiled a 30-second national TV ad that says McCain's selection of Palin should not obscure the fact that McCain candidacy represents a continuation of President George W. Bush's policies.

"So, while this may be his running mate," an announcer says with a photo of Palin on the screen, "America knows this is McCain's agenda." The visual then switches to a shot of McCain and Bush. "We can't afford four more years of the same."

Obama himself offered a noncommittal assessment of Palin during a television interview released Saturday. Speaking during a taping of CBS' "60 Minutes," Obama said of Palin, "Obviously, she's a fine mother and an up and coming public servant" but added "It's too early for me to gauge what kind of running mate she'll be. My sense is that she subscribes to John McCain's agenda."

Obama and Biden hit the diner circuit in Ohio before attending a funeral service in Cleveland for the late U.S. Rep. Stephanie Tubbs Jones. The Ohio Democrat was a member of the Congressional Black Caucus and a staunch support of Hillary Clinton during the primaries.

Obama told reporters he was "deeply concerned" about the threat of Hurricane Gustav to New Orleans and had asked his staff to monitor the situation.

McCain said in a taped segment of "Fox News Sunday" that this week's Republican National Convention in St. Paul, Minn., might be trimmed or altered because of the threat from the storm.

August 29, 2008 Friday
THIRD EDITION


LETTERS FROM READERS


SECTION: EDITORIAL; Pg. C10


LENGTH: 1363 words


Cahokia Mounds site is doing quite well under state control

Regarding "Proposal may incite turf war" (Aug. 24): So, a couple of out-of-town academics want to move decision-making for Cahokia Mounds State Historic Site from Springfield, Ill., to Washington, D.C. Professors Thomas Emerson and Timothy Paukatet fawn over the alleged superiority of the National Park System and claim Illinois' stewardship is lacking.

Are there no resource issues in the National Park Service? As a visitor to the National Parks from 1952 to the present, I can catalog a litany of neglect and disrepair in our nation's parks.

In 2008, an NPS contractor recruited and imported young people for seasonal work from Singapore, among other places. Young Americans who might live near the parks were denied jobs in their homeland. Do the professors favor such distortion, pirating of resources and intrusion into America's heartland?

At the Visitors' Center at Mesa Verde National Park, there is no access for mobility-challenged people. Do the professors favor excluding the differently abled from the experience of Cahokia Mounds as well?

As a volunteer for more than seven years at the Cahokia Mounds exhibit area, I have heard appreciation and praise from visitors from around the world. One gentleman from the Washington, D.C., area compared the museum favorably to ones he had visited in Beijing and Moscow.

If the professors really want to enrich the site, let them buy a table at the Museum Society's next trivia night. Otherwise, they would do well to stay within their area of expertise: archeology. The suggestion to transfer "the Mounds" to the National Park Service is clumsy and ill-informed. It does not satisfy the needs of the site.

Richard V. Wagner | Highland

Like flies to honey

A developer is calling on Affton ("Developer eyes Affton, seeks to buy businesses, 120 homes," Aug. 19). You almost can hear him saying, "Hello, Affton. You have such a nice climate, I would like to visit for awhile. If I enjoy my stay, I might even buy homes and businesses. I might make part of Gravois a 'commercial destination point.'"

St. Louis County Councilman John Campisi expects that some form of economic incentive would be offered, possibly tax increment financing. Mr. Campisi said, "I would not support this plan unless residents are offered at least one-and-a-half times the 2007 assessed value of their homes." Mr. Campisi should have told the homeowners and business people, "Remember Sunset Hills."

Property owners should sign no papers and make no comment until they have a certified check in their hands.

To the developer, Mr. Campisi should say, "Show me the money first; then we will talk."

The Sunset Hills homeowners bought new homes and committed to new mortgages, only to learn the developer had no money. What happened to those folks?

Missouri's revised eminent domain laws are not property-rights friendly. As the law now is written, Missouri will attract out-of-state developers like flies to honey. We can stop eminent domain abuse only by changing the state constitution. And soon.

Aim Calzone | Manchester

Life's injustices

It is one of life's injustices that young men must fight the wars that older men begin. To a great extent, therefore, the lives and hopes of the present student generation turn on the wisdom and judgment of the men of an older generation to whom the people have entrusted political power. Surely, considering what they have at stake, it is not improper for young people to question the wisdom and judgment of the makers of our foreign policy. Surely it is the right of citizens in a democracy, especially citizens of military age, to ascertain that the great decisions of war and peace are made with care and deliberation.

The calling of public men to account unquestionably adds to their burdens, but the convenience of policy-makers is not sufficient reason to shut down public discussion. The responsibilities of high office are burdensome, indeed, but they are borne, let it be remembered, by men who actively sought or freely accepted them, men who accepted not only the obligation to use power, but also the obligation to account for its use.

Edward Johnson | St. James, Mo.

Haters and traitors

Gratitude to President George W. Bush for his unrelenting courage in keeping America from being attacked again on his watch is ingrained deeply in many of us.

Disaster might have ensued if we had left Saddam Hussein available to al-Qaida. That would have been very dangerous to all Americans.

If our military had been allowed to respond as the Greatest Generation did in World War II, the war would have ended almost immediately, with little bloodshed and cost.

The opposition to Mr. Bush has been demeaning and unintelligent. If you find fault, you must have proof, not repeated lies from haters and losers. The opposition's contribution has been hate rather than help. They prevented Mr. Bush from performing in the best interest of our country. Lies embolden the enemy and should be challenged in court during war.

Mr. Bush and his wife, Laura Bush, are among the finest people on this planet. They do not express anger. I hope that their kind always will be there for us. It is left for historians to decide about haters and the traitors.

Richard Barry | Town and Country

Unconventional coverage

When I reluctantly left the sports department to become the Post-Dispatch's first television columnist in the 1970s - my beloved NBA Hawks had been sold to Atlanta - I wasn't much good at reporting inter-station rivalries and gossip, but I did devote an inordinate amount of time and space to KETC (Channel 9) and public television. DENVER

McCain picks his No. 2 but stays tight-lipped

Republican presidential candidate John McCain decided on a running mate early Thursday, and one top prospect, Minnesota Gov. Tim Pawlenty, abruptly canceled numerous public appearances.

The Arizona senator will appear with his No. 2 at an Ohio rally today, aides said, though they provided no details on whom McCain had picked.

Others believed to be in contention for the No. 2 slot on the GOP ticket included former Massachusetts Gov. Mitt Romney, who was meeting with donors throughout California, and Democrat-turned-independent Sen. Joe Lieberman of Connecticut, who was vacationing on New York's Long Island.

Former Pennsylvania Gov. Tom Ridge, too, was still a possibility, as was the idea that McCain would choose a dark horse from any number of names that have circulated.

DENVER

Close race is due to fear of change, Gore says

Al Gore told Democrats Thursday night that the 2008 election is close because defenders of the status quo "are desperately afraid of the change Barack Obama represents." And the former vice president said he knows something about close elections.

Gore said that if he had been elected in 2000, the United States would not be bogged down in Iraq or facing "a self-inflicted economic crisis."

"John McCain ... is now openly endorsing the policies of the Bush-Cheney White House and promising to actually continue them, the same policies all over again." Gore said he's for recycling, "but that's ridiculous."

DENVER

Obama supporters push for new voters

In the hours before Obama made his entrance, supporters at Invesco Field were asked to text messages and make phone calls from specially tailored call sheets as part of an unprecedented effort to mobilize voters and get nonvoters to register.

Obama's campaign has identified 55 million voting age Americans across the country who are not registered to vote. Campaign officials estimate more than two-thirds would vote for Obama.

DENVER

McCain congratulates Obama in TV ad

John McCain is airing a one-evening-only ad with a simple message for Barack Obama: "Job well done." The ad coincides with Obama's nomination acceptance speech.

The ad was to run Thursday on national cable television before and after Obama's address.

McCain says: "Senator Obama, this is truly a good day for America. Too often the achievements of our opponents go unnoticed. So I wanted to stop and say, congratulations. How perfect that your nomination would come on this historic day. Tomorrow, we'll be back at it. But tonight Senator, job well done."

August 26, 2008 Tuesday
THIRD EDITION


Unity . . . sort of


SECTION: EDITORIAL; Pg. B6


LENGTH: 671 words


Of all the 23 gazillion words written, uttered, blogged, Twittered, texted, broadcast, cablecast, uploaded or downloaded during the first day of the Democratic National Convention, here are some of the more prescient:

- Humorist Dave Barry, writing for The National Journal Online: "Sen. [Hillary] Clinton is scheduled to address the convention Tuesday night, when she will either call her supporters to unite behind [Barack] Obama, or attempt to snatch the nomination and escape with it by helicopter to a secret mountain fortress."

- An unnamed "senior Obama supporter" tells Politico.com that aides to Mrs. Clinton negotiating over her role at the convention act "like Japanese soldiers in the South Pacific still fighting after the war is over."

Even as the Democrats in Denver try to bring unity to their fractious party (the convention's slogan, according to Mr. Barry, is, "A Unified Party, United in Unity, Together as One, Undivided"), the party's genetic coding for self-destruction was taking over.

On Sunday, the Gallup Organization released a poll saying that fewer than half of those who supported Sen. Clinton of New York during the primaries said they definitely will vote for Sen. Obama of Illinois in November. Forty-seven percent of Mrs. Clinton's supporters said they are solidly behind Mr. Obama; another 23 percent said their support is soft. Thirty percent said they'd vote for Republican Sen. John McCain of Arizona or sit out the election altogether.

The Republican Party's impressive response team was ready with a campaign spot featuring a Clinton supporter who now supports Mr. McCain, as well as a second spot questioning why Mr. Obama chose Sen. Joe Biden of Delaware over Mrs. Clinton as his running mate.

All of which suggests that when she speaks tonight in prime time, Mrs. Clinton will have the important role in the convention that she demanded, although not the precise one she wanted. She will have to be an agent of unity. Given her Bataan Death March primary campaign, it's not a role that fits her very well. She'll have to be a lot more persuasive - and, we dare say, sincere - than she has been to date.

Most voters alive today can't remember when the nominating conventions last included any meaningful suspense, when ballot followed ballot and floor whips twisted arms and made outrageous promises in return for votes. Today's conventions (of both parties) belong to marketers and message experts, to say nothing of the corporate lobbyists who tiptoe through new ethics rules (bagels for breakfast, OK; eggs for breakfast, not OK; quesadillas con queso, OK; quesadillas con carne, not OK) in wining and dining the powerful and the would-be powerful.

Political junkies have the opportunity to overdose on this all day long (Wow! Claire McCaskill!) on cable networks and websites. But the true public work of the convention is reserved for the prime-time hour between 9 and 10 p.m. each night (Central time) when the broadcast networks beam convention events to a mass audience. Michelle Obama, the candidate's wife, got last night's key slot.

Tuesday night went to Mrs. Clinton; her husband, the former president, is scheduled for a non-prime-time slot on Wednesday, playing second fiddle to Mr. Biden, presented to the world early Saturday morning as Mr. Obama's choice as running mate.

It was a solid choice. Those who know Mr. Biden only for his self-indulgent rhetorical excesses are missing one of the brightest minds in the U.S. Senate. He is a formidable campaigner; quick on his feet and deft with the needle. His foreign policy experience fills a gaping hole in Mr. Obama's bona fides. At least 90 percent of a president's job lies in picking smart, independent people to carry out his policies. If a President Obama's appointments are as good as Candidate Obama's first appointment, the nation would be well served.

But first there is a campaign to be waged and an election to be won. What Mrs. Clinton says tonight - and how she says it - could have a great deal of bearing on that.

DENVER

2004 Missouri pullout is linked to Kerry

Former Democratic National Committee Chairman Terry McAuliffe said in a sidewalk interview Monday that it wasn't his idea to withdraw all those Democratic workers from Missouri in October 2004 - an act that essentially ceded the state to Republican George W. Bush, who handily carried the state a few weeks later.

Durbin pushed Obama to run, still counsels him

Illinois' "other" senator, Dick Durbin, has a close, mentoring relationship with his fellow Illinoisan, Barack Obama. He recalls counseling Obama to think about running for president not long after Obama's seminal speech to the last Democratic convention four years ago.

"In politics, you can pick a time, but the moment has chosen you," Durbin recalls telling Obama.

Barack Obama has quietly begun airing a television ad countering an outside group that is spending $2.8 million in an attempt to highlight his recent relationship with a former 1960s radical.

The 30-second TV spot is a response to an ad by the conservative American Issues Project, a nonprofit group that questions Obama's ties to William Ayers, a founder of the Weather Underground organization that took credit for a series of bombings, including nonfatal explosions at the Pentagon and U.S. Capitol four decades ago.

"With all our problems, why is John McCain talking about the '60s, trying to link Barack Obama to radical Bill Ayers?" the Obama ad states. "McCain knows Obama denounced Ayers' crimes, committed when Obama was just 8 years old."

"I feel like I should be more worried," he said as he walked into the Pepsi Center.

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


August 26, 2008 Tuesday
THIRD EDITION


A showcase for women's clout Convention events highlight rise of female influence as Obama camp courts angry Clinton supporters


BYLINE: By Jo Mannies Post-Dispatch Political Correspondent


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A1


LENGTH: 1001 words


DATELINE: DENVER


Without women, Democrats can't win. Missouri Democratic delegate Kristy Manning of St. Peters knows it, and so does her party's presumptive presidential nominee, Barack Obama.

The importance of female voters to Democrats has been a given since 1980, when experts began realizing that women as a bloc tend to lean Democratic, in some cases by double digits.

And because women have cast a majority of the nation's presidential votes since 1964, their strong preference for a particular Democrat can help that candidate weather the usual leanings of male voters toward Republicans.

In other words, if female voters defect in large numbers, the Democrat loses.

That's a key reason why so many of this year's convention events in Denver celebrate and promote the rising political influence of women since they garnered the right to vote 88 years ago today.

The importance of female voters also helps explain why Obama and the Democratic Party can't afford to ignore the lingering concerns - and in some cases outright anger - expressed by some supporters of Obama's last major rival for the nomination, Hillary Rodham Clinton.

"A lot of women did believe that this was going to be 'Year of the Woman,'" said state Sen. Joan Bray, D-University City, one of Missouri's Clinton delegates at the convention. "When a lot of expectations are made and not met, there's some threshing about as a result."

U.S. House Speaker Nancy Pelosi acknowledged to reporters that Democrats are not yet united after the bitter primary fight. "But to stay wallowing in all of this is not productive," she said.

Others are more blunt.

"A lot of girls are pissed," said Democratic consultant Judi Roman.

Roman is among dozens of St. Louis area Clinton supporters who aren't delegates but made the trip to Denver anyway to cheer her on during tonight's prime-time speech.

Clinton and Obama were working out a deal to give her some votes in the Democratic roll call for president. Democratic officials involved in the negotiations said Monday that the plan calls for a state-by-state vote for the presidential nomination Wednesday night, with delegates casting their ballots for Clinton or Obama.

But the voting would be cut off after several states, the officials said, perhaps ending with New York, when Clinton herself would call for nomination of Obama by acclamation from the convention floor.Manning, the St. Peters delegate, says she hopes tonight's celebration of Clinton's second-place finish and her expected endorsement of Obama will persuade her supporters to follow suit. But Manning, 29, observed that resentment is particularly strong among older women who see Clinton's defeat "through the prism of all they had to face growing up."

Betty Van Uum, a Democrat and the first woman elected to the St. Louis County Council in the 1970s, recalls how she and other party activists worked so hard to force through the decades-old requirement that at least half of the Democrats' presidential delegates be women. The national Republican Party doesn't have that mandate.

And it's not just Clinton's defeat that upsets some of her allies in St. Louis. Feelings are still raw, said Roman, from the narrow loss Aug. 5 of another Democratic woman, state Rep. Margaret Donnelly, in the party's contest for Missouri attorney general.

Van Uum, like Bray, emphasized that she will vote for Obama. But Van Uum recounted the private talk she has heard from other Clinton supporters who won't back him.

"It's stunning to me, the level of their fury," Van Uum said.

U.S. Sen. Claire McCaskill, D-Mo., says she has felt the brunt of some of that anger since she decided months ago to endorse Obama instead of Clinton.

"I think it's fair to say that a number of my supporters are upset with me," said McCaskill, who also addressed the convention Monday night. "I'm not sure some will ever enthusiastically support me again."

McCaskill agrees that Obama risks defeat in November if Clinton's women supporters don't embrace his candidacy. For that reason, she said, the candidate and all Democrats must emphasize the stakes if Republican John McCain is elected president instead.

McCain has opposed efforts to protect women from discrimination in pay, and to expand access to day care and early childhood education. He also opposes abortion rights, an important issue to many Democratic women.

Yolanda Wheat, vice chairwoman of the Missouri Democratic Party, was neutral during the primary contest but now backs Obama. She says party leaders "have got to forcefully and effectively address the issues that women care about on a daily basis."

Such concerns include the rising costs of milk and gasoline, and the growing lack of access to health care, Wheat said. "Those concerns aren't exclusive to women, but they will greatly influence who they vote for in November."

McCain's campaign says he will attract more female voters with his promise to keep taxes low and his strong stance on national security issues. McCain also has begun to reach out to Clinton's supporters with a new TV ad that recounts some of her criticisms of Obama during their primary fight.

But Obama campaign manager David Plouffe said in a briefing Monday to reporters from targeted battleground states, including Missouri, that the Democratic ticket plans to make it clear to female voters in stark terms what they risk if McCain wins. He cited McCain's anti-abortion statements at the Saddleback Forum, where he also pledged to appoint more socially conservative judges to the U.S. Supreme Court.

Gail Elble, president of the St. Louis Stonewall Democrats, a gay-rights group, said her fears about McCain's potential Supreme Court appointments are a major reason why she will vote for Obama - despite her lingering resentment over the party's treatment of Clinton.

But for now, Elble - sporting a vest packed with pro-Democrat and pro-Clinton campaign buttons - says she can't bring herself to campaign for Obama. No pro-Obama button is on her vest.

Explained Elble with a sigh, "I'm not there yet."

WASHINGTON

White powder in letter to McCain not anthrax

The suspicious white powder found in a letter sent to a Colorado campaign office for John McCain is not anthrax or another lethal substance, officials determined Friday.

The Colorado National Guard Civil Support Team completed its testing of the powder early Friday, Guard spokesman Rick Breitenfeldt said. Officials do not know what the powder is, but they determined it is not lethal.

Thursday's scare had sent at least 19 people to hospitals for testing. Officials said the threatening letter was sent by a prison inmate.

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


August 21, 2008 Thursday
THIRD EDITION


The latest buzz: Biden is Democrats' choice Delaware senator could address Obama's lack of experience and his reluctance to attack.


BYLINE: By Nedra Pickler THE ASSOCIATED PRESS


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A4


LENGTH: 764 words


DATELINE: CHICAGO


Sen. Joe Biden has emerged as the clear favorite among Democrats to be Barack Obama's running mate for his understanding of foreign policy in grave global times and his fighting spirit against the rival Republican ticket.

Obama is keeping his decision quiet, but his staff in Chicago and party activists who await a decision are buzzing about Biden in large part because he can address two of Obama's biggest weaknesses - his lack of experience, especially on world affairs, and his reluctance to go on the attack. The speculation among Democrats is less of an indicator of whether Biden will ultimately be Obama's pick, and more of a recognition of the challenges their candidate faces at this pivotal moment in the race.

Obama plans to appear with his newly selected running mate Saturday, with the pick announced via text message to supporters. Obama also is widely thought to be considering Govs. Kathleen Sebelius of Kansas and Tim Kaine of Virginia, and Sen. Evan Bayh of Indiana.

Biden, 65, was first elected to represent Delaware in 1972. He is a charismatic and hard-charging campaigner with a compelling personal story - his wife and daughter were killed in a car accident a few weeks after he was first elected, but two sons survived serious injuries in the same crash. Biden commuted home to Wilmington daily from the Senate to care for them. The older son, Beau, is now Delaware's attorney general and a National Guard member whose unit is being deployed to Iraq in October.

Biden got another scare 10 years ago, when two brain aneurysms kept him out of the Senate for several months.

Biden returned Monday from a trip to Georgia at the invitation of the embattled country's president, a well-timed reminder of the value he could bring to Obama's ticket.

Obama's GOP rival John McCain brings a military background and leadership on the Senate Armed Services Committee. Obama only has served three years in Washington, but Biden is chairman of the Senate Foreign Relations Committee on which he has served for 33 years. Polls show the race between Obama and GOP rival John McCain tightening, and Obama is responding by stepping up his attacks in speeches and commercials targeted to key states. Obama has never been entirely comfortable going negative, a move that threatens his call for civility in politics, but Biden has never shied from a fight.

Biden is "passionate, he's articulate and he's persuasive," said Democratic consultant Steve McMahon, among those who consider Biden Obama's smartest pick. "I think he would do for Senator Obama what Lyndon Johnson did for John Kennedy. He's got serious foreign policy experience, a long and distinguished Senate résumé and he is one of the most effective surrogates that Senator Obama has right now who can go toe-to-toe with any Republican on any issue at any time."

Obama could have been describing Biden when he said in a speech Tuesday that he wants his running mate to be "somebody who is mad right now" about the state of the economy, an independent who will speak out when he's wrong and help him through major issues.

obama barbs

During the Democratic primary when he also ran for the presidential nomination, Biden often made the most memorable impression in debates even though he was barely registering in the polls. He got big laughs for accusing Republican candidate Rudy Giuliani of mentioning three things in every sentence - "a noun, a verb, and 9/11" - and also leveled barbs at Obama.

He said he didn't think Obama was ready to be president, saying it's "not something that lends itself to on-the-job training." He offended some blacks when he tried to compliment Obama as "the first mainstream African-American who is articulate and bright and clean." He dropped out of the race after a poor showing in Iowa.

Republicans would be sure to revive Biden's criticisms of Obama and already envision a line of attack that says Obama is so inexperienced he needs a running mate who has been in Washington longer than McCain.

Biden is famous for being able to talk at length - sometimes a mind-numbing length - on any topic, but he has enhanced his standing in the vice presidential race by avoiding discussion of it.

Biden bluntly acknowledged that he'd take the job if asked, while jokingly warning Obama might not want him.

"I made it clear to him and everybody else, I never worked for anybody in my life," he told reporters last month. "I got here when I was 29. I never had a boss. I don't know how I'd handle it."

LETTERS FROM READERS


SECTION: EDITORIAL; Pg. C10


LENGTH: 1404 words


Penalizing hospitals for unavoidable error will harm health care

Most health care providers are dedicated to providing quality care in a compassionate, caring manner. We work with patients and their families for optimal care. We encourage and appreciate patient and family participation to ensure that we are aware of special needs, previously prescribed medication and care routines. Patients and their families always should feel comfortable asking questions about their care so that they understand how we are treating their medical issues and can facilitate safety initiatives to prevent injury or error.

The editorial "The economics of error" (Aug. 6) noted that Medicare plans to stop paying doctors and hospitals for extra costs associated with medical errors. Private insurance companies surely will follow. What the editorial failed to point out is that health care providers already are overburdened with more than 47 million uninsured people. Patients are admitted to hospitals with acute and complex medical problems. The resources and reimbursement rates are shrinking, and an ongoing nursing shortage exacerbates quality-of-care concerns.

Hospitals that treat frail or high-risk patients will be penalized. Additional screenings will be done for all patients to identify potential preexisting infections that previously might not have been detected at admission. If you went to the the emergency department with a possible heart attack, would you want me to swab every orifice, draw blood and collect a urine sample before I admit you to the intensive care unit or rush you to the catheterization lab? I would have to do so to protect my hospital's reputation and provide documentation of a preexisting infection before admission or risk losing reimbursement for your care.

Instead of reducing or withholding payment for care or complications that are not always preventable, why not applaud efforts to implement best practices? Why not provide funding to train more nurses and nurse educators? Can't we work together to turn the failing health care system around?

Mary Barlett | Alhambra

Not so far away

It was awesome to see 100 Army guys parachuting from two planes at MidAmerica St. Louis Airport near O'Fallon, Ill., last week. The planes made three passes over the airport, and the men floated down from about 1,000 feet. I've only seen that in old war movies. The ceremony was moving, too, with many retired 82nd Airborne paratroopers shaking hands with the new guys and all of them thanking each other for being there.

My nephew, Mike, parachuted down with his Advanced Infantry medal in his pocket. He had been awarded the medal earlier that week. After the jump, we congratulated him, and he pulled the medal out of his pocket and gave it to his dad.

The war can seem very far away until you stand next to someone who has been there, and he hugs you.

Rosemary Kilgore | Oakville

Bandits, not kings

A-B chiefs make out like ... bandits. That's what the headline on the story "A-B chiefs to make out like kings" (Aug. 17) should have said. The Anheuser-Busch executives awarded themselves the spoils in the company's surrender. But then, kings and bandits are similar: Both gratify themselves with impunity at others' expense.

But is any reward warranted for executives who left A-B prey to its nimbler competitor? Executives justify their inexcusable compensation by claiming just the kind of skill, expertise and foresight that should have averted A-B's submission to InBev. Four hundred million dollars for the noble executives whose failure of leadership left vulnerable an Amercan icon and its employees? This can't be the reward system that Americans believe in.

Thomas Grote | Creve Coeur

Equal billing

On the front page of the Aug. 15 Post-Dispatch were three pictures of Olympic gold medalists, all men. On the first page of the Sports section were two small pictures of women competitors and another huge headline, picture and story about phenomenal gold medlist Michael Phelps.

But what about the women gold medalists? What about Nastia Liukin, whose breathtakingly exquisite gymnastic performance earned her a gold medal, closely followed by Shawn Johnson for the silver?

What about Rebecca Soni, who broke the previously held world record in the women's breaststroke?

It is thrilling to watch the superb prowess of our athletes. All of them, female and male, are a source of pride to all Americans. The women - as well as the men - deserve to have their achievements and their photographs given top billing by the Post-Dispatch.

Margaret P. Gilleo | Ladue

Omitting details

Any progress the Post-Dispatch had made in an objective approach to the presidential election, especially calling out the candidates for manipulating people with lies about offshore drilling, was stripped away with the recent article comparing the candidates on the "energy crunch" ("Quest for energy fix fuels race," Aug. 10).

Instead of providing readers with an objective look at the facts behind each candidates' proposed energy policies, the article reminded me of listening to the opinions of those who gain their sole political education from watching campaign commercials. The story did not mention presumptive Democratic presidential nominee Sen. Barack Obama's recently released eight-page energy policy plan (readily available for anyone to read online) and the experts' accolade of said plan.

Mr. Obama's energy plan offers details and well-thought economic strategies that Sen. John McCain, R-Ariz., the presumptive Republican presidential nominee, has failed to offer. I recommend the Post-Dispatch place both candidates' proposed plans in writing, without any convenient omissions, and let the readers formulate their own opinions based on actual facts.

This article painted a wholly inaccurate and incomplete picture of the two candidates' stances on the energy economy. I was disappointed and alarmed that such a biased piece might be printed anywhere other than the editorial page.

Tim Maher | St. Louis

Time to rebuild

Eric Mink's column "Bush's attack on the law; we will need a long time to heal" (Aug. 13) was a strong and effective analysis of the way the Bush administration has undermined the basic institutions of our democratic government. It should be posted in every college and high school across this land. Before it is too late, the call must come to begin rebuilding our most cherished American instruments of government and remove them from the hands of the political hacks and ideologues planted in every agency in Washington.

Most damaging is the undermining of our moral outrage and the dilution of our most cherished ideals and practices. We now have a generation of people who assume that we can continue to evade our fiscal responsibilities, do away with the middle class, strip the central government of its authority, stack the courts and politicize the halls of justice, ignore the expert advice of our scientists, substitute pablum for performance in our educational system, continue to borrow from others as we continue our fiscal irresponsibility and wipe out the system of checks and balances, that system of delicate scales that continually adjudicates between the vital interests of powerful elements in our diverse society.

Don and Mart Morey | St. Louis

When I read President George W. Bush's reaction to Russia's recent military actions in Georgia, I cringed. His accusing Russia of "bullying and intimidation" proves my grandmother's proverb about "the pot calling the kettle black." It is beyond any explanation that Mr. Bush could accuse Russia of these tactics without admitting that his administration has done the same in Iraq.

Our country has lost credibility in the world under Mr. Bush's watch. From human rights violations in secret CIA prisons abroad to domestic spying on U.S. citizens without warrants, to knowingly misleading our country into war, this administration leaves office having ruined America's reputation as being the land of the free and the home of the brave. St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


August 19, 2008 Tuesday
THIRD EDITION


'If it ain't broke' . . . but our corporate tax system is


BYLINE: david nicklaus


SECTION: BUSINESS; Pg. D1


LENGTH: 559 words


Last week's headlines trumpeted a story guaranteed to provoke outrage: Two-thirds of American corporations pay no taxes in any given year.

"The Great Corporate Tax Heist," one website screamed. "The Corporate Free Ride," a New York Times editorial proclaimed.

So, are greedy business bigwigs conspiring to bilk Uncle Sam? The Government Accountability Office, which did the headline-grabbing study, makes no such claim. It just presents the numbers, along with a discussion of why foreign-owned companies paid less taxes than domestic ones.

Transfer pricing abuse - manipulating the prices on intracompany transactions to make them look unprofitable - might explain the disparity, the GAO says. But there are innocent explanations, too, such as the fact that many foreign-owned companies are new and not yet profitable.

The outrage dissipates, in fact, when one reads the numbers carefully. Why do two-thirds of companies pay no taxes? A lot of them are small and, according to Eric Toder of the Tax Policy Center in Washington, "they aren't what you think of as corporations with a separation of ownership and control." In many small businesses, the owner draws just enough salary to make sure most taxes are incurred at the individual level, not the corporate one.

Ah, but even among large corporations, nearly a quarter pay no income tax. Isn't that abusive on its face?

No, it's not. Most of those big deadbeats gained their no-tax status the old-fashioned way: They lost money. Ford, General Motors, American Airlines and Citigroup are among the big companies currently engaging in this tax dodge, and they have plenty of company. Only a few got their liability to zero using special provisions in the law, such as credits or tax-loss carryforwards.

If you want to read a shocking fact about our corporate tax system, it's this: Our marginal tax rate on corporate income is the second-highest among countries in the Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development.

In fact, our combined state and federal rate of 39.3 percent is far above the OECD average of 26.6 percent. The conservative Tax Foundation claims that the high rate hurts the U.S. economy by giving multinational companies an incentive to invest elsewhere.

Toder takes issue with that argument: He says the effective tax rate, after all deductions and credits, is what matters for investment decisions. Nevertheless, he agrees that the nation would benefit from an overhaul of its corporate tax system.

"It would be desirable if we could eliminate some preferences, have a broader base of taxable income and lower the rates," Toder said. "Higher rates give companies an incentive to shift their income to lower-tax countries. You would have less of that profit-shifting going on if you could bring the rates into line with our trading partners."

There's some hope that the next president will tackle this issue. Republican candidate John McCain's economic plan calls for reducing corporate tax rates. Democratic candidate Barack Obama wants to close corporate loopholes, and he told The Wall Street Journal in June that he'd be open to lowering rates, too.

We may have the beginning of a consensus, then. The real message of the GAO study isn't that evil, greedy corporations are manipulating the system and evading taxes; it's that our corporate tax system is broken and badly needs fixing.

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


August 17, 2008 Sunday
FOURTH EDITION


Laughing matter Election brings new voices in political humor


BYLINE: By Diane Toroian Keaggy ST. LOUIS POST-DISPATCH


SECTION: A&E; Pg. F1


LENGTH: 1407 words


How old is John McCain?

So old he remembers when Johnny Carson quipped, "Did you know Richard Nixon is the only president whose formal portrait was painted by a police sketch artist?"

Back in the 1970s, "The Tonight Show" was America's top source for political humor. Today, comedians can mock McCain's age on any number of network and cable stations, blogs and websites. And they're not just cracking jokes about McCain's prostate, but also John Edwards' sleazy dalliance, the media's infatuation with Barack Obama and our politicians' inherent inability to pick a position and stick to it.

Perhaps the biggest viral smash came this month when online comedy site Funny or Die spoofed a recent McCain ad. In case you haven't already seen the video, it features a bikini-clad Paris Hilton proclaiming she's "not from the olden days and I'm not promising change like that other guy ... I'm just hot." She then delivers an energy plan that sounds pretty darn smart. The video has drawn 7 million views and spawned spoofs of its own. But before Hilton, there was Obama Girl, the super-hot chick who sang the hit, "I Got a Crush on Obama" ("Universal health care reform. It makes me warm ... You can Ba-Rock me tonight"). That video instantly turned producer BarelyPolitical.com into a cultural phenomenon. Founder Ben Relles said the birth of YouTube has unleashed America's funniness.

"In 2004, there was no YouTube," Relles said. "People weren't creating and posting videos online. There was JibJab (a website), but that was done by a production crew. What you're seeing now are individuals participating in the process and that's really exciting."

Like the news, funny now happens around the clock. No need to wait for late-show monologues or the morning comics. Hours, sometimes minutes, after news breaks, comedians are on the job tearing down our nation's leaders.

"So often with these it's not the huge production values that make them work," said Relles, whose typical budget is about $200 per video. "In a couple of hours, we can turn something around. It's the ideas behind them, not the money."

It's a great time to be young and cynical and not just because of the Internet, said Second City actor Seth Weitberg, 27. "Deface the Nation," Second City's take on American politics, will stop at the Touhill Performing Arts Center Sept. 17-20.

"What makes this such a fun election is that there is so much awareness. It's like when you're at the water cooler and everybody watched the same television show the night before. People are just really ready to be engaged," Weitberg said. "Then, of course, there's just so much to rip on."

New stars of political humor

Here are a few of the fresh comic voices who have made this election so laughable.

Rob Riggle and Larry Wilmore "Daily Show" correspondents

www.thedailyshow.com and www.indecision2008.comJoining "The Daily Show" in 2006, Riggle and Wilmore are comic equals to the ever-funny Jon Stewart. The fearless Riggle has covered the war in Iraq, the Olympics and a Barack Obama rally, where cameras caught him screaming like a little girl at a Jonas Brothers concert. Senior black correspondent Wilmore - we love the title - is less doting. Last spring, Wilmore predicted Obama's running mate would turn him into "the magical negro," i.e. "the guy who sits on the sidelines teaching the white guy valuable lessons like Bagger Vance, the big magical brother from 'The Green Mile' or when Shaq played Kazaam."

BarelyPolitical.com

Mixing sharp analysis and hot chicks, BarelyPolitical made a big splash in 2007 with its debut video "Crush on Obama" starring "Obama Girl" Amber Lee Ettinger. Since then, the site has produced more than 100 videos, including "Al Gore's Voicemail" where presidential hopefuls try to curry favor with the former VP (McCain: "I want to bomb Iran using ... solar powered nuclear weapons.") and "Hillary Wasn't Lying!," which features faux footage of heads exploding while a calm Clinton greets children in "snipery" Bosnia.

236.com

The comic arm of the Huffington Post (already considered a joke by some), 23/6 features videos, columns by liberal comedians, animated comics and running features such as "If they IM'd," imaginary instant-message exchanges between politicians. In the "Swift Kids for Truth," adorable toddlers mispronounce Barack Obama's name ("Black-o-Rama? Bama-lama? Do we want a president with a name like that?"), and "A Message to Voters Older than McCain" lays out McCain's plan for seniors: "Apple butter will no longer be a second-class condiment. Dinner shouldn't be so spicy. Sen. McCain promises never to negotiate with Prussia. And yes, if elected, Sen. McCain will vote yes on cats being soft."

The Margins of Error blog

www.hmatkin.blogspot.com

Average Americans are getting into the act, posting their own columns, cartoons and videos to the Internet. But our favorite isn't a registered voter, but Australian Hugh Matkin, 24. His video "Barack Roll," splices the phrases and words from various Obama speeches to re-create the Rick Astley hit, "Never Gonna Give You Up." Even funnier is the unaltered clip of Obama getting down with Ellen DeGeneres. He gives Hillary the same treatment in another viral parody. We can't tell you the name, but let's just say Sarah Silverman and Matt Damon would be proud.

Ahmed Ahmed

www.ahmed-ahmed.com

Ahmed Ahmed, 28, only lived in Egypt for his first month of life, but his heritage has provided plenty of fodder for his stand-up routine. Ahmed appeared in the not-so-funny "Vince Vaughn's Wild West Comedy Show," but really knocked them dead - eek, wrong phrase for someone constantly mistaken for a terrorist - during the "Axis of Evil" comedy tour, co-starring Maz Jobrani and Aron Kader. "Whenever I get on a plane I know who the air marshal is. It's always the guy holding the People magazine upside down looking right at me," cracks Ahmed.

John McCain?

"John McCain" and "new" seem like mutually exclusive concepts. After all, the dude is "crazy old," according to "Saturday Night Live" and "looks like the guy at the hardware store who makes the keys," says David Letterman. But McCain has hit his comic stride this election cycle. He rejected federal funding to jam Gaydar on "SNL" ("That's something best left to state and local government"), revealed busybody Dwight Schrute of "The Office" would serve as his running mate on "The Daily Show" and has cracked wise about his age on all of the above ("I have the oldness it takes to protect America, honor her, love her and tell her what cute things the cat did."). McCain's new Web ad, "Fan Club," mocks American girls going ga-ga for Obama. After one girl compares Obama to Bono and another swoons over his "very soft eyes," a cheesy announcer urges viewers to "act now and don't delay. We know he doesn't have much experience and isn't ready to lead but that doesn't mean he isn't dreamy."

Keith Knight, aka Keef

www.kchronicles.com

Keith Knight calls himself the "other black cartoonist" and like "Boondocks" creator Aaron McGruder, Knight isn't afraid to use a little profanity to make a political point, often at the expense of President George W. Bush's administration. In "With Friends Like These," Bush offers McCain help winning the election ("We can start up that rumor about you having a black baby with a prostitute") while "Bush's Other Twins" shows two babies fused at the head. "But sir, it would be healthier for the two if they were separated," the surgeon tells Bush. "No. You leave church and state alone," Bush responds.

REGULARS ARE STILL CRACKING STRONG

While political comedy has many new stars, here are a few old standbys that are still going strong.

The Onion

www.theonion.com

America's most reliable source of fake news has launched "War for the White House" with in-depth candidate profiles ("Obama: Pro hope, also supports dreams." "McCain: Supports universal health care for all current and former POWs.")

'Saturday Night Live'

www.nbc.com/Saturday_Night_LiveThe late-night sketch comedy premieres Sept. 13 with four primetime political specials in October.

'The Colbert Report'

www.comedycentral.com/colbertreport

"The Daily Show" alum Stephen Colbert widens his net not only to snare politicians but the journalists who cover them.

--- ---

Watch STLtoday.com/entertainment St. Louis comedian Bill Chott explains the anatomy of a political joke. See Chott at Comedy on Parade at 8 p.m. Mondays at the Atomic Cowboy, 4140 Manchester Avenue.

Movie that denigrates people with disabilitiesis far from funny

I read with much dismay the review of the movie "Tropic Thunder" starring Ben Stiller ("Stiller's gem keeps you laughing," Aug. 12). The review completely overlooked the fact that the movie denigrates and makes fun of individuals with a mental disability. The review fails to mention that the movie often refers to the character in a subplot as a "retard."

I certainly have a sense of humor, but the blatant and careless use of the "R" word is far from humorous. In fact, the "R" word is hate speech, plain and simple. What is even more disturbing is that absolutely no positive information is put forth about mental disabilities. In one instance, a character in the movie expresses that he is ashamed of his child who has a mental disability. This movie is hateful toward anyone who has a mental disability and harmful to any organization that attempts to raise awareness of mental and intellectual disabilities.

When choosing what movie you are going to attend next, I strongly urge people to look beyond "Tropic Thunder" and spend their money on entertainment that does not resort to denigrating a segment of our society for the sake of attempted humor.

Matt Lauer | St. Louis St. Louis Metro Area Director, Special Olympics Missouri

Why pay premium price for bland, nostalgic beer?

Thanks for the "'Staff shortage" (Aug. 9), a "Short Take" on resuscitated Schlitz Beer. While Milwaukee isn't the beer capital it once was, SAB/Miller has two breweries there. Another brewery is in Chippewa Falls, Wis. And LaCrosse, Wis., is the home of the Heileman/Old Style brewery, sold by Pabst in 1999, now known as City Brewing. Wisconsin still must be considered a strong contender for brewing capital of the United States.

Pabst products are brewed under contract by others, primarily by SAB/Miller (under an agreement put together when Stroh's was divided between Pabst and Miller in 1999). Pabst's market share in 2007 was 2.8 percent, compared to 48.2 percent for Anheuser-Busch and 29.5 percent for SAB/Miller including Molson/Coors. That 2.8 percent includes sales of several traditional urban leaders: Old Style (Chicago), Stroh's (Detroit) and Schaefer's (Brooklyn/New York City).

In beer, market share is everything. Leaders have large, efficient plants and clout with suppliers, distributors and retailers. They have advertising dollars to attract young, new customers, who, thanks to brand loyalty, may buy their products for life.

Nostalgia beers such as Schlitz or Falstaff may be viable, but market share numbers tell the story. They will be a novelties intended to attract attention to a product line. Profitability requires premium pricing. But why pay premium prices for a bland American lager, when you could have a full-flavored craft brew for the same money?

Paul E. Eckler | Wildwood

If the university can afford such a plush luxury, I don't think expecting a decrease in tuition is out of the question. Unintended consequences

A letter writer recently asked why the United States is putting more troops in Afghanistan. He said the Soviet Union was unsuccessful in Afghanistan for 10 years and finally withdrew without victory. Though I understood that we could not let al-Qaida's Sept. 11, 2001, terrorist attacks, go unanswered, I shared similar concerns about ultimate success when we went into Afghanistan.

The Soviet's final withdrawal occurred in early 1989 during the few weeks we were hosting a high school exchange student from Moscow. As we all watched TV reports of the last troops crossing a bridge out of Afghanistan, he told us that when Mikhail Gorbachev announced the withdrawal a year earlier, the student's mother and all the other mothers said, "There is a God in the sky."

One reason for the Soviet defeat was assistance provided to Afghanistan rebels by the United States. Given the many complexities and changing loyalties in that region of the world, I continue to question whether "success" is possible, however it is defined. I also am concerned about future unintended consequences from U.S. actions and alliances taken in attempts to achieve that success.

Peggy Kruse | Florissant

Saving wildlife is worth any cost

It is appalling that the Bureau of Land Management and a "blue-ribbon panel" of the American Ornithologists' Union find it too costly to try to maintain wild native American species in what is left of their own habitat ("U.S. wants to cull herds of wild horses" and "Condors hang by a thread in the wild," both Aug. 10).

It is unfortunate that when wildlife becomes too bothersome to the public or interferes with income from ranching, farming or building, they get labeled as a "nuisance" instead of "endangered" or "protected," as they once were by law.

It should be a priority for the United States to keep these animals around. The article about horses said, "Efforts to date have been successful in placing 235,000 wild horses into adoption in the past 37 years"; and the California condor was "rescued from extinction" in "an elaborate and expensive recovery effort." Why not keep these efforts going?

A lot of government funds go toward "expensive" projects, many of which do not have the same goal as saving creatures that have been with us for thousands of years.

If I have anything to say about where my tax money goes, I certainly would prefer to see it spent on preserving habitat and recovery for our native American wildlife.

Mary Ann Moore | Eureka

Withholding information from review boards shows lack of transparency

Two new disclosures in court records cast further aspersions on Bishop Wilton Gregory, who led America's bishops during the high-profile years of the Catholic church's ongoing child sex abuse and cover-up scandal ("Sex abuse panel lacked key data, member reports," Aug. 9)

While head of the Belleville Diocese, Bishop Gregory won praise in 1993 for setting up a review board to look at allegations against abusive clerics. The trouble is that the former head of that panel now has testified that no information about pedophile priests prior to 1993 was given to board members. She also testified that Bishop Gregory and his staff never brought child molestation reports against the Rev. Raymond Kownacki to the board in 1994.

This is further proof of what we've long said: These hand-picked abuse panels often get no information, little information or biased information from church officials. So they end up functioning more as a public relations tool than an investigative tool.

As president of the U.S. Bishops Conference in 2002, Bishop Gregory repeatedly promised to be open and transparent about clergy sex abuse. Yet just two years later, he was found in contempt of court for refusing to turn over documents in a civil child molestation case. And in 2006, newspapers disclosed that an admitted predator priest quietly moved into the Belleville Diocese four years earlier at the very same time Bishop Gregory was touring the nation and pledging reforms. He has yet to explain his own continuing secrecy surrounding this move.

As more cases come forward and depositions are taken and documents are disclosed, it increasingly seems as though Bishop Gregory's well-crafted public image of reform is just that: a well-crafted image.

Barbara Dorris | St. Louis National Outreach Director, Survivors Network of those Abused by Priests

Moral outrage

We are a nation of hypocrites. Former Democratic presidential candidate John Edwards confessed to an extramarital affair and the nation goes crazy. We get nonstop coverage. Reporters are delighted. The columnists and the hate-mongers are breathless with glee and excitement.

In the meantime, our president and his lackeys have destroyed the Constitution, broken numerous laws, taxed the poor and rewarded the wealthy and lied.

Worst of all, President George W. Bush, who claims to be pro-life, has caused the death of hundreds of thousands of Americans and Iraqis, destroyed a country, ruined our national image and plunged us into astronomical debt, yet he suffers little or no serious criticism. Where is the moral outrage?

What Mr. Edwards did is none of my business. What Mr. Bush did and still is doing is my business.

Whatever happens in November, we deserve what we get.

Gene Dalton | Richmond Heights

The truth from Hollywood

I have followed presidential campaigns for 50 years, but I finally have to give up. Politicians and Washington insiders cannot or will not admit that the common citizen thinks that the politicians have forgotten what is important to them. To the common American, the politicians are all lost in their own gobbledegook and obviously feel that the public will accept whatever they say as intellectual brilliance.

Example: Paris Hilton's explanation of what we should do to combat the ongoing energy crises made more sense than anything we have heard from either Sen. John McCain or Sen. Barack Obama on the energy problem. She mentioned immediate steps to take to alleviate our current crises and long-range steps to take to prepare our country for our energy use in the future. Why haven't we heard anything like these basic, simple ideas from either of our presumptive presidential candidates?

Of course, Ms. Hilton's video was made as a humorous rebuttal to a McCain ad, but as often is the case anymore in D.C., our leaders don't or won't get it. They can't see that the common-sense truth is not spoken by them, but by a Hollywood "celebrity" in a funny video clip.

Randy Hoover | Florissant

Ignoring oil shale

In 1948, I was a student at St. Louis University. I toured a plant in Lousiania, Mo., that recovered oil from oil shale. Although the process worked, it cost more than crude oil. But it was a beginning.

The U.S. Geological Survey estimates that there are a trillion barrels of shale oil in the Green River Formation of Colorado, Utah and Wyoming, and all of it is on government land. There may be 50 billion barrels of crude oil in the United States.

The Republicans, who believe national defense is their forte, want to search and drill. The Democrats want to develop alternative energy. How much longer will oil shale be ignored?

Robert Burkemper | Waterloo

Putting my breakfast in your gas tank

Lower prices at the grocery store will not occur unless we stop trying to put our breakfast in our gas tanks. It is an insult to the American people that House Speaker Nancy Pelosi will not allow a vote in the House on off-shore drilling. She should be removed.

Deanna Heuermann | Hazelwood

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


August 10, 2008 Sunday
FOURTH EDITION


Quest for energy fix fuels race Barack Obama He proposes studying how to clean coal emissions, versus phasing out the fuel. John McCain He pushes for opening 45 nuclear power plants in the U.S. by 2030 in his Lexington Project.


BYLINE: By Bill Lambrecht o Post-Dispatch Washington Bureau Chief


SECTION: NEWSWATCH; Pg. B1


LENGTH: 1494 words


Giving consumers $1,000 energy rebates. Drilling for more oil offshore. Inflating our tires properly.

These and other proposals by presidential hopefuls highlighted an increasingly fractious debate over energy last week, as Americans said sky-high gas prices would play a big role in how they vote in November.

Energy experts were heartened that an overdue discussion about the nation's energy future has begun in earnest. But they worried that much of what voters hear is long on gimmickry but short on frank talk and long-term solutions.

"The campaigns are operating at a superficial level," said Robert Alvarez, a former Energy Department official in the administration of President Bill Clinton and a senior scholar at the Institute for Policy Studies, a think tank in Washington. "It's like magical thinking, with no one bothering to scratch the surface."

Kenneth Green, an energy expert at the American Enterprise Institute, said candidates were in "full-on pander mode, flailing around looking for anything that has traction in polls."

And neither candidate is addressing the biggest issue of them all, said Robert Kaufmann, director of the Center for Energy and Environmental Studies at Boston University.

That issue is an upcoming peak in global oil production followed immediately by decline as soon as the next decade, when the United States will have to be ready with affordable energy alternatives.

Experts say that even though gas prices have dipped slightly of late, consumers can expect high costs and a volatile market from here on out, starting with painful heating-price increases this winter.

As energy took center stage in the campaign last week, Republican John McCain stepped up efforts to promote offshore drilling, something that most Americans endorse and that Democrat Barack Obama also recently accepted after a change of heart.

Kaufmann says that having more drill rigs in the ocean is beside the point.

"Saying that we can drill our way out of it, this idea that every little bit helps is denying reality," he said. "And we see where denying reality has gotten us."

'Perfect storm'

Politicians reflexively avoid giving voters bad news. During a brief oil crisis in 1979, President Jimmy Carter donned a sweater, installed solar panels in the White House and delivered a solemn speech trying to enlist Americans in an energy battle that he called "the moral equivalent of war."

Carter's approval ratings sank further and he lost the presidential election to Ronald Reagan, who struck a more upbeat tone - and removed the solar panels.

What we are seeing now is far more serious than in the 1970s, "a perfect storm," says Peter Garforth, who advises businesses and governments around the world on energy problems.

That coming storm will involve:

- Uncontrolled and unpredictable energy prices.

- Interruptions in supplies.

- Environmental damage from climate change beyond what many expected.

Garforth argued that neither Obama nor McCain were communicating clearly that the nation had failed dramatically to conserve energy. Nor are the candidates saying enough about how Americans' waste - using twice as much energy per person than Europeans - is damaging the United States' ability to compete in the world.

"It may not be good politics, but it's not fair to the electorate to let them think that a few little tweaks are going to fix the problem," said Garforth, who heads Garforth International LLC, based in Ohio.

A nuclear solution?

Given the seriousness of the problems, 2008 may be recalled as the energy election. And no bolder and transforming plan has been introduced than McCain's proposal to build 45 nuclear power plants by 2030 - even though no new reactors have been ordered in the United States since the late 1970s.

"If we want to enable the technologies of tomorrow like plug-in electric cars, we need electricity to plug into," McCain said last week while touring a nuclear plant in Michigan.

Illinois is the state with the most nuclear plants. Its junior senator, Obama, also has said that nuclear power needs to be an option after the nation deals more aggressively with nuclear waste and the security of nuclear fuel.

Energy experts note that there has been little mention of Wall Street's aversion to funding nuclear energy because of risks and costs, which could leave taxpayers on the hook for more than $200 billion in loan guarantees under McCain's Lexington Project - far beyond the $18.5 billion in loan guarantees approved last year by Congress.

Building nuclear plants in France and Japan has been less challenging because those governments are financial partners with industry. Alvarez, a longtime nuclear critic, questioned whether American taxpayers were ready for what he called "state capitalism, with direct intervention in a large sector of the economy."

Nuclear Energy Institute spokesman John Keeley acknowledged the need for ongoing federal support but said investors would be playing a bigger role in coming years when they saw the dividends of billions in taxpayer loan guarantees.

"If Wall Street sees 4-8 plants built with important federal support over the next seven years, on cost and on schedule, I think you're going to see robust support from the financial sector," he said.

Conflict over coal

In a speech last month that roiled the waters among environmentalists and policymakers, Al Gore said the United States needed to end its reliance on fossil fuels within a decade because of the imminent threats of global warming.

Gore's challenge to phase out coal - which generates roughly half of the electricity in America - is one that fellow Democrat Obama has not taken up.

In a speech at Michigan State University last week, Obama reiterated his support for research into technology that would use more coal rather than phasing it out. He said that his goal was to build five coal-fired demonstration plants that capture and sequester the carbon pollution that worries Gore and environmentalists.

Meanwhile, McCain wants to commit $2 billion annually for research to develop clean-coal technology.

William Shilts, who heads the University of Illinois' Institute of Natural Resource Sustainability, argued that the candidates were wisely disregarding Gore's advice because it would be impossible to replace an equivalent amount of electricity.

"That's just not a feasible or responsible thing to say," Shilts said. "If we're going to cut down on greenhouse gases, we have to find a way to sequester them and emissions in general."

Renewable alternatives

Politicians are among those paying attention to billionaire T. Boone Pickens' recent campaign to promote renewable energies - particularly wind farms. Pickens says in an Internet message: "I have been an oil man my whole life, but this is one emergency we cannot drill our way out of."

Both major candidates point to their plans to promote wind energy, solar power, biofuels and other renewable energies. McCain wants to make tax credits that promote renewable energy permanent, although he has stressed offshore drilling of late over other options. Obama says extending the tax credits for five years would be a good start.

Neither candidate has focused heavily on solar power, still an expensive technology but potentially a breakthrough if we can believe assertions that in one hour's time, enough sunlight hits the earth to meet the world's energy needs for a year.

Michael Moynihan, an energy expert at NDN, a think tank in Washington aligned with Democrats, said the candidates needed a far more robust plan for solar power.

"On this issue, I think that Americans are ahead of the candidates of both parties," he said.

Richard Kearney, an energy expert who heads the School of Public and International Affairs at North Carolina State University, offered an explanation as to why the major candidates seem to be talking about energy in piecemeal fashion: Voters don't like to deal with complicated matters.

But Kearney said that candidates need to find a way to communicate better about energy even though the issues may be complex.

"Our energy economy is undergoing a transformation, and we're just in the early stages," he said.

August 9, 2008 Saturday
THIRD EDITION


Koster was king of TV ad spending in week before vote And over the last month, he even outspent McCain and Obama in Missouri.


BYLINE: By Jo Mannies Post-Dispatch Political Correspondent


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A8


LENGTH: 407 words


State Sen. Chris Koster, a Democrat who barely won his party's nomination for attorney general, spent more on TV ads in the state during the final week than any other candidate on Tuesday's primary ballot.

In fact, Koster is the top spender on TV campaign ads during the last month - outpacing even the two presumptive nominees for president, Republican John McCain and Democrat Barack Obama.

Koster spent at least $1.21 million over the last month to run 2,722 ad spots, most of them on TV stations in the state's three largest cities, according to TNSMI-Campaign Media Analysis Group, a nonpartisan firm monitoring campaign ad spending for the Post-Dispatch.

About a quarter of Koster's ad spending came in the final week. The ad getting the most airtime during the finals days of his campaign was a spot that targeted his two chief Democratic rivals: state Reps. Margaret Donnelly and Jeff Harris. Donnelly, of Richmond Heights, trailed Koster by less than 800 votes statewide in the final unofficial tally.

Donnelly spent at least $138,796 and Harris spent at least $220,487 on TV ads during the final week. Both candidates concentrated most of their ad buys in St. Louis and Kansas City.

Missourians for Honest Leadership, a campaign committee bankrolled largely by Koster's ex-wife, spent $142,311 to run 275 anti-Koster ad spots during the final week of the campaign.

McCain actually ran the most Missouri ad spots of any candidate during the last week - 1,031. But McCain spent less than Koster because more of McCain's ads aired in lower-cost markets, including Columbia and Joplin, Mo.

Over the last month, Obama spent slightly more than McCain on TV ads, but Obama's campaign spending dropped sharply during the last week. Even so, both presidential contenders spent more than $1 million apiece in Missouri during the last month.

Meanwhile, Missouri's two Republican contenders for governor - U.S. Rep. Kenny Hulshof, the victor, and state Treasurer Sarah Steelman - almost matched each other in ad purchases during the final week and month of the campaign.

All told, over the last month, Steelman spent $928,135 to run 2,320 ad spots. Hulshof spent $914,308 for 2,892 spots.

The Democratic victor for state treasurer, state Rep. Clint Zweifel, spent $375,650 on ads running in the final two weeks of the campaign. According to the ad-monitoring firm, Zweifel was the only Democratic candidate for the post to air TV ads on broadcast stations.
