August 8, 2008 Friday
THIRD EDITION


LETTERS FROM READERS


SECTION: EDITORIAL; Pg. C10


LENGTH: 1442 words


Another ATV danger: children - and parents who allow kids to use them

While the inferior quality of Chinese-made all-terrain vehicles certainly is a safety issue, a much greater safety issue is the use of ATVs by children. The article "Foreign ATVs may be new danger" (Aug. 3) points out that while Missouri requires drivers of ATVs to be licensed drivers at least 16 years of age, in Illinois children of any age may operate ATVs. Illinois and the rest of the country would do well to emulate Missouri and treat ATVs as the motor vehicles they are.

While it seems inconceivable that a parent would allow a 7-year-old to operate a car, truck or motorcycle, some parents allow them to operate ATVs as if they were toys. ATV manufacturers deliberately build smaller machines targeted directly at children. Importation and sales of these vehicles should be prohibited. Parents who buy them for their children should have their heads examined.

Missouri should strive to tighten enforcement of existing ATV laws. Where I live in rural Shannon County, I frequently see neighborhood children, some as young as 10, operating ATVs on my road. This is winked at by area law enforcement and condoned and even encouraged by parents. While I am not advocating giving 10-year-olds traffic tickets, it would be appropriate to charge their parents with felony child endangerment. After all, the 10-year-old did not buy that machine himself; somewhere an adult is enabling this dangerous and criminal behavior. While this sounds harsh, it is better than all the remorseful newspaper articles and editorials after some young life is snuffed out by the irresponsible operation of an adult motor vehicle.

Bill O'Donnell | Winona, Mo.

Challenging tests

Regarding "Prostate cancer test is under fire" (Aug. 5): The prostate-specific antigen study was financed by the insurance industry and a federal board packed with representatives from that industry. I guess 29,000 prostate cancer deaths a year are meaningless.

I am a seven-year survivor of a serious and uncured cancer. I fight each day to live. My cancer was not detectable by digital rectal exam. Most prostate cancer is detected by rectal exam, but if I had not had a biopsy, I would be dead.

No urologist will operate on one high PSA result. Several PSA tests are taken over several weeks or months. The levels of all the PSA tests done and the rise of the level over time determine if a biopsy is needed. A biopsy, while not pleasant, is over in 20 minutes, and the result of the biopsy determines the course of treatment, if needed.

Peter Shoff | University City

A city out of control

I hope that Deborah Bailey, the woman who was given the run-around when she tried to retrieve her 1999 Escort from St. Louis Metropolitan Towing, can find a lawyer who will not be afraid to take a case that involves suing the city of St. Louis, the St. Louis Police Metropolitan Department and St. Louis Metropolitan Towing and its "sales" arm, Parks Auto Sales ("Auto biography of one towed car," Aug. 3). Ms. Bailey deserves to be compensated for the entire cost of the Escort. She also should be awarded punitive damages for the bankruptcy she filed.

This city is out of control. The mayor and his cronies grab as much as they can for themselves, and the Police Department has been out of control for too many years. Shame on all of them, and may they be forced to make restitution for ruining this woman's life.

T. Richardson | St. Louis

Move over, Detroit

Well, the city of St. Louis is well on its way to setting another homicide record for the year. Move over, Detroit; St. Louis is gaining on you. If St. Louis cannot manage a contractual relationship with a towing service properly and has a homicide rate to be ashamed of, perhaps the city should close and reorganize.

Joe Colegrove | Grover

Assured spectrum

I find it ironic that beginning with the Aug. 11 edition, all lottery results will appear on the same page, A2, as columnist Bill McClellan. They do seem to belong together. Devotees will search the entire publication to locate them, and, once found, each must be carefully read and digested. There could be joy, or there may be tears, but a spectrum of emotions always is assured.

Gordon Crow | St. Charles

Ultimate leverage

Missouri's citizens will become better informed voters when there is complete transparency, especially in the area of campaign finance. Knowing where a candidate is getting his funding helps me make a decision. Do I think this candidate's vote will be influenced by his contributors? Do I have ideological values that conflict with those contributors?

There is nothing wrong with receiving campaign contributions in any amount. Organizations give to candidates who already align with their goals. It becomes a problem when those contributions are veiled and the voting public loses the ability to hold candidates accountable.

Limiting campaign contributions doesn't do a thing to limit influence. Instead of choosing the "appearance" of being unbiased, Missourians can make informed choices and use accountability to the public as the ultimate leverage.

Jim and Judy Boen | Ballwin

Exploiting racism

The issue raised by probable Republican presidential nominee Sen. John McCain's' "celebrity" attack ad about U.S. Sen. Barack Obama, D-Ill., the probable Democratic presidential nominee, is not whether Mr. McCain is a racist; it is whether Mr. McCain is willing to exploit racism to win. Plainly, he is.

The ad features the same undercurrent as the notorious "playgirl" attack ad against U.S. Senate candidate Harold Ford Jr. from Tennessee. Juxtaposing a young, beautiful white woman with a black man is the 21-century version of Lee Atwater's Willie Horton ad. Before his death, Mr. Atwater apologized for the ad's racial exploitation. Too bad Mr. McCain has not yet been so honest.

Sally E. Barker | University City

Tied: Health care and jobs

With American businesses spending more than twice as much for health insurance as most foreign competitors, is it any wonder they have a tough time competing with imports? Yes, it is only one factor in a very complicated economy, but health care spending is something that could be addressed if politicians would make it a priority.

If Americans want a market-driven health care system, then get rid of the mandates and rules that pile cost on top of cost. Cancel Medicare, Medicaid, the Veterans Administration and all government health programs. But let's be prepared to accept the premature loss of life that goes along with the denial of care.

If, on the other hand, Americans want a system that covers everyone and spreads the risk and cost across all income levels, then "Medicare for All" is the way to lower cost. Let's stop the insanity of having private insurance cover the young, healthy, wealthy and working population - the profitable segment - while saddling government programs with the elderly, ill, disabled and poor people. The existing system drives up the cost of care paid by our government. It also drives up the cost of private insurance, which means reduced business competitiveness and, thus, costs jobs.

Klaus Illian | Manchester

Nettles of allegory

Poor Maureen Dowd. Treading into the nettles of drawing an allegory to contemporary politics out of a romantic novel published in 1813 can be tricky ("Obama as Mr. Darcy; America as Miss Bennet," Aug. 5). Whether because of space or ideological constraints, she sees the characters of Jane Austen's novel "Pride and Prejudice" and America's presidential candidates from only one side.

Any high school English teacher would expect a student to recognize and articulate that each of the characters in Ms. Austen's novel displays the title traits. Fully developing Ms. Dowd's allegory to candidates John McCain and Barack Obama and the American public would cause us to consider Mr. Obama's prejudice (against Americans' freedom to live without the interference of the federal government?), or Mr. McCain's pride (rightly held but so rarely shown) or the American people's wish, similar to the Bennets' desire as parents, to raise their children well and see them become honorable people who live productive lives and contribute positively to society.

If Ms. Dowd is correct in drawing her comparison of Mr. Obama to Mr. Darcy and America to Miss Bennet, I hope that Mr. Obama can overcome his prejudice, as Mr. Darcy did when he realized that the good in Miss Bennet, like that in America, far outweighs her shortcomings and that he can accept her and love her for who she is rather than who he thought she should be.

Leave all that out and, were I the teacher, ®cwould get a C.

Tim Keeney | Webster Groves

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


August 7, 2008 Thursday
THIRD EDITION


Paris Hilton checks into presidential race: It's hot


BYLINE: sylvester brown, jr.


SECTION: METRO; Pg. D1


LENGTH: 755 words


This week, celebrity hotel heiress Paris Hilton announced she's in the race for president.

Lord, help us all.

Hilton said Republican presidential candidate John McCain pulled her into the campaign with an ad equating Barack Obama to bubble-gum stars Hilton and Britney Spears.

She released a retort Tuesday on the website "Funny or Die - funnyordie.com. Taking an apparent swipe at McCain, 71, Hilton says she's "not from the olden days."

"I'm just hot," she coos.

In tongue-in-cheek style, Hilton said she's in the race, because a "wrinkly, white-haired guy" used her in his campaign ad. "Thanks for the endorsement, white-haired dude," she beams.

On the way to work Wednesday, I listened to KWMU host Don Marsh as he discussed Hilton and politics with three local political science professors.

"I hope the campaigns haven't boiled down to Paris Hilton and Britney Spears," said Steven Puro of St. Louis University.

Well, professor, I'm afraid the pillars of this campaign already are rooted in the muck and mire of celebrity silliness.

Obama, for example, had to renounce another black person who made slanderous remarks. First, it was Nation of Islam leader Louis Farrakhan. Then, it was his former pastor, Jeremiah Wright. Most recently, it was a rapper, Ludacris.

In a statement, Obama said the Grammy-award winning artist should be "ashamed" of his new rap song that made fun of McCain's age, called President George W. Bush "mentally handicapped," and used the B-word to slam Sen. Hillary Clinton.

The song, Obama said, is "outrageously offensive" and insults those trying to raise children "with the values we hold dear."

Maybe so. But I'm still trying to figure out how rap lyrics even became news fodder in this presidential campaign.

But wait, it gets even sillier.

This week, actress Scarlett Johansson, 23, said she is embarrassed by the publicity over her so-called "e-mail relationship" with Obama.

The actress told reporters she sent Obama e-mails only to express support for his "refreshing" campaign. In response to the reports, Obama said he replied to one of Johansson's notes forwarded to him by an assistant.

The communiques were manipulated into "an unfortunate media frenzy," Johansson said.

She's probably right, but in a society fixated on sleazy gossip, an innocent e-mail exchange can easily morph into a hot, clandestine politician-celebrity affair.

What's behind all of this?

These types of low-ball, sordid media stories are right out of the "Karl Rove school of politics," Puro, the political science professor, said on the KWMU program.

"The idea is to divert attention from major ideas, to get the public talking about other issues with coded words that, apparently, the Republican Party understands."

Although I think the attention on Wright, Hilton or Ludacris goes deeper than the GOP and Rove, Puro's observation adds interesting perspective to current news stories.

During a recent swing through Missouri, Obama told audiences that McCain will try to make voters "scared" of him because he doesn't look like past presidents and has a "funny name."

"He's playing the race card," McCain supporters charged.

Rick Davis, McCain's campaign manager, went on television, proclaiming that his team will not allow McCain "to be painted as racist."

Clearly, race is a still a hot-button issue in this campaign. A rapper who insults GOP politicos and allegations that Obama has stooped to race-baiting raises the ire of those voters who are already skittish about the notion of a milk chocolate president.

Which brings me back to McCain's Obama-as-celebrity campaign ad. It seemed sort of odd and counterproductive to show the candidate standing in front of 200,000 excited Germans, most shouting "Obama! Obama!"

Although this statesmanlike image might be impressive on the world stage, among many in our tabloid news nation, it easily was depicted as nothing more than a Hannah Montana concert.

After all, we are a nation addicted to entertainment. Why do you think "American Idol" and "Dancing with the Stars" got the top TV ratings last year.

All the media-driven gossip makes complex issues of off-shore drilling, the crisis in Darfur and the slumping U.S. economy seem downright boring.

It's a competitive world. In order to hold the fleeting attention of voters, politicians need Ludacris, Britney and tidbits about race and celebrity dalliances.

Lord help us, but Paris Hilton could run for president. She already introduced a slogan that would resonate soundly with entertainment-addicted Americans:

"Vote for me. I'm just hot."

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


August 7, 2008 Thursday
THIRD EDITION


Flip, flop and pander


SECTION: EDITORIAL; Pg. D10


LENGTH: 675 words


"The sad duty of politics is to establish justice in a sinful world."

So wrote the theologian Reinhold Niebuhr, a Wright City native who wrote a lot about the relationship between Christianity and democracy. As Mr. Niebuhr well knew, to reform a sinful world a politician first must get elected, which sometimes involves compromising political principles.

So it is in the early stages of this year's presidential campaign. Both of the presumptive candidates, Republican Sen. John McCain and Democratic Sen. Barack Obama, are honorable men who want what's best for their country. But they are blurring their principles in the quest for votes.

This sort of thing tends to win elections, but it's not pretty to watch.

During the Republican primary campaign, Mr. McCain had to appease a party base that he often had irritated. So he promised to extend the Bush administration's tax cuts, which he had voted against in 2001 and 2003. He also abandoned the enlightened immigration reform plan that he'd championed in the Senate.

More recently, Mr. McCain launched a pair of low-road TV commercials that attempted to liken Mr. Obama's popularity to fawning over such celebrities as Paris Hilton and Britney Spears. So much for his famous "straight talk."

Mr. McCain made his reputation as a maverick willing to battle his party's leadership for what he believed in. That's what made him so attractive to middle-of-the-roaders. But he since has morphed into a Republican conformist.

Mr. Obama, too, has taken to flipping and flopping. His biggest about-face was his decision to shun public financing after promising to accept it. That shocked some of his supporters, who are appalled by the ability of big donors to warp government policy.

But Mr. Obama emerged from the Chicago school of hard-nosed politics, where he became a master at courting both the Democratic machine and reformers alike. The first lesson on the curriculum: The candidate with the most money usually wins.

Mr. Obama also riled civil libertarians by voting for a compromise bill, favored by the Bush administration, making changes to the Foreign Intelligence Surveillance Act. Mr. Obama initially had threatened to filibuster the bill over its grant of immunity to telecom companies that may have cooperated illegally with U.S. intelligence agencies. By changing his position, Mr. Obama gained some protection from any soft-on-terror charges that Republicans surely would have launched against him.

Sometimes a "flip-flop" is actually an adjustment to changed circumstances. So it was with Mr. McCain's switch to support of offshore oil drilling in the face of $4-per-gallon gasoline. The $1.1 million he received from oil industry executives and employees in the month after he switched may have been merely a fortuitous coincidence. Mr. Obama, too, now says he'd vote for limited offshore drilling if it would help pass a compromise energy bill with funds supporting the development of alternative fuels. Thus far, this position that has not produced a sudden influx of oil-borne campaign contributions.

The gasoline issue has thrown both sides into full pander mode. Mr. McCain's plan for a summer gas tax holiday would have encouraged more consumption, generated less money needed for highway construction and done nothing to solve our oil dependency problem. Mr. Obama has proposed giving $1,000 to every American family, paid for by taxing the windfall profits of oil companies. That might make voters happy, but it would discourage investment in oil production, which the nation sorely needs.

The trick for voters is to distinguish between flip-flops and principled changes of position, and what any changes say about how either man would behave in the White House. The best bet: They will revert to their old selves, Mr. McCain as a moderate conservative with a bent for practical problem-solving, and Mr. Obama as a pragmatic progressive with an eye for reform.

Both men are better than their recent campaign maneuvers would indicate. Will the real Mr. Obama and Mr. McCain please stand up?

August 7, 2008 Thursday
THIRD EDITION


Obama, McCain tout energy solutions


BYLINE: FROM NEWS SERVICES


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A3


LENGTH: 465 words


ELKHART, IND.

Obama chides McCain over tire inflation

Barack Obama taunted Republican presidential rival John McCain on Wednesday for first ridiculing him for advising voters to keep tires inflated and then later acknowledging that the practice saves gasoline.

"It will be interesting to watch this debate between John McCain and John McCain," Obama said as he campaigned in Indiana with Sen. Evan Bayh, widely considered a top-tier candidate for his Democratic running mate.

Obama had noted that keeping tires inflated and cars tuned was endorsed by both NASCAR and AAA and should be part of any comprehensive plan to reduce reliance on imported oil.

In mocking Obama on Monday, McCain told a motorcycle rally in Sturgis, S.D.: "My opponent doesn't want to drill, he doesn't want nuclear power, he wants you to inflate your tires."

On Tuesday night, McCain said he wasn't opposed to Obama's suggestion, "but I also don't think that that's a way to become energy-independent."

On Wednesday, McCain criticized Obama for not fully embracing nuclear power as part of a comprehensive energy solution and called on Congress to return from its summer recess to address immediately the U.S. energy crisis.

RALEIGH, N.C.

Democrats want Edwards to answer Enquirer

Sen. John Edwards has a deadline to save his spot on the national stage.

With two weeks to go before their national convention, a number of Democrats are saying that Edwards needs to publicly address National Enquirer stories that have alleged he had an affair with a campaign worker and fathered her baby.

Edwards' political currency declines with each day the story goes unresolved, former Democratic National Committee Chairman Don Fowler and other Democratic strategists said.

If Edwards fails to clear up the story in short order, he risks party officials' deciding not to have him speak or, if they do, creating a distraction from a week focused on Sen. Barack Obama's acceptance of the nomination.

Democrats gather in Denver on Aug. 25.

Briefly

- Olympics ads: McCain plans to run $6 million worth of TV commercials on NBC broadcast and cable channels during the Olympics in Beijing. Obama's campaign already signaled its intention to spend some $5 million on ads during the Olympics.

- Tired of Obama? Nearly half the people who responded to a poll released Wednesday by the Pew Research Center said they're tired of hearing about Obama. Just 26 percent said the same about McCain.

- Nader's goal: Independent presidential candidate Ralph Nader said Wednesday he plans to get on the ballots in 45 states by the November election, but complained that he and other third-party candidates still struggle for attention in the nation's two-party political system.

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


August 6, 2008 Wednesday
THIRD EDITION


Hot air and energy Is the gas crisis really a "guts" crisis?


BYLINE: ERIC MINK


SECTION: EDITORIAL; Pg. D11


LENGTH: 1040 words


There's no cheap energy anymore, but there's an absolute glut of energy policy proposals from the two guys running for president, who seem to be stalking each other from one end of the country to the other.

On Monday, Democrat Barack Obama was in Lansing, Mich., talking up and tweaking the comprehensive energy plan he announced in Las Vegas on June 24.

Republican John McCain showed up in Michigan on Tuesday, touring the Fermi 2 nuclear power plant in Newport and talking up the comprehensive energy plan he announced in Las Vegas on June 25.

What actually is surprising about the candidates' energy plans is how similar they are. Both acknowledge that America's dependence on oil is unsustainable in the long run, damaging to the environment and dangerous to our national security.

Both say we need to diversify the sources and kinds of energy we use, raise the mileage and lower the emissions of vehicles, update the nation's electric grid, improve the energy efficiency of new and existing commercial and residential buildings and make the federal government itself an example of state of the art energy practices.

There are, of course, differences: McCain embraces nuclear power; Obama - reluctantly - won't rule it out. McCain recently (last month) endorsed lifting the ban on offshore drilling; Obama even more recently (Friday) said he'd consider it, but only as part of a broad congressional compromise that would include incentives to encourage and develop non-petroleum alternatives.

But the more substantive difference between them is philosophical:

McCain has confidence in "the power and innovation of free markets" to solve these problems, as he put it in an energy-related speech last year.

Obama, looking at the historical record, believes we can't depend on the profit motive alone, so the government's job is to define the problems, set the priorities and use its collective power to ensure success.

Lisa Margonelli isn't impressed. "The current dialogue is profoundly depressing and fake," she told me. "I think both candidates need to be more honest about our options."

Margonelli is a California-based fellow at the high-powered New America Foundation policy think tank in Washington and the author of the fascinating and immensely entertaining best-seller "Oil on the Brain."

Published last year and issued recently in paperback with an essay updating some of her observations, "Oil on the Brain" is the result of three years of field reporting and research that took Margonelli across America and to China, Iran, Nigeria, Chad and Venezuela. She even wormed her way into the U.S. Strategic Petroleum Reserve.

Dotted with respectful and sometimes affectionate profiles of improbably colorful characters, the book documents how oil is discovered and recovered; how it's refined, wholesaled and retailed; how it's priced and sold worldwide as a commodity and as a financial instrument; how it's wound through the social fabric of America and the lives of individuals; and how it shapes politics, diplomacy, democracy, autocracy, psychology, sociology and economics.

When I reached Margonelli via cell phone, she was riding shotgun in a van chugging through California's Sierra Nevada mountains heading for Colorado to help a friend move. The vehicle, she admitted, was exceeding the speed limit from time to time.

The energy debate has been disappointing, she said, noting the "theatrical approach to high gas prices" that involves talking about what amount to more subsidies and taxes to pay for more driving. We need to be talking about driving less, she said.

Subsidies speak to one of the recurring concepts of "Oil on the Brain": the hidden penny. That 9/10 of a cent tacked on to the price of a gallon of gas stands for countless costs that aren't paid directly, she writes, "from air pollution and greenhouse gases, to traffic congestion and tax breaks for oil companies, to taxpayer-subsidized oil investments in developing countries, to the costs of the U.S. military presence in the Persian Gulf."

Margonelli criticized the idea Obama floated Monday of using some of the oil from the Strategic Petroleum Reserve stocks near the Gulf Coasts of Texas and Louisiana. "If you want to use it to moderate prices," she said, "you need to set a target and say 'If oil hits, say, $150 a barrel, we're going to release, say, 750,000 barrels a day.' Ideally, you'd never have to use it, but it says we're going to do something. It's more potent as a future step," she said, and it could tamp down prices in the short term.

McCain's enthusiasm for offshore drilling, she says, is likewise out of whack. It amounts to to a giveaway to the oil companies, Margonelli said, because operating in U.S. waters is safe, legal contracts are dependable and the royalties exacted by the U.S. government are much lower than those of other countries. On top of that, as she notes in her book, advances in technology have long since taken most of the uncertainty out of drilling, although "the myth of the lucky wildcatter lives on."

"We want the oil companies to make money," she told me, "but we want them to work for it." Offshore drilling, she said, is essentially "free money."

Mostly, though, she'd like to see some courage from the candidates. "I think they should say, 'Look, there are no quick fixes here for the mess we're in. I'm sorry I'm the person who has to say this, but we have to use our greatness to rethink the way we live.'"

And she expects this in an election year? "I think someone needs to say it," she insisted. "Both of these people want to be centrists. They could redefine the center."

I reminded her of what happened to Jimmy Carter when he told Americans hard truths about an earlier oil crisis. Margonelli replied, "In fact, the country benefited from his energy policies and Jerry Ford's energy policies for years. We need to embrace our faith in our ability to transform the American economy and transform our lifestyles.

"The government needs to have more guts," she said. "It needs to start taking as activist a role in domestic energy policy as it does in pursing energy policies overseas. There have been a lot of political interests working to preserve the status quo, and that's not working for us as citizens anymore."

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


August 6, 2008 Wednesday
THIRD EDITION


Kenny v. Jay


SECTION: EDITORIAL; Pg. D10


LENGTH: 871 words


The short analysis of the results of Tuesday's Missouri primary would be this: Money talked.

In nearly every major race, the candidate with the deeper pockets won. Those candidates were able to afford a barrage of last-minute television ads and automated phone calls, some of them misleading and many of them negative.

If this kind of campaigning wins primaries, when candidates ostensibly are on the same side, just imagine what the fall election will be like - particularly since the limits on campaign contributions to candidates for state office will be removed as of Aug. 28.

At the top of the ticket, just below the presumptive presidential match-up between Democrat Barack Obama and Republican John McCain, will be Kenny v. Jay. It sounds like a Khoury League pitching match-up, not a governor's race. In more formal terms, the governor's race will pit Republican U.S. Rep. Kenneth C. Hulshof, 50, of Columbia by way of Bertrand, against Democratic Attorney General Jeremiah W. Nixon, 52, of Jefferson City by way of Hillsboro.

The two veteran party loyalists emerged from their respective primaries, Mr. Nixon unbruised and all but unopposed, Mr. Hulshof dinged up by a rugged primary battle with state Treasurer Sarah Steelman. Mr. Hulshof, who had the backing of most party leaders and major contributors, outspent her on television ads in the last month of the campaign by nearly two to one.

Ms. Steelman ran a classic outsider's campaign, portraying herself as the "people's candidate" despite a long record of support for business interests. She attacked Mr. Hulshof, a veteran of 12 years in the U.S. House, for a record of support of pork-barrel spending projects and ethanol producers. The message resonated in many parts of southwest Missouri, but the turnout there wasn't large enough to offset Mr. Hulshof's big margins in St. Charles and St. Louis counties.

Mr. Hulshof, despite his years in Congress, had to introduce himself to most voters outside the 9th Congressional District of north central Missouri. He did so largely on television, emphasizing his farming background and solid conservative credentials. His task was made more difficult because, until January, incumbent Republican Gov. Matt Blunt had been expected to run for a second term. When Mr. Blunt dropped out, state Republicans - with the notable exception of Ms. Steelman - got in line behind Mr. Hulshof.

Although Mr. Nixon polled better than either Mr. Hulshof or Ms. Steelman, Mr. Hulshof is capable of making that ground up in a hurry, now that there are no limits on how much contributors can give directly to state office seekers. Mr. Nixon is no slouch as a fundraiser himself, suggesting that the fall campaign will easily shatter the record of $11.9 million spent in the 2004 governor's race between Mr. Blunt and Democrat Claire McCaskill, now a U.S. senator.

In other statewide races, Republican Lieutenant Gov. Peter Kinder easily won renomination for a second term. He will face Democrat Sam Page of Creve Coeur, a physician and state representative, who outpaced five minor candidates.

In the race to succeed Mr. Nixon as attorney general, Republican state Sen. Mike Gibbons of Kirkwood, who was unopposed, will face either state Sen. Chris Koster of Belton or state Rep. Margaret Donnelly of Richmond Heights. At press time their race was too close to call, with Mr. Koster showing strength in the western side of the state, Ms. Donnelly closing fast in the St. Louis area.

Should Mr. Koster emerge, it will be remarkable, considering that he became a Democrat only a year ago. As a state senator, he was a loyal Republican voter, a fact he managed to obscure with heavy spending on television ads focusing on his record as a prosecuting attorney. Mr. Koster also was battered by Ms. Donnelly and a third candidate, state Rep. Jeff Harris of Columbia, for playing fast and loose with campaign funding restrictions.

In the third contested statewide race, that for state treasurer, Republican state Sen. Brad Lager of Maryville was unopposed. On the Democratic side, it appeared for a while Tuesday night that Arnold Mayor Mark Powell would be the exception to the "money talks" rule. The party's unsuccessful 2004 candidate for treasurer, Mr. Powell ran a shoestring campaign this year, spending only about $25,000, some $450,000 less than state Rep. Clint Zweifel of Florissant. In the end, Mr. Zweifel, who had heavy support from organized labor, pulled away.

Money played a major role in the race for Mr. Hulshof's 9th district congressional seat. In the GOP race, the two candidates who could afford television ads, former state Rep. Blaine Luetekemeyer of St. Elizabeth and state Rep. Bob Onder easily outpolled state Rep. Danie Moore and former Mizzou football star Brock Olivo. Mr. Leutkemeyer won the race.

On the Democratic side, both state Rep. Judy Baker of Columbia and former House Speaker Steve Gaw of Holts Summit bought heavy television schedules, but Ms. Baker's heavy support in her home city of Columbia proved decisive. The other two Democrats in the race, Marion County Presiding Commissioner Lyndon Bode of Palmyra and former state Sen. Ken Jacob of Columbia, couldn't afford TV commercials and fell out of the running.

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


August 3, 2008 Sunday
FOURTH EDITION


LETTERS FROM READERS


SECTION: EDITORIAL; Pg. B2


LENGTH: 1448 words


Missouri wants to help makes decisions about state air quality

Regarding "Missouri, Illinois battle EPA, separately" (July 27): Everyone in Missouri needs and deserves clean air. The Missouri Department of Natural Resources wants the ozone standard decreased. Our lawsuit seeks to protect Missouri air quality, not prevent that protection. But we believe the Environmental Protection Agency needs to look at this at the national level, rather than state by state or urban area by urban area in piecemeal fashion. This lawsuit forces that issue. Being at the table ensures Missouri has the chance to provide input.

The new federal standard will have a huge impact on Missouri. Currently, only one ozone monitoring station in the state would be in compliance with this new proposed standard, and that one would just barely meet it. We have no guarantee that new federal requirements will protect Missouri citizens who may be impacted by these regulations.

It is much easier to prevent a problem with federal regulations than to clean it up later. This is similar to the Mississippi River hypoxia issue: The state of Missouri expressed concerns about the impact on our citizens, with few benefits, of a pending federal agreement, and other states then joined us. Only then did the U.S. Environmental Protection Agency change the agreement to be more protective of the environment.

No one questions that ozone is a problem that must be addressed to protect the health of Missouri citizens. However, a one-size-fits-all solution won't work for the East Coast, West Coast and Midwest. We could spend a lot of resources and have very little impact on the health of our citizens. Entering the lawsuit is our way of being at the table if changes are needed and making sure any resources spent will have a positive impact on the health of our citizens and our air quality.

Doyle Childers | Jefferson City Director, Missouri Department of Natural Resources

Health should be No. 1

It is hoped that Doyle Childers, director of the Missouri Department of Natural Resources, is interested in protecting citizens' health more than the questionable economic benefit of keeping the air as dirty as it is now, but his quote in "Missouri, Illinois battle EPA, separately" (July 27) demonstrates that such is not the case. In fact, Mr. Childers wishes to sue the Environmental Protection Agency for its audacity in lowering the ozone standard to protect public health.

Missouri has a Department of Economic Development that protects and advocates for businesses. The Department of Natural Resources is to protect and advocate for our natural environment, including clean air. In taking a position against the modest steps that EPA is taking, Mr. Childers has come down on the side of industrial interests. With friends such as him, those interested in clean air don't need enemies.

Ken Midkiff | Columbia, Mo. Conservation Chair, Osage Group Sierra Club

Double down on MetroLink

"Next MetroLink line?" (July 29) accurately pointed out that if voters had passed a small sales tax for Metro in the mid-1990s, lines to St. Charles County and Madison County might have been in place today for riders to use and avoid the high price of gasoline. The article could have said the same thing about St. Louis County. Had St. Louis County voters passed a quarter-cent sales tax in 1997, which passed then in the city of St. Louis, another MetroLink line might have been near completion at a time when people are clamoring for more MetroLink.

Voters in St. Louis County have a chance to rectify the mistake of a decade ago and support a measure in November to fund the expansion of MetroLink. The 43 miles of MetroLink that the region has put in place over the last 20 years has proven its ability to offer people a great alternative to $4-a-gallon gasoline. We need to be thinking about doubling the system over the next 10 to 15 years.

What's more, recent reports about Sunnen's plan to build a $500 million mixed-use development anchored by the Manchester and Sunnen MetroLink stations in Maplewood is an excellent example our investment in the Cross County line getting an immediate return to the region. We just need more of it.

Sandy MacLean | St. Louis Board of Directors, Citizens for Modern Transit

Missed the train

How and why did we miss the train on rebuilding U.S. Highway 40/Interstate 64 without including light rail? I live in South St. Louis and work in West County and would love the opportunity to take mass transit to work. With gas at $4 a gallon, I go through what I would spend on a monthly Metro pass ($60) in about seven working days - not counting insurance cost, wear and tear, etc.

The added benefits: I would be a better steward of our Earth by creating less pollution and using resources better. This should not happen again.

Ken Williams | St. Louis

Routing decisions

A map with the story "Next MetroLink line?" (July 29) showed the proposed MetroLink line to St. Charles as going over the old Highway 115 bridge. It left the reader thinking that the line just somehow left Lambert International Airport to get to that point. In reality, the St. Charles line would have left the current line near the intersection of Interstates 170/70 and proceeded south alongside I-170 to near Page Boulevard and continued west alongside the old Rock Island Railroad.

The current thinking, with the help of St. Louis County Executive Charlie Dooley, is to use the same routing (albeit running north, out of Clayton) in going to Westport, if the November sales tax initiative passes. This line probably would leave the railroad right of way and go west on the north side of Page. With the St. Charles proposal of 1996, after getting to Westport, the line was to head northwest toward the old 115 bridge through St. Charles and on to the Mid-Rivers area of St. Peters.

David J. Neubauer | Hazelwood Editor, Whistle Talk, St. Louis Railway Enthusiasts

Remarkable tribute

Regarding "Conservation Corps alumni museum is closing here" (July 28): The Civilian Conservation Corps Alumni Headquarters and Museum in Jefferson Barracks Park now has closed forever. I live close to it and had visited it several times. It is a remarkable tribute to one of the greatest ideas and programs that government ever created. In the Great Depression, the CCC was formed to put youth to work while training them in a trade and building infrastructure. This allowed them to support their families through a period of massive unemployment and poverty.

All across this nation, you still can see evidence of the CCC. Most of the state and national parks contain structures and roads built by and forests planted by the young men of the CCC. Seventy years later, much of their work still is used.

Lest we ever forget, the CCC was born in a nation experiencing economic hard times and world turmoil. It exemplified the spirit that we all are in this together. Its good works have far outlasted the program's existence. We need that kind of leadership today, not leadership based on greed. We need leadership that recognizes that we can pull together and help each other and that government has a role in doing that.

I am saddened that this memorial is gone. It commemorated government that brought out the best in our people. Would that there were more like it, that people would be reminded of the virtues of self-sacrifice, teamwork and everyone doing his part in a great endeavor.

Rich Brown | Lemay

Why do they hate us?

The U.S. Geologic Survey says there are 90 billion barrels of oil in the Arctic, enough to take care of U.S. needs for 12 years. The Democratic Party refuses to allow us to develop it.

Why do the Democrats hate us so much?

Jack Collins | Kirkwood

Look at the record

In his latest ad, U.S. Sen. John McCain, R-Ariz., the presumptive Republican nominee for president, accuses his Democratic rival, U.S. Sen. Barack Obama, D-Ill., of not supporting U.S. troops fighting in the Middle East. A closer examination of the two senators' records reveals that it is Mr. McCain who consistently has opposed legislation in support of our men and women in uniform.

While the TV ad says that Mr. McCain is "always there for our troops," he opposed the new GI Bill recently passed by Congress. The bill expanded benefits for military veterans. In 2005, Mr. McCain voted against a bill to provide $360 million for armored tactical wheeled vehicles for units deployed in Iraq and Afghanistan. Mr. McCain also voted against increased funding for veterans' health care on multiple occasions in 2006, but he did vote for tripling veterans' health care fees. Mr. McCain's rhetoric may favor our military men and women, but his record does not.

William Hart | Overland

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


August 3, 2008 Sunday
FOURTH EDITION


McCain positions for attacks on NY Times


BYLINE: By Devlin Barrett THE ASSOCIATED PRESS


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A5


LENGTH: 396 words


DATELINE: WASHINGTON


It is a tradition at many kitchen tables to yell at the newspaper. At John McCain's kitchen table, it is becoming a tradition to yell at one paper in particular: The New York Times.

The latest dustup between the Republican presidential candidate and the "All the News that's fit to Print" newspaper centered on the editorial board's criticisms of McCain, one dispatch accusing him of taking the low road and another contending that he was playing politics with race.

The response from the McCain campaign was equally cutting. "If the shareholders of The New York Times ever wonder why the paper's ad revenue is plummeting and its share price tanking, they need look no further than the hysterical reaction of the paper's editors to any slight, real or imagined, against their preferred candidate," said McCain campaign spokesman Michael Goldfarb.

The relationship between McCain - a frequent reader of the newspaper - and the Times has been rocky. Yet such a grudge could pay political dividends for McCain, as criticizing the liberal media often improves a candidate's standing with Republican conservatives.

Back in January, the Times endorsed McCain's candidacy for the Republican nomination, saying, "Sen. John McCain of Arizona is the only Republican who promises to end the George Bush style of governing from and on behalf of a small, angry fringe."

Since then, it's been McCain v. The New York Times.

In February, the newspaper printed a story about McCain and a female lobbyist, reporting that unnamed McCain associates years ago had become concerned the relationship may have become sexual. Both McCain and the lobbyist have unequivocally denied that it was.

A month later, McCain flashed his temper at a Times reporter, repeatedly cutting her off when asked whether he had spoken to Democratic Sen. John Kerry about being Kerry's vice president in 2004.

Then last month, Republicans complained that the paper rejected an Op-Ed piece by McCain about the Iraq war after one by Obama was printed. The paper said it had only tried to get McCain to be more specific about his plan.

Mike Paul, a former aide to New York Republicans, said several recent moves by McCain showed he was turning toward more conservative positioning "and some of that includes not being afraid to go negative, not being afraid to call a liberal a liberal, and not being afraid to go after a newspaper."

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


July 31, 2008 Thursday
THIRD EDITION


Obama on Republican turf Campaign stops show he hasn't written off rural areas


BYLINE: By Jo Mannies Post-Dispatch Political Correspondent Adam Jadhav of the Post-Dispatch contributed to this report.


SECTION: METRO; Pg. C1


LENGTH: 1146 words


DATELINE: UNION, MO.


Presumptive Democratic presidential nominee Barack Obama promised enthusiastic crowds Wednesday in stops across southern Missouri that he would attack economic and energy problems that he said are responsible for Americans "losing faith in the essence of the American dream."

"That is what's at stake in this election," Obama said in Union, his last stop and one that took place in a park pavilion amid a pouring rain.

Obama told several hundred supporters, some clustered under umbrellas, that many Americans fear that "we're going to have an America that we pass on to our children that's a lot poorer" than the country and the economy that they inherited from their parents and grandparents.

That fear will come true, he said, unless the nation and its leaders change direction - the cornerstone of his campaign for the White House.

Wednesday night, Obama's Republican rival - John McCain - was in Kansas City for a private fundraiser. McCain made no public remarks.

But several Missouri Republican allies, including U.S. Sen. Christopher "Kit" Bond and Rep. Jo Ann Emerson, spent Wednesday publicly jabbing at Obama's energy proposals and his opposition to the expansion of offshore drilling.

In a conference call with reporters, Emerson accused Obama of a "lack of vision ... in not trying to use every single thing we could possibly use to increase our independence from those who hate us and to reduce our reliance on imported gasoline and diesel fuel."

In reply, Obama asserted that Republican critics were failing to tell the public that oil companies already had leases on 68 million offshore and on-land acres, but weren't drilling on them.

"First, let's get the oil companies to use what they've got," Obama said in a brief interview in Union.

He said that the United States can get control of the energy problem only if it expands the use of alternative sources of energy and increases conservation of fuel.

"We've got to be a lot more honest with Americans," Obama said.

Obama suggested that the heightened Republican attacks, including a new TV ad that portrays him as an empty-headed celebrity, stem from the GOP's fear that its own lack of policy proposals will be rejected by voters.

"John McCain spent the last month talking about me," Obama said.

If McCain had serious proposals for addressing the nation's problems, he'd be talking about those instead, Obama added.

Obama said that the best part of Wednesday's bus tour, where he stopped at four communities along Interstate 44, was to see support in what he acknowledged was traditional Republican turf.

Obama also heard first-hand about the economic woes plaguing rural Missouri communities.

In a question-and-answer session at the Missouri University of Science and Technology in Rolla, Wade McBride recounted how he'd recently lost his job at Briggs and Stratton's local engine-manufacturing plant, which was replaced by an identical operation in China.

McBride, 31, said he'd trained some of the Chinese workers who took over the job he had held for nine years.

Obama said he may not be able to prevent all plants from closing and the jobs being shipped overseas, but, if elected president, he said he could stop the tax breaks that often reward such behavior.

He expanded on that pledge in Union. "We're not going to stand idly by and let jobs get shipped overseas," Obama said, igniting cheers.

However, Obama's day-long trek was as much about promoting his personal attributes - and dispelling Internet smears - as his policies.

"They say he's arrogant, not patriotic, blah, blah, blah," said Sen. Claire McCaskill, D-Mo., as she introduced Obama in Union. The truth, she continued, is "he's humble, he's patriotic, and he's a devout Christian."

But not everyone who showed up Wednesday to see Obama agreed with her. "For me, he's just too liberal," said Tyrone Kozlowski, among roughly 20 protesters who mingled with Obama supporters across the street from the park in Union.

Most of the protesters had disappeared when Obama walked over to the crowd, despite the rain, to shake hands.

Based on past voting, the Missouri counties Obama visited Wednesday are a lock for Republicans. Four years ago, almost two-thirds of Phelps County's ballots went for President George W. Bush.

In Franklin County, the Democratic presidential ticket snagged only 40 percent of the vote.

But two years ago, McCaskill performed a few percentage points better in each county - additional votes that she says made the difference in her successful Senate bid against the Republican incumbent, Jim Talent.

Obama's targeted audience Wednesday included rural Democrats such as retired teacher Jude Wilson, who had preferred Hillary Rodham Clinton in Missouri's Feb. 5 presidential primary.

Although Obama narrowly carried the state, Clinton carried all of the rural counties.

Wilson was among several Clinton supporters from rural Mountain View who traveled to Rolla to hear Obama. All said Wednesday that there's no question that they'll vote for Obama in November.

Said Debi Larson, a retired psychologist: "The Republicans have made such a god-awful mess of things."

But random interviews in downtown Rolla found a wide range of opinions of Obama - and some erroneous beliefs.

Chuck Mayes, 28, a construction worker from nearby Belle, Mo., said he was ardently against the election of Obama.

"Everyone I've talked to doesn't want him in there," Mayes said, referring to the presidency.

"The first reason is his name," Mayes continued. "Second, he wasn't born here," referring to the United States.

When told that Obama was born in Hawaii and was indeed an American citizen, as required by the U.S. Constitution, Mayes expressed surprise: "I didn't know that."

Across the street, retired Highway Patrolman Tony Viessman said he wasn't surprised to hear that false information about Obama continues to circulate.

"If he was a white man, he'd be elected tomorrow," said Viessman, 74, who was holding up one end of a large banner that declared, "Rednecks for Obama."

Viessman had paid for the banner himself. The opposite end was held up by a friend, Leslie Spencer, a retired insurance agent.

Both men said they strongly support Obama's views on the economy and many other issues. "He's brilliant," Viessman said.

Viessman and Spencer added that they are confident Obama could get more votes in southern Missouri than recent Democratic contenders, though they had no illusions that he would carry their hometowns.

The fact that Obama dared to campaign in Rolla at all, said Spencer, showed that he wasn't writing off rural parts of the state.

In the interview, Obama said that if he got that point across, Wednesday's bus trip achieved its goal.

July 30, 2008 Wednesday
Correction Appended
THIRD EDITION


Obama, McCain both reach out to veterans The number of soldiers is growing They form a big part of the electorate.


BYLINE: By Philip Dine POST-DISPATCH WASHINGTON BUREAU


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A1


LENGTH: 813 words


DATELINE: WASHINGTON


As John McCain and Barack Obama tangle over Iraq and the surge, they're also waging a less-noticed military-related battle:

Each is vying to be the voice of America's veterans.

The importance of this effort is reflected in the vigor with which Obama's campaign stresses the work he's done on veterans issues during his relatively brief time in Washington, and his bid to contrast that with McCain's positions.

It is reflected in the time McCain spent last week organizing a veterans organization in Missouri and reminding members of his own service and sacrifices in Vietnam.

And it is reflected in the haste with which the McCain campaign jumped on Obama's decision not to visit wounded troops in Germany last week, a flap featured in a new McCain ad. On Tuesday, the McCain campaign used that incident to question Obama's capacity to serve as commander in chief. In response, McCain's contention was labeled as "disgraceful" by an Obama adviser.

"This is truly a remarkable election in terms of the attention that's being paid to veterans," Terry Madonna, who directs the Center for Politics and Public Affairs at Franklin and Marshall College, said Tuesday. Unlike the Vietnam era, even antiwar forces are showing respect for veterans, he added.

Much of the political maneuvering is taking place in conference calls, press releases or quiet visits with veterans, but it matters.

- There are 23.4 million veterans, including 522,798 in Missouri and 822,104 in Illinois, and with the 2.2 million active-duty troops and reservists, almost 26 million Americans have worn or are wearing the uniform. They form strong voting blocs in key states such as Florida. Other swing states, including Ohio and Missouri, have some combination of large veteran populations, sizeable military bases, major defense contractors or key legislators on military committees.

- Establishing trust and confidence with veterans groups helps a candidate build credibility with the public as a potential commander in chief. This is particularly important for Obama, given his lack of military experience.

- The veterans issue is important to both candidates. Veterans are a natural constituency for McCain, but the administration's problems in meeting their needs, from substandard conditions at Walter Reed Hospital to gaps in providing mental health care, provide Obama an opening. Meanwhile, Obama's work on the Senate Veterans Committee has led to some demonstrable achievements, though potential missteps such as refusing to recognize the surge's success allow his foes to raise questions.

As Obama traveled overseas last week, McCain helped set up veterans organizations, including Missouri Veterans for McCain, in about 20 states.

"He's been shot at, he's been a POW, and he knows what is required when he sends young men and women off to defend our nation," said Jack Jackson, a retired colonel and former Missouri state representative. Missouri state Rep. Jason Brown, a co-chairman of the veterans group who returned last year from 14 months in Iraq as an Army reservist, accused Obama of using veterans issues to "shore up his credibility in an area, foreign policy, he's weak in."

Obama's campaign counters by citing key areas where he has helped spur changes, including aiding homeless veterans, cutting red tape after the Walter Reed scandal, improving disability pay for veterans in Illinois and sparking a Pentagon review of its practice of discharging veterans for alleged "pre-existing personality disorders." The Post-Dispatch reported last year that more than 22,000 troops have been told by the military that they don't have post-traumatic stress disorder but rather brought personal problems into the service.

McCain acknowledges problems in veterans care. He told the American GI Forum in Denver a few days ago, "When our government forgets our debts to you, it is a stain upon America's honor. ... We should give veterans the option to use a simple plastic card to receive timely and accessible care. ... I will not stand for requiring veterans to make an appointment to stand in line for substandard care of the injuries you have suffered to keep our country safe."

Rep. Ike Skelton, D-Mo., chairman of the House Armed Services Committee, predicts Obama will get a fair hearing from veterans.

"Veterans understand the issues before our country," Skelton said. "I think veterans want someone who will listen to them and understand their challenges. I've found that to be true. (House Democratic Leader) Nancy Pelosi got a tremendous award from the American Legion last year, and she's not a veteran - but she understands their issues."

The political importance of veterans, especially after years of war in Afghanistan and Iraq, is magnified by their mounting problems with health and relationships, Madonna says.

"They not only have votes," he said, "they live in families and families have votes."

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


July 26, 2008 Saturday
THIRD EDITION


Political ads saturate Missouri TV screens


BYLINE: By Jo Mannies Post-Dispatch Political Correspondent


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A16


LENGTH: 600 words


During the last two weeks, Missouri TV viewers have faced a barrage of campaign ads - at least 7,200 spots - from candidates who have spent more than $2.7 million to promote themselves and their views.

The avalanche of ads largely reflects the ramping up of the contests in the Aug. 5 primary, notably the Republican battle for governor between U.S. Rep. Kenny Hulshof and state Treasurer Sarah Steelman.

But when it comes to TV ads, Hulshof and Steelman are being outspent by others - some of whom won't be on that ballot.

During the last two weeks, presumptive Democratic presidential nominee Barack Obama has been the biggest ad-spending candidate in the state, even though he and Republican rival John McCain won't face Missouri voters until Nov. 4.

Since July 10, Obama has spent at least $516,410 to run TV ads in the state, compared with $408,567 for McCain. Obama outspent McCain in every major Missouri media market except Joplin, Mo., according to figures compiled by TNSMI/Campaign Media Analysis Group. The nonpartisan ad-monitoring firm is tracking campaign spending for the Post-Dispatch.

McCain actually outspent Obama statewide during the last week. Presumably, the Democratic contender sharply curbed his Missouri ad buys because of the free TV coverage of Obama's overseas trip.

Evan Tracey, the ad-monitoring firm's chief operating officer, said the TV spending makes clear that McCain and Obama are targeting Missouri voters.

McCain is running ads in fewer swing states than Obama, Tracey said.

Dave Robertson, a political science professor at the University of Missouri-St. Louis, said McCain's ad spending here reflects the fact that "he must win Missouri" in November.

For Obama, Missouri may be less crucial if he carries other swing states, Robertson said.

Among the candidates on the Aug. 5 primary ballot, the biggest ad spender is state Sen. Chris Koster, a Democratic candidate for attorney general.

Since July 10, Koster has doled out at least $429,595 to air 1,061 ad spots. Most of those ads have aired on TV stations in St. Louis and Kansas City, where the bulk of Missouri's Democratic voters live.

Koster's spending, so far, has overwhelmed that of his Democratic opponents. State Rep. Margaret Donnelly has paid $264,684 for 477 spots, all running on St. Louis or Kansas City stations.

No figures were available for state Rep. Jeff Harris, whose campaign said he was slated to begin airing ads Sunday night. Harris led in the recent Post-Dispatch/KMOV poll of likely Democratic primary voters.

In the Republican contest for governor, Steelman and Hulshof have been neck and neck in TV ad buys. During the last two weeks, she has slightly outspent Hulshof statewide. But he has run more ad spots because he's airing some in cheaper, rural TV markets.

Since July 10, Steelman spent $330,968 for 905 spots, compared with $303,587 (1,093 spots) for Hulshof.

Hulshof is running more ads on St. Louis and Springfield TV stations, and is overwhelming Steelman in Joplin, a GOP stronghold. However, she has had the airwaves to herself on Kansas City stations, which reach Republican-leaning suburban voters.

Meanwhile, the likely Democratic nominee for governor, Attorney General Jay Nixon, has spent almost $209,000 to run ads on stations in Kansas City, Springfield, and Columbia. His campaign ads didn't run in St. Louis during the last two weeks. However, an independent group - the Service Employees International Union - spent $195,000 to run ads in St. Louis and other media markets around the state.

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


July 23, 2008 Wednesday
THIRD EDITION


McCaskill's star soars with Obama She often is on TV on his behalf, offers advice behind the scenes.


BYLINE: By Deirdre Shesgreen Post-Dispatch Washington Bureau


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A1


LENGTH: 713 words


DATELINE: WASHINGTON


When John McCain's economic adviser recently pooh-poohed the country's fiscal troubles as a "mental recession," Barack Obama's campaign had a ready retort - from Claire McCaskill.

The Missouri Democrat zipped in to the Senate Radio/TV gallery, where she generated soundbites for nationwide broadcast blasting the comments from ex-Sen. Phil Gramm as "Exhibit A of which candidate for president is not in touch."

McCaskill's appearance was evidence of something else: her increasing prominence as one of a handful of top surrogates in Obama's presidential bid. While others, such as Sen. Dick Durbin, D-Ill., may hold more sway in Obama's inner circle, McCaskill is one of several politicians that the campaign is increasingly turning to for TV and campaign-trail appearances and for behind-the-scenes advice.

- On Monday, she was on the Fox News Network defending Obama's plan for a phased withdrawal from Iraq and an increased focus on Afghanistan.

- On Tuesday, she was on a conference call with reporters blasting a new McCain ad criticizing Obama on gas prices.

- Today, she's to be on another call - this one a private strategy session with a few top campaign advisers.

- On Sunday, she's scheduled to be back on Fox, talking about the economy opposite McCain campaign adviser and former Hewlett-Packard CEO Carly Fiorina.

"She's someone we have no hesitance in putting on TV or on the road because she understands and reflects the message of this campaign perfectly," said David Axelrod, Obama's chief campaign adviser. "She makes sense. She doesn't sound like the typical Washington palaver."

McCaskill makes political sense for Obama, too, said Larry Sabato, director of the Center for Politics at the University of Virginia. "She was with him early. She reaches out to women, which he needs to do after defeating Hillary Clinton and she's from a swing border state," he said.

Peter Fenn, a Democratic strategist not affiliated with the Obama campaign, said McCaskill's surprise endorsement last January helped cement her role in the operation.

"She was very reasoned and articulate" and "that didn't go unnoticed," he said. "It doesn't escape anyone that Missouri is tight and is potentially in play here, so her being visible nationally as an Obama spokesperson I think helps Obama in Missouri."

RURAL PITCH

In her role as an adviser, McCaskill has weighed in on strategy, such as where to deploy ground troops in advance of Missouri's Feb. 5 primary, which Obama narrowly won; and on policy, such as Obama's position against a gas-tax holiday.

McCaskill said she talks by phone or e-mail to Obama's top campaign strategists "every couple of days" and with his Missouri aides even more often.

Sometimes it's to dissect Missouri politics, sometimes it's to get briefed in advance of a TV appearance, and "sometimes it's ticky-tacky strategy."

What kind of advice is she giving?

"I've been sounding pretty much one note for a while: If you want to win, you have to get outside the traditional Democratic strongholds," she said.

McCaskill said her latest pitch is for a rural tour of key states, such as Missouri, Ohio and Indiana, to focus on McCain's record on farm issues. She has argued for getting local farmers, not big-name campaign surrogates, to host the events.

Axelrod said the campaign is chewing over a memo from McCaskill on that idea now.

In the meantime, he said, "You'll see as much of Claire McCaskill as she's able and willing to commit."

VP BUZZ

All the visibility has created fresh buzz about McCaskill's prospects as a vice presidential candidate, and she's repeatedly appeared on lists of possible choices.

Some pundits have noted that McCaskill wouldn't help Obama quiet questions about his experience, particularly on foreign policy. And picking a woman who is not Hillary Clinton could look like a slight to the New York senator just as he's trying to win over her supporters.

Both Axelrod and McCaskill sidestepped the veep question.

"I never discuss that," Axelrod said.

McCaskill also declined to talk about the issue, except to play down her chances of landing on the ticket. Still, she doesn't seem to mind all the attention and declined to take herself out of the running, as some have done.

"How could anyone really honestly say they're not interested?" she asked.

FOURTH EDITION


PRESIDENTIAL BUZZ WEEKLY UPDATE ON THE RACE FOR THE WHITE HOUSE


BYLINE: Compiled by Matthew Fernandes | Post-Dispatch


SECTION: NEWSWATCH; Pg. B4


LENGTH: 246 words


Who's excited?

John McCain faced an excitement deficit as a poll showed that his backers were less fired up than Barack Obama's. About four times as many Obama supporters say the election is exciting than McCain backers.

Iraq-usation

McCain launched a new television ad that accused Obama of switching positions on Iraq "to help himself become president." It aired as Obama's commitment to withdrawing combat troops within 16 months of becoming president has come under question.

Spoken like a car salesman

McCain pledged to help auto workers rebuild their industry and in the process jump-start the entire U.S. economy. Standing in a town hall meeting with hundreds of people and several shiny new cars, McCain sounded at times like a confident, encouraging salesman as he praised General Motors' plans for a long-range electric car.

Invitation rescinded

Former Georgia Sen. Max Cleland, who was an icon of Sen. John Kerry's 2004 campaign, was abruptly disinvited from appearing with Obama in Atlanta on July 8. His offense? He's a registered lobbyist. Obama's policy is to ban lobbyists from participating in fundraising or giving money. An associate of Cleland's said he has never actually lobbied in Washington.

Quoted

He will land in a very different Baghdad than the one he would have landed in ... if we had done what he wanted to do."

McCain on Obama's upcoming trip to Iraq

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


July 19, 2008 Saturday
FIRST EDITION


political Digest


BYLINE: From News Services


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A17


LENGTH: 527 words


WASHINGTON

McCain TV ad hits Obama on Iraq policy

Republican John McCain launched a new television ad Friday that accuses presidential rival Barack Obama of switching positions on Iraq "to help himself become president" just as the Democratic candidate prepared to make a high-profile trip to Baghdad.

McCain's sharply worded criticism was not limited to the ad. He said Friday that Obama would be facing a far less secure Iraq "if we had done what he wanted to do."

The 30-second ad, running on national cable and in 11 battleground states, is the hardest hit aimed at Obama so far by McCain.

"Barack Obama never held a single Senate hearing on Afghanistan," the ad's announcer says. "He hasn't been to Iraq in years. He voted against funding our troops. Positions that helped him win his nomination. Now Obama is changing to help himself become president."

The ad suggests that Obama was placing politics ahead of the country's interests.

Responding to the ad, Obama campaign spokesman Bill Burton said, "Given his calls for a civil campaign, it's disappointing that Senator McCain has slipped so easily into the same, tired campaign tactics that have become so familiar to the American people."

WASHINGTON

McCain's backers lagging on enthusiasm, poll finds

John McCain is facing an excitement deficit.

While overall interest in the presidential campaign has swelled since last fall, backers of Democrat Barack Obama are more fired up and express more loyalty to their candidate than McCain's do, a poll by The Associated Press and Yahoo News reported Friday. In addition, individual groups backing Obama - African-Americans, Democrats and liberals - are more enthusiastic than whites, Republicans and conservatives, who are more aligned with McCain, GOP senator from Arizona.

Obama faces hurdles of his own. The poll shows lagging fervor for the Illinois senator by supporters of his vanquished rival, Hillary Rodham Clinton. And there are lots of dispirited and undecided independents, who are expected to be pivotal on Election Day, now less then four months off.

The passion and interest shown by blocs of voters are important because they affect who will be motivated to vote. For now, the numbers favor Obama: 38 percent of his supporters say the election is exciting compared with 9 percent of McCain's. Sixty-five percent of Obama's backers say they are hopeful about the campaign, double that of McCain's, and the Democrat's supporters are three times likelier to express pride.

BERLIN

Obama to visit Thursday with German chancellor

German Chancellor Angela Merkel will welcome Democratic presidential candidate Barack Obama to her office in Berlin on Thursday of next week, her spokesman said Friday.

Obama's stop in Berlin is part of a tour of the Middle East and Europe aimed at burnishing the his foreign policy credentials.

Obama also is expected to deliver a speech in Berlin. It remains unclear where that event might take place.

Campaign officials have announced stops in Jordan, Israel, Germany, France and England. Obama also has pledged to travel to Iraq and Afghanistan this summer, but aides have not said whether those war zones will be part of the same trip.

THIRD EDITION


political Digest


BYLINE: From News Services


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A17


LENGTH: 541 words


WASHINGTON

McCain reports raising over $21 million in June

Republican presidential candidate John McCain reports raising more than $21 million in June - his best fundraising month so far - and spending nearly $26 million, according to filings with the Federal Election Commission.

McCain spent more than $16 million on advertising during the month, five times more than he spent in May, when the Democratic presidential primary was still being contested by Barack Obama and Hillary Rodham Clinton. Obama clinched the Democratic nomination on June 3.

McCain's spending, more than twice what he spent in May, exceeded his fundraising in June by more than $4 million.

WASHINGTON

McCain TV ad hits Obama on Iraq policy

Republican John McCain launched a new television ad Friday that accuses presidential rival Barack Obama of switching positions on Iraq "to help himself become president" just as the Democratic candidate prepared to make a high-profile trip to Baghdad.

McCain's sharply worded criticism was not limited to the ad. He said Friday that Obama would be facing a far less secure Iraq "if we had done what he wanted to do."

The 30-second ad, running on national cable and in 11 battleground states, is the hardest hit aimed at Obama so far by McCain. The ad suggests that Obama was placing politics ahead of the country's interests.

Responding to the ad, Obama campaign spokesman Bill Burton said, "Given his calls for a civil campaign, it's disappointing that Senator McCain has slipped so easily into the same, tired campaign tactics that have become so familiar to the American people."

WASHINGTON

McCain's backers lagging on enthusiasm, poll finds

John McCain is facing an excitement deficit.

While overall interest in the presidential campaign has swelled since last fall, backers of Democrat Barack Obama are more fired up and express more loyalty to their candidate than McCain's do, a poll by The Associated Press and Yahoo News reported Friday.

In addition, individual groups backing Obama - African-Americans, Democrats and liberals - are more enthusiastic than whites, Republicans and conservatives, who are more aligned with McCain, GOP senator from Arizona.

Obama faces hurdles of his own. The poll shows lagging fervor for the Illinois senator by supporters of his vanquished rival, Hillary Rodham Clinton. And there are lots of dispirited and undecided independents, who are expected to be pivotal on Election Day, now less then four months off.

BERLIN

Obama to visit Thursday with German chancellor

German Chancellor Angela Merkel will welcome Democratic presidential candidate Barack Obama to her office in Berlin on Thursday of next week, her spokesman said Friday.

Spokesman Ulrich Wilhelm said the meeting at the chancellery is expected to take place on Thursday morning.

Obama's stop in Berlin is part of a tour of the Middle East and Europe aimed at burnishing the his foreign policy credentials.

Obama also is expected to deliver a speech in Berlin. It remains unclear where that event might take place.

Campaign officials have announced stops in Jordan, Israel, Germany, France and England. Obama also has pledged to travel to Iraq and Afghanistan this summer, but aides have not said whether those war zones will be part of the same trip.

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


July 17, 2008 Thursday
THIRD EDITION


Union spends $450,000 to air ad for Jay Nixon


BYLINE: Jo Mannies


SECTION: METRO; Pg. B3


LENGTH: 804 words


St. Louis television viewers are getting their first look at a campaign ad promoting Attorney General Jay Nixon, the likely Democratic nominee for Missouri governor.

But the ad isn't from Nixon's campaign.

It was produced and paid for by the Service Employees International Union, which plans to spend $450,000 to air the ad statewide over the next two weeks.

That is, by far, the most ad money spent this year by an independent group for a Missouri candidate. The spending doesn't count as a donation, as long as there's no coordination with the candidate.

To put it in perspective, SEIU is spending more than the combined tally for the past two weeks (roughly $281,000) spent by the two chief Republican contenders for governor: U.S. Rep. Kenny Hulshof and state Treasurer Sarah Steelman.

SEIU spokeswoman Sara Howard, who's based in St. Louis, said the union's hefty ad buy is "clearly a recognition of how important this election is, and how much it matters who's elected governor."

The SEIU ad is focusing on health care and the 2005 budget cuts that tossed about 100,000 people off Missouri's Medicaid roles. Nixon has promised to restore that coverage.

Nixon spokesman Oren Shur said the campaign had no input on the ad, and no advance knowledge of SEIU's help. Shur said campaign staffers first read about the ad on the Post-Dispatch's political blog.

Coincidentally, the SEIU ad began airing Tuesday, the day Nixon launched his own first campaign ad: a 60-second biographical spot that features Nixon driving a car and speaking to the camera about his life.

For now, Nixon's own campaign ad is not running in the St. Louis area. Shur said Nixon will air ads soon in St. Louis, in the state's most expensive media market.

When asked about both ads, Hulshof spokesman Scott Baker asked, "Why now?"

Unlike Hulshof and Steelman, Nixon faces no major opponent in the state's Aug. 5 primary.

"He's supposed to be the candidate hoarding money and standing on a big lead in the polls,'' Baker said. "What do they know that has made it important for them to get on the air now?"

SEIU's Howard replied: "This is a perfect time to remind voters they have a pretty clear choice in November.''

Shur offered a similar view.

But another reason may be to whip up Democratic voters for the Aug. 5 primary.

Although the Republican battle for governor is garnering headlines, there has been some behind-the-scenes speculation that voter turnout - particularly among Democrats - could be down for Aug. 5.

In St. Louis County, for example, Republican elections director Joe Goeke said the absentee ballot traffic so far has "been slow.''

With about three weeks remaining, 2,392 county residents have cast absentee ballots. That compares to 13,674 absentee ballots cast overall for the August primary in 2004. The current pace could hint at a lower final tally this year.

A lower-than-expected August turnout in Missouri would be bad news for either major party, especially after the record-setting turnout in the Feb. 5 presidential primary.

A low August turnout could signal potential voter burnout, which neither major party wants to see, heading into the November presidential election.

MCCASKILL MUM ON VP

After months of serving as a preferred surrogate for presumptive Democratic presidential nominee Barack Obama, U.S. Sen. Claire McCaskill continues to attract speculation that she might be under consideration as his running mate.

Obama and McCaskill have each played down such talk. Last Sunday, while on NBC's "Meet The Press," McCaskill told moderator Tom Brokaw that she wouldn't bet on her chances.

Since then, she's told aides that she'll no longer comment at all on the subject.

McCaskill's strengths include her close political and personal ties to Obama.

But if selected, her family's finances - notably, the business dealings of husband Joseph Shepard - could come under public and press scrutiny again. Shepard, a successful developer and financier, was a target of GOP ads in McCaskill's failed bid for governor in 2004, and her successful campaign for the U.S. Senate in 2006.

In any event, McCaskill underscored her loyalty to Obama this week when she loaned her communications director, Adrianne Marsh, to his presidential campaign. Marsh, who asked for the leave, will work for Obama in Florida.

LIBERTARIAN ROOT IN TOWN

Presumptive Republican presidential nominee John McCain isn't the only White House aspirant in town this week.

Libertarian vice presidential nominee Wayne Allyn Root is flying in today for a two-day foray around the state. Root is the running mate of Libertarian presidential nominee Bob Barr, a former GOP congressman.

At the moment, the Libertarian ticket is the only third-party pairing to qualify for Missouri's November ballot.

For more details on his visit and for more political news, visit stltoday.com/politicalfix
