July 14, 2008 Monday
THIRD EDITION


LETTERS FROM READERS


SECTION: EDITORIAL; Pg. B6


LENGTH: 1438 words


Protect the park: No land-grab at the Arch

I am appalled that anyone, particularly the good former Sen. John Danforth, would not see the folly in attempting to take land away from the Jefferson National Expansion Memorial in order to promote narrow, short-term commercial interests.

As a 32-year veteran of the National Park Service and speaking for the 650-member Coalition of National Park Service Retirees, we strongly are opposed to any effort to tamper with NPS management and control of this important unit of the National Park System. The Arch already contributes enormously to the economic viability of St. Louis, by way of its 250,000 annual visitors who are attracted to one of the most widely recognized architectural symbols in the United States.

The existing museum, under the Arch, was designed to avoid detracting from the central feature. Taking land to build an above-ground building would be a travesty. Furthermore, it simply would not work. Does anyone really believe that someone would be induced to drive to St. Louis to see another museum on the grounds, much less one that would detract from the existing open space?

Further, open space is at a premium in this area. A quick glance at any map reveals that there are plenty of buildings, roads, parking spaces and hotels outside the park grounds. The green space, like Central Park in New York City, is much more valuable as it is. If the mayor and the Danforth Foundation want to help, why not put a facility just outside the park where there are plenty of unused buildings?

We do agree with placing a lid over Interstate 70, since that would help both the city and the park visitors. We urge area residents to tell their elected representatives to not allow the land-grab and to protect the national memorial for all people for all time.

Don Castleberry | Little Rock, Ark.

Burke is a true believer in the safety of children

Regarding the editorial "The true believer" (June 29): The Catholic Church laments the very real damage done to those who were sexually abused by priests. Archbishop Raymond Burke strongly has supported all efforts to protect our children since the day he arrived in St. Louis in 2004. His commitment to the safety of children is unwavering.

The Archdiocese disputes the statement from the Survivor's Network of those Abused by Priests, a statement the Editorial Board did not verify with the Archdiocese.

The Archdiocese of St. Louis has no knowledge of any priest against whom a credible accusation of sexual abuse against a minor has been proven holding any pastoral role within the Archdiocese of St. Louis. Those are the priests for whom the archbishop is responsible.

Archbishop Burke's record on child safety stands for itself. The Archdiocese of St. Louis had an extremely strong showing when it was audited last year for compliance to the Safe Environment Program, mandated by the United States Conference of Catholic Bishops. Not only did the Archdiocese pass the audit, it was commended as one of the strongest programs in the country.

Compliance figures compiled for June 30 this year show that than 99.6 percent of all the 35,000 local church employees and volunteers - everyone from the archbishop to soccer referees - are in full compliance with the SEP. The remainder are those who have just begun the compliance process. Thanks to the strong leadership of Archbishop Burke, archdiocesan institutions and activities are safe for children.

By repeating in print unproven and spurious assertions, the Post-Dispatch is complicit in perpetuating a myth and is giving credibility to information that is simply not true. It was an example, par excellence, of journalism at its weakest.

Monsignor John Shamleffer | St. Louis Judicial Vicar

Monsignor Richard Stika | St. Louis Vicar, Office of Child and Youth Protection

Deacon Phil Hengen | St. Louis Director - Office for Child and Youth Protection

Spinning Cerberus

The editorial "Failure in Fenton" (July 2) was outrageous and well outside the boundary of spin in which newspapers such as the Post-Dispatch are allowed by the First Amendment to operate.

Cerberus did not sign an agreement to acquire econtrol of Chrysler until mid-May 2007, and did not acquire control until August. To imply that Cerberus is responsible for Chrysler's present circumstances, that Cerberus broke promises and failed Fenton and the United Auto Workers, that Cerberus drove Chrysler into a ditch or that because of Cerberus, Chrysler has not innovated is dishonest and holds Cerberus to impossible standards. No one could turn Chrysler around in nine months, especially in the present economic environment. But Cerberus is ahead of schedule to turn Chrysler around.

The editorial should have called on us and on Chrysler to learn from past mistakes, roll up our sleeves and get on with competing in a world driven by competition.

My deepest concern is the impact the editorial could have on anyone who might consider St. Louis as a home for a new or expanding business. Who would want to invest in St. Louis, risking both fortune and a good name to the mendacity of the editorial page?

John L. Davidson | Des Peres

Set on war

A few months ago, Iran held an American woman and accused her of anti-Iranian activity. The United States denied the charge and said America doesn't do things like that.

A recent issue of the New Yorker reports that Congress has appropriated $400 million for anti-Iranian activities. Is it possible that the woman was guilty as charged? Is Bush set on war?

James Elliott | Florissant

History repeats

President George W. Bush probably will start a preemptive war with Iran before his term is out to boost his legacy and power. Whiteman Air Force Base is preparing bombers and weapons for the purpose of war with Iran. Funds were approved by Congress. The Lieberman-Kyl amendment that threatens to "combat, contain" and stop Iran with "military instruments" was passed in the Senate in September.

Like Mr. Bush, both Sen. Barack Obama and John McCain, the probably nominees for president, are ignoring the December 2007 National Intelligence Estimate that that Iran halted its nuclear weapons operations in 2003. Recently, people visiting Iran have found incredible hospitality and generosity among its people toward the United States.

Mr. Bush's position has been that Iran is evil and a threat and that "military options are not off the table." In addition, he is striving to gain congressional validation for military action against Iran. With an attack on Iran, the entire Middle East could flare up and cause a loss of Iranian oil, which could send the price to $200 a barrel.

Once again, is this for control of oil and power in the Middle East? Will we have more loss of life and limb, total chaos and debt disaster again - just as in Iraq and Afghanistan?

Ava Jordan | St. Louis

Protected status

The story "Federal law is now a shield against employee benefits" (July 6) told about a dying Spherion employee who was misled by his employer, which had switched insurance carriers, resulting in his widow being denied $426,000 in benefits, and the beneficiary of a deceased man's retirement plan that lost $500,000 in account value due to Schwab's 16 months of delays. The law, as it is being interpreted, is a shield protecting employers from lawsuits for such losses.

Corporations are legal fictions possessing all the rights of natural persons. Apparently, they have all the rights except the ones from which they can be protected - including being sued. How oddly Orwellian that some "natural persons" may be "more equal than others" in the eyes of the law.

Dan Jestic | O'Fallon, Mo.

Theft by small-print

Regarding "Federal law is now a shield against employee benefits" (July 6): Strangely enough, the law designed to protect employees is being used to take away their benefits. It's hard to believe - but perhaps to be expected in our society. It seems to be a case of small-print theft, a variation on white-collar crime.

No wonder our economy is in disarray if the law has been perverted so that the rich and wealthy are able to profit from the distress of the poor and disadvantaged. There doesn't seem to be any enthusiasm on the part of government, Congress or the courts to rectify the situation.

There has been all this talk of patriotism. What is patriotism? I think it's very simple: spirited defense of freedom, liberty and justice for all. I would like to see it from the Congress, the courts and the executive branch. But cases such as those described in the article indicate that patriotism is in short supply in the places where its needed the most.

Steven Brenner | Creve Coeur

campaign notebook


BYLINE: FROM NEWS SERVICES


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A3

BELLEVILLE, MICH.

McCain distances himself from adviser's remarks

Republican presidential candidate John McCain sharply broke from an economic adviser who dubbed the United States "a nation of whiners" in a "mental recession" as he sought to counter criticism that he's weak on the economy.

"I strongly disagree" with Phil Gramm's remarks, McCain told reporters in what amounted to nothing short of a smackdown against one of his top surrogates and longtime friends. "Phil Gramm does not speak for me. I speak for me."

McCain said a person who just lost a job or a mother struggling to pay for a child's education "isn't suffering from a mental recession."

Gramm, a former Texas senator who is a vice chairman of the Swiss bank UBS and has a doctorate in economics, made the remarks in an interview with the Washington Times.

Campaigning in Fairfax, Va., Democrat Barack Obama used Gramm's comments to say McCain is out of touch: "America already has one Dr. Phil. We don't need another one when it comes to the economy."

WASHINGTON

June is McCain's best fundraising month

Campaign officials say Republican John McCain raised more than $22 million last month for his presidential bid.

That's McCain's best month yet. He ends the month of June with nearly $27 million cash on hand, according to campaign officials.

McCain campaign manager Rick Davis said Thursday that McCain and the national Republican Party together entered this month with about $95 million in the bank.

The money has given McCain the ability to outspend Democratic rival Barack Obama on television advertising in key battleground states, Davis says.

Obama has not yet revealed his June fundraising.

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


July 10, 2008 Thursday
THIRD EDITION


Obama plan for Missouri staggers state GOP head


BYLINE: Jo Mannies


SECTION: METRO; Pg. D3


LENGTH: 769 words


For the past three weeks, presumptive Republican presidential nominee John McCain has been heavily outspending Democratic rival Barack Obama on TV ads in Missouri.

But McCain may not match Obama's plans for a huge field operation.

Within weeks, Democrats say, Obama should have more than 100 paid staffers in the state - some estimate 150 - and should have opened roughly 35 field offices, most of them in rural areas. That's far more than the 15 or so offices and 80 full-time staff that Sen. John Kerry posted in the state, for a time, during his 2004 Democratic presidential bid.

"This will be the largest political commitment in Missouri by a Democratic presidential campaign, ever,'' said local Democratic consultant Mike Kelley, former executive director for the Missouri Democratic Party.

Obama's new Missouri spokesman, Debbie Mesloh, would not confirm specific numbers but didn't dispute the gist. On Wednesday, the campaign announced the hiring of at least a half-dozen senior staffers to oversee two tandem field operations. One, called the Missouri Campaign for Change, will be solely a voter contact organization led by paid Democratic operatives with Missouri ties.

The rural communities expected to see Obama offices include Cape Girardeau, Maryville, West Plains and Kennett.

"We're making a significant investment,'' Mesloh said.

McCain regional campaign manager Gentry Collins said the campaign had a few paid staffers in Missouri at present. The McCain operation is coordinating its efforts with the Missouri Republican Party, which Collins said already has opened a half-dozen offices around the state.

State GOP Executive Director Jared Craighead said he has seven campaign field workers in place and several more soon to be hired. Field offices in St. Louis and St. Charles should be opened within the week, he said.

"We're going to deploy the resources we need to deliver Missouri for John McCain,'' Craighead said.

He also disputes how big of an operation Obama will place in the state. "There's absolutely no way they're going to devote 150 full-time paid staff in Missouri,'' Craighead said. "I believe they'll pull out staff and send them to states that Obama has a better chance of carrying."

Collins, with the McCain campaign, contended that its hefty outlay for Missouri ads is a better indication of which candidate is more committed to the state.

Since June 3, McCain has spent at least $850,787 to air TV ads on Missouri stations, according to Campaign Media Analysis Group, a nonpartisan Virginia-based firm monitoring ad spending for the Post-Dispatch. That compares to $312,898 for Obama.

Clay backs Koster

One of Obama's biggest area boosters is U.S. Rep. William Lacy Clay, D-St. Louis, the region's most prominent African-American elected official.

But this week, Clay shifted his attention to another contest: his party's nasty three-way primary fight for Missouri attorney general.

Clay announced he was supporting U.S. Sen. Chris Koster, D-Harrisonville. Clay also joined Koster, a St. Louis native, at several stops this week in north St. Louis County. Clay's endorsement puts him at odds with Koster's rivals, state Reps. Margaret Donnelly of Richmond Heights and Jeff Harris of Columbia.

Clay praised Koster's "great courage to cross over into the light'' when he switched political parties last year. Clay also chastised Koster's opponents for repeatedly questioning whether he's a true Democrat.

Clay's support comes as Koster has been under fire for his campaign fundraising practices. Most of Koster's campaign money originated from a few wealthy individuals, including multimillionaire Rex Sinquefield, who gave to numerous campaign committees that donated to Koster.

Clay's endorsement appeared aimed at helping Koster woo African-American votes. Koster also has hired former state Rep. Yaphett El-Amin, D-St. Louis, as part of that outreach.

Koster spokesman Danny Kanner denied behind-the-scenes talk that the campaign also has hired state Rep. Juanita Head Walton, or her husband, former state Rep. Elbert Walton.

Hulshof and Steelman Agree on many issues

Area Republicans heard little dissent Tuesday night during the first local forum featuring Missouri's two Republican candidates for governor - state Treasurer Sarah Steelman and U.S. Rep. Kenny Hulshof.

Held at the Brentwood Community Center, the event underscored that Hulshof and Steelman agree on many issues.

Both support lower taxes, fewer regulations on businesses and expanding the right to carry concealed weapons. Both oppose abortion, embryonic stem-cell research and the use of eminent domain for private developments.

LETTERS FROM READERS


SECTION: EDITORIAL; Pg. D6


LENGTH: 1411 words


Despite poor management, we can help prevent tragedy in future floods

As many families grapple with catastrophic flooding in our region and others, my thoughts and prayers are with those in harm's way. My home is out of danger, but the entrance to my village closes when the water rises. That is to be expected with how the river is managed: narrower rivers cause higher flood waters. The U.S. Army Corps of Engineers keeps building navigation structures that narrow the river, and it permits the building of levees around floodplains.

My colleagues at the National Wildlife Federation and Washington University's Department of Earth and Planetary Science and I agree: We must adopt policies that restrict new building behind decertified levees and restrict the building of any levee that encourages development in high-risk areas. There are also things we can do now to reduce floodplain risk:

- Review all significant dams and levees for risks and public safety;

- Increase wetlands protections to reduce flooding and keep people safe;

- Recommit to a national hazard mitigation policy with emphasis on voluntary buyouts of high-risk, flood-prone properties and dedicating high-risk, flood-prone lands to open-space uses;

- Increase the nation's commitment to updating and improving floodplain maps, including urbanization;

- Reduce global warming pollution that is fueling a warming climate and making tragedies such as these more frequent and more intense.

We now have a more chaotic, more flood-prone river; we can't even predict when the historical spring-rise flood will occur. That's not good for the environment, fishermen, barge employees trying to get their products to market or residents of flooded towns.

Congress passed the Water Resources Development Act in November. It includes funding for an ecosystem-restoration program for the Upper Mississippi River. Many projects will require up to 35 percent cost-share funding from the states. Legislators must secure the funding to take advantage of the federal money for floodplain purchasing from willing land owners. Our flooded towns, fishermen, towboat operators and environment will thank them for being proactive.

Christine Favilla | Elsah Three Rivers Project Coordinator, Piasa Palisades Group of the Sierra Club

No more levees

The recent flooding, both here and in Iowa, and in the spring in Valley Park, proves it is time for the state and federal governments to step in and stop the building of levees and the insuring of homes and businesses in floodplains. You can't control Mother Nature.

Let these plains and river bottoms be used for farming and other basic uses without buildings or levees. We have enough government debt. We do not need more foolish development. Never mind that we are losing agricultural land and need more crops for our own use.

Ed Herr | Florissant

Unfettered greed

Regarding the editorial "Failure pays" (June 23): To the words "obscene" and "unfair," as the paper characterized the payout packages for corporate executive failure, we might add "embarrassing," "fraudulent" and "legal embezzlement."

Congress should reconsider the fact that corporations are considered legal entities shielded and protected to a degree from the welfare of public policy. And institutional and individual shareholders must continue to object strongly to the mismanagement of corporate pay in all its freebie forms.

The idea that boards of directors listen to consultants and design executive contracts loaded with stock options, restricted stock and bonuses to attract top talent is a fiction. If these managers believe in their own ability, they can buy into their own stock with after-tax dollars, just like every other shareholder does. Managers and boards are running amok. If this unfettered and unwarranted greed is not contained, we may see the end of capitalism as we know it.

We can express our "customer satisfaction" rating by boycotting General Motors and Chrysler vehicles, tearing up Citicorp credit cards, moving brokerage accounts out of Merrill Lynch, and passing up lunch at Panera's Bread Co. And then "short" the stocks.

Laurence C. Day | Ladue

Spreading the wealth

The editorial "Failure pays" (June 23) addressed the money given wealthy CEOs, especially to those who are incompetent and lose their positions. The point about those wealthy individuals would be more credible if the paper would have included others whose wealth is subsidized by the average American.

The editorial omitted failing sports teams whose players draw millions per year. It omitted the owners who get tax advantages and who have the nerve to ask for new stadiums and other expensive items, expecting people to come up with the money instead of taking it from their own bloated pockets. It omitted wealthy businesses that have the nerve to ask for tax breaks.

It failed to point out the entertainment industry, which is raking in billions and then paying exorbitant salaries. Average citizens pay for this, through such means as increased prices for the items advertised.

Of course, there are the congressmen, senators, judges, etc., who can serve a short time and receive inflated retirement and health benefits for the rest of their lives. And there are all the add-ons to bills that provide luxury pork to countless areas and groups. Who pays for that? We do.

Instead of focusing on only one aspect of undeserved wealth, let's look at the wider picture. The Post-Dispatch should be a leader in this effort.

Ruth Hemmann | Des Peres

Working the advantages

David Brooks marshals a kitchen sink-full of specious arguments to convince us that U.S. Sen. Barack Obama, D-Ill., the presumptive Democratic nominee for president, is a sell-out "Fast Eddie" ("Obama: 'Slick' doesn't even come close," June 24).

Mr. Obama did not take positions on politically sensitive issues in the Illinois legislature? Never mind that his non-votes reflected solidarity with the strategies of the Democratic leadership on issues on which there was no chance of prevailing and where votes might endanger at-risk Democrats.

He finally disavowed the Rev. Jeremiah Wright's rhetoric? If guilt-by-association won't work, crucify him because, after continuing provocations, he finally let Mr. Wright know that enough is enough.

Not a workhorse senator? Mr. Obama was involved with more than 500 pieces of legislation that addressed such significant issues as foreign policy, legislative ethics, environmental and climate change, veterans' benefits, homeland security, consumer protection and education, among others. Compared to other junior senators, his record is outstanding.

Mr. Brooks professes to be shocked that Mr. Obama exploits his campaign advantages, seeking some control over the terms of debate and selecting campaign finance protocols that may be most beneficial to him in an environment in which the public financing system already is broken.

Why is Mr. Brooks not equally concerned with the maneuvering of U.S. Sen. John McCain, R-Ariz., the presumptive Republican nominee, who poses as Mr. Clean while staffing his campaign with corporate lobbyists, embracing 527s, accepting special interest contributions and on and on ad nauseam? It is very clear to me which candidate is the real Fast Eddie who so disgusts Mr. Brooks, and his name is not Barack Obama.

Willy Kessler | Ballwin

Meet the new new, same as the old new

The Post-Dispatch positively gushed like a schoolgirl when it endorsed U.S. Sen. Barack Obama, D-Ill., trumpeting his candidacy as if he represented something new and refreshing.

It hasn't taken long, however, to see that Mr. Obama is no different from candidates of the past. Despite being the least-experienced candidate in recent memory, Mr. Obama has shown that when it comes to flip-flopping, he's a veteran politician.

Add his connection to convicted political fixer Tony Rezko, and Mr. Obama looks every bit the tawdry politician that he claims not to be.

If he goes back on his word so readily now, why should we expect any different if he's elected in the fall?

Steve Reiss | Affton

I want my candidate on MTV!

MTV has been active in trying to get its young viewers interested in politics. Now the network is looking to profit from it.

MTV is selling time for political spots this election season, meaning pictures of Barack Obama and John McCain may turn up among the women of "The Hills" and souped-up vans on "Pimp My Ride."

The network hasn't taken political advertising since its "Choose or Lose" political activism campaign in 1992, spokeswoman Jeannie Kedas said. "Given where we are and how the young voters have become increasingly engaged, we decided political advertising would be a good fit for our audience," she said.

MTV's corporate parent Viacom recently reported that its second quarter domestic advertising revenue growth would be 3 to 4 percent, after earlier forecasting a growth of 7 percent.

Strong ad sales for the fall and strong ratings, along with the political ad money, should help revenue growth, said Richard Greenfield, an analyst at Pali Capital.

Kyle Roberts, president of Smart Media, a Washington-based firm that buys advertising time for GOP candidates, said, "From a financial perspective, now is the best time ever because young people are going to have more of an impact on this election than ever before." The decision is most likely to benefit Democrat Obama, he said.

THIRD EDITION


McCain puts on a show in Missouri Republican's campaign has outspent Obama's.


BYLINE: By Jo Mannies Post-Dispatch Political Correspondent


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A5


LENGTH: 289 words


Since his visit to southwest Missouri last week, presumptive Republican presidential nominee John McCain has aired more than three times as many campaign commercials in the state as his Democratic rival, Barack Obama.

McCain's campaign outspent and out-aired Obama in every major media market in Missouri, including St. Louis, from June 19 through Wednesday. McCain held a town hall forum in Springfield, Mo., on June 18.

Both candidates' ad spending in Missouri and Illinois is being monitored for the Post-Dispatch by the Campaign Media Analysis Group, a Virginia-based, nonpartisan firm.

McCain spent $224,696 for 791 spots that ran on local broadcast stations around Missouri. That compares to $115,054 spent by the Obama campaign to air 212 spots.

McCain's more aggressive spending comes as both campaigns say that Missouri is among their targeted states as they head into the remaining four months before the Nov. 4 general election.

Obama is returning to Missouri on Monday, with a stop in Independence. That's his second visit to the state since he won enough delegates on June 3 to become the presumptive Democratic nominee.

McCain is running no TV ads on local stations in Illinois, while Obama is spending about $25,000 for spots airing in Southern Illinois markets.

The Obama ad airing in Missouri is a 60-second biographical spot, while McCain's two ads each run 30 seconds. McCain's newest ad, which began airing Friday, promotes his energy proposals.

Both campaigns are running the bulk of their ad spots during morning and evening TV news programs.

Both also seem to be targeting women and the elderly, running spots on Oprah Winfrey's talk show, the Wheel of Fortune game show and Entertainment Tonight, a celebrity gossip show.

THIRD EDITION


Obama plans to focus on Missouri


BYLINE: By Jo Mannies Post-Dispatch Political Correspondent


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A7


LENGTH: 374 words


Presumptive Democratic presidential nominee Barack Obama is targeting Missouri with some of his strongest campaign operatives, as well as ads, as part of a serious effort to carry the state in November, his national campaign manager said Thursday.

"We're going to mount as vigorous a campaign as we can in Missouri,'' said campaign manager David Plouffe in a conference call with reporters.

"It'll be close," he acknowledged, referring to Obama's Republican rival, John McCain.

Missouri already is among 18 states, most of them swing states or Republican leaning, where Obama is running his first general-election ad. The 60-second spot is largely biographical.

Missouri television viewers with cable also are seeing some McCain spots on national cable stations such as MSNBC.

Obama narrowly carried Missouri in the Feb. 5 Democratic primary, defeating Hillary Rodham Clinton. McCain was the Republican victor in the GOP primary, although he received fewer votes than Obama or Clinton.

But in a general election, Missouri hasn't been captured by a Democrat since 1996, when Bill Clinton won re-election.

In 2004, Republican George W. Bush defeated Democrat John Kerry in Missouri by more than 200,000 votes.

This time, Plouffe contends, Missouri is fertile territory for Obama's message. "Foundationally, people in Missouri are looking for change,'' he said, adding that they were "very unhappy about the economy" and "the way Washington works."

"We're obviously going to have to do a very good job of optimizing the Democratic base,'' he said, adding that Obama's Missouri campaign operation was among his strongest on Feb. 5.

The campaign is sending some of its "strongest operatives'' into Missouri now, Plouffe said.

McCain spokesman Jeff Sadosky replied that their camp agreed with Plouffe's general assessment of the discontent among Missouri voters and the potential closeness of the contest between the candidates.

But Sadosky predicted that, by November, Missouri voters would reject Obama's message and the man.

McCain's life history, he said, shows that he "put his country ahead of his personal political ambitions."

Obama, said Sadosky, "time and time again ... has placed his own personal and political ambitions ahead of the good of the country."

Walking to save cash is a natural reaction to $4-a-gallon gas

One recent morning, in my unemployed splendor, I walked to the bank - much to the chagrin of every other person on the road. With gas at $4 a gallon and the price of every item on every shelf increasing seemingly by the day, I wanted to save money. So I decided to walk. It isn't very far from my home, and I did sweat a little, but the awkward looks that I received from passers by when I had to cross Big Bend Boulevard were remarkable.

Every head in every car stared at me as if I were an anomaly of nature.

I strolled through the crosswalk as people looked at me as if I were crazy. What is wrong with walking? Why has the American society turned its back on such a natural gift? I know it was hot, but it wasn't painful or uncomfortable.

The Eisenhower Interstate project may be to blame, bisecting our city, segmenting and chopping our neighborhoods into small pockets of unused sidewalks. Perhaps our discomfort with being slightly uncomfortable has created the car culture, with air-conditioned seats and climate-controlled cup holders. We are resigned to an unnatural state of living.

Sweating is a natural reaction to the summer months; walking is a natural reaction to $4 gas. I urge St. Louis to get up and walk to the nearest grocery, coffee shop, bank or drug store and let's see how much gas our Midwestern city can save. I'm not a proponent of changing everything, and I know that if you live in Brentwood and you work downtown, driving is essential. But once you get home from work, don't get back in the car; instead, walk to local businesses to support your health and your neighborhood.

Dan Presson | Richmond Heights

Toot St. Louis' horn

How inspirational it is that the bi-state region was named an "All-America City" for the first time since 1956. More than 100 communities competed this year, and the St. Louis region was the only metropolitan area, rather than just a city, to receive the award. What's disappointing is that the story of this prestigious national-level recognition was buried on page A15 of the Saturday paper ("'All-America City' title is bestowed on this region," June 7). When will our mainstream media move past the mentality of "when it bleeds it leads"?

The people of the St. Louis metro region are accomplishing outstanding deeds that are remarkable in terms of the sacrifice and creative approaches to problem solving often involved. This May, FOCUS St. Louis honored 20 regional success stories at our annual "What's Right With The Region!" awards celebration. These successes - and the three projects that just won us the All-America City award - are major stories, each worthy of a large headline on the front page. Will we ever see them there?

Christine A. Chadwick | St. Louis Executive Director, FOCUS St. Louis

If we open it, they will come

St. Louis Convention & Visitors Commission Chairman Dan Dierdorf's responsibility is far greater than keeping a football team in St. Louis for eight to 10 macho, high-decibel, manic Sundays a year. He is charged with recovering for St. Louis a healthy flow of conventions, associations meetings, travel groups and people who travel from one area to another for entertainment.

That means Kiel Opera House, a 100-nights-a-year Muny and, as the cherry on the sundae, the Goldenrod Showboat on the riverfront.

Ed Golterman | Kirkwood

Breeder reactors

Regarding "2nd nuclear plant is back in play in Callaway County" (June 9): It is good news that AmerenUE is considering another nuclear plant. The next step is to get the government off its duff and allow the building of breeder reactors. A breeder reactor produces more fuel than it consumes, with no waste.

R.N. Kersting | Sunset Hills

The spirit of A-B

Years ago, I moved to St. Louis with TWA. Before, during and after those wonderful years, Anheuser-Busch was important to me as a product and as a representation of what a corporate citizen should be. We spoke of the Busch family as if we knew them personally and defended the brand to all comers. I even booked travel for the cockatoo when I worked at Maritz.

We have been back in North Carolina for three years now, and I still tear up at Clydesdale commercials and Bud Light radio spots. I recently spent a month in the United Kingdom on business and was pleased to see Budweiser prominently displayed and consumed. I miss seeing the big neon eagle on Interstate 64/U.S. Highway 40 and the camels at Grant's Farm. The day the stockholders sell their souls to InBev, I will have had my last A-B product.

Pamela Gladney | Charlotte, N.C.

St. Louis could be beer capital of the world

Why can't St. Louis be the beer capital of the world and not just the United States? St. Louis has the tradition, the resources and certainly the competitive spirit in Anheuser-Busch. It's common knowledge that the consumption of beer is going up in Latin America, and even more so throughout Asia.

A loss of Anheuser-Busch could mean the loss of thousands of jobs and a further weakening of the city's reputation as a corporate headquarters center. It certainly would make the city less of a tourist and convention attraction.

By all means, our civic leaders and media should encourage all shareholders of Anheuser-Busch stock to reject any possible offer coming from Belgium or Brazil.

In spite of what some people say, they don't like us in brewing circles in Europe. St. Louis is a great city, and we all should fight to make it better and more successful.

Louis F. Desloge Jr. | Creve Coeur

Victory for rule of law

The recent decision by the U.S. Supreme Court should be applauded by every lawyer in America. Whether you are John Adams, Woodrow Wilson, Abraham Lincoln, Franklin Roosevelt, Richard Nixon, Lyndon Johnson or George W. Bush, you may not step outside the boundaries of the law. The decisions were a major victory for the rule of law in America.

Rory Ellinger | O'Fallon, Mo.

Not the end, a beginning

Hillary Clinton's concession speech was her first campaign speech of the 2012 election contest. The Clintons know that U.S. Sen. Barack Obama, D-Ill., the presumptive Democratic nominee for president, is not electable (and they said so during the nomination campaign) and is going to lose to U.S. Sen. John McCain, R-Ariz., the presumptive Republican nominee, in November.

That leaves 2012 up for grabs, and Bill and Hillary Clinton eagerly are awaiting another chance. Although graciously pledging to support Mr. Obama, as a good Democrat should, she must hope he will lose in November.

She will be only 64 years old in 2012. If Mr. McCain decides to run again, at age 76, Ms. Clinton should be a shoo-in against such a lackluster opponent.

Charles E. Williams | St. Charles

What price will we pay?

Regarding "Obama's health care RX" (June 11): U.S. Sen. Barack Obama, D-Ill., the presumptive Democratic nominee for president, accompanied a nurse during her shift at Barnes-Jewish Hospital.

People who are uninsured need to be cared for. As a society, we need to help our neighbors in need. That is not the issue. The issue is whether we want to provide universal health care for everyone or provide only for those who are uninsured or underinsured because of financial hardship? And how much are we willing to pay?

A few years ago, we visited friends in Brussels and talked at length about the soft socialism that defines Europe. People in most Europeon countries are cared for from cradle to grave. In some countries, housing, higher education and health care are provided. It all sounds wonderful. But there are problems that are not discussed. It isn't all happiness and light.

To pay for all of this, Europeans are taxed, taxed and taxed some more. In Belgium, people are taxed if they have more than one television, inheritance taxes are over the top and income is taxed. You name it, and it probably is taxed and taxed heavily. This doesn't even begin to discuss the bureaucracy and rules governing all of these "free" programs.

We need to be very careful as a nation as we decide what price we are willing to pay. No matter what, it will cost us and it could cost every individual dearly in terms of independence and freedom. Americans value our rights to freedom, privacy and independence from government intervention in our lives. We are treading on very shaky ground.

Donna Wolf | Maryland Heights

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


June 11, 2008 Wednesday
THIRD EDITION


Lobbyists abound in Obama, McCain camps what both say o They tout themselves as foes of special interests. WHAT BOTH DO o They employ fundraisers who double as lobbyists.


BYLINE: By Sharon Theimer THE ASSOCIATED PRESS


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A5


LENGTH: 446 words


DATELINE: WASHINGTON


Barack Obama and John McCain are billing themselves as distant from special interests. It doesn't take a very deep look into their White House bids to see that's not quite the case.

Presidential races tap into the same political circles that keep lobbyists employed and the revolving door spinning. Obama and McCain have a long way to go to free themselves of insiders and special interests.

Roughly one-third of 93 "Reagan alumni" who endorsed McCain have been or are registered to lobby, though McCain never said so. The Florida fundraising team for McCain and the Republican National Committee, announced last week, includes at least two Florida lobbyists: Fred Karlinsky is Florida counsel to the Property Casualty Insurers Association, which lobbies in Washington on consumer issues, disaster planning and insurance; the other, Thomas Panza, was on Florida business regulation and health care study commissions.

Obama's team in Nevada included at least two lobbyists: one who represented Barrick Gold of North America, a mining company that also lobbies in Washington, and another whose clients included U.S. Airways, Corrections Corp. of America and the consulting firm Accenture, which lobby in Washington.

McCain's advisory committee on legal issues, announced last month, included at least nine lobbyists, but McCain didn't identify them as such. Instead, his campaign listed their former government positions.

Obama accepted an endorsement from former Sen. Don Riegle of Michigan but never mentioned Riegle also is a Washington lobbyist whose clients include London-based metals merchant Norimet Ltd. and Geneva-based liquor distributor SPI Group SA.

Obama and the Democratic National Committee announced Thursday that the party will follow Obama's policy of turning away donations from Washington lobbyists.

Last month, McCain adopted a conflict-of-interest policy that drove some Washington lobbyists from his campaign.

To consort only with outsiders, McCain and Obama would have to shun leaders of their own parties. The Democratic and Republican national committees include well-known Washington lobbyists among their top officials. Some Democratic superdelegates are lobbyists.

Lobbyists have made campaign appearances for McCain. They include former Oklahoma Gov. Frank Keating, chief executive of the American Council of Life Insurers and co-chairman of "Catholics for McCain."

At least 17 lobbyists are among McCain's top fundraisers.

Obama's biggest fundraisers include Charles Adams Jr., a partner in Hogan & Hartson, a law and lobbying firm active in Washington, and Michael Froman, an executive at Citigroup, which spent $8 million lobbying in Washington last year.

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


June 10, 2008 Tuesday
THIRD EDITION


Race is on - for campaign cash Notions of reform compete with desire to win White House.


BYLINE: By Bill Lambrecht Post-Dispatch Washington Bureau Chief Jo Mannies and Sarah Lohman of the Post-Dispatch contributed to this report.


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A1


LENGTH: 1047 words


DATELINE: WASHINGTON


Barack Obama and John McCain turned their eyes to fundraising Monday, with their reputations as campaign reformers on the line.

The first week of the general election campaign for the presidential nominees-in-waiting saw Obama at a fundraising stop in St. Louis, while McCain appealed to contributors in Virginia.

Obama's appearance in a St. Louis hotel ballroom was an old-school event for the politician who has moved the money chase into cyberspace, raising $265 million in the process. Nonetheless, his local backers hoped Monday's fundraiser would snag $1 million for the campaign.

Obama has built a party-sized donor base of over 1.5 million people, and on Monday he appealed for 30,000 more in an e-mail asking supporters to continue building the organization "by stepping up and owning a piece of the campaign."

Meanwhile, McCain is raising money in New York today after two events with donors in Virginia on Monday. His campaign repeated its call for Obama to live up to an earlier pledge to submit to public funding and spending limits.

"Senator McCain made a pledge to do that, and we expect that Senator Obama will stand by his pledge to accept public financing," said McCain spokeswoman Crystal Benton, adding that McCain was "moving toward" accepting federal funds.

Obama's Internet money machine - buttressed by Hillary Rodham Clinton's financial backers signing on - could overwhelm McCain's fundraising operation, experts said Tuesday. But they expect McCain to be well-funded with the help of the Republican National Committee.

McCain had a moderately strong April, bringing in $21.5 million, and his $37 million on hand enabled him to unleash an early ad campaign in Missouri and several other swing states.

Benton contended that McCain "will be well-positioned to run an aggressive and competitive campaign through the general election." McCain aides pointed to the help they can expect from the RNC, whose $50 million war chest can be diverted to McCain's use.

Being amply funded in the months ahead will be vital, given both campaigns' stated intentions to expand the usual electoral map. Obama wants to focus on Republican-voting states like Virginia and Colorado, while McCain tries to reverse pro-Democrat voting patterns in the upper Midwest and elsewhere.

disarmament?

Obama's campaign has not said whether he will follow up on an earlier assertion that it would "aggressively pursue" an agreement with McCain to take public financing, which would provide about $85 million for each candidate from September through the Nov. 4 election.

Candidates who agree to accept the public money are then bound by spending limits, which have yet to be set. That would not prevent spending by the national committees and others.

Obama spokesman Bill Burton said the campaign would decide "soon" on general election financing. He noted that the broad participation the Obama campaign has generated is a goal of campaign finance reform.

To many Obama partisans, submitting to the federal system would have the effect of "unilateral disarmament" given the fundraising trails the Illinois senator has blazed through the Internet.

U.S. Rep. Rahm Emanuel, D-Ill., developed the reputation as one of his party's most astute financial operatives, raising money first for Bill Clinton's presidential campaigns and later for the Democratic Congressional Campaign Committee that orchestrated a takeover in the House.

He said his fundraising efforts for Clinton were "horse-and-buggy" compared with Obama's computer-generated millions. "They hit shift-F7, there's no sweat equity, no time difference, and you produce millions of dollars."

obama's decision

Anthony Corrado, a fundraising expert and a professor at Colby College in Maine, said he expects Obama to have a significant fundraising advantage. But he added that McCain will still have plenty of money.

Corrado said each campaign likely will have about $50 million to $60 million for operating until the political conventions in late August and early September.

Beyond the $85 million in public financing, McCain could raise $10 million to cover legal and accounting expenses, then spend another $19 million in coordination with the RNC. The Republican effort could total a respectable $250 million this fall, he said.

For Obama, deciding to take the federal money also could limit his campaign's ability to control its message. Asking his supporters to direct their contributions to the national Democratic Party would complicate advertising and other facets of an Obama operation that so far has been tightly and frugally run.

Corrado is among those who expect Obama to be a realist rather than a reformer. "The practical reality of wanting to win is going to trump any desire to abide by public funding for the sake of abiding by public funding," he said.

Public finance advocates argue that while Obama's grass-roots fundraising has reduced the prevalence of big-money contributors, he could impede broader reform if he chooses to become the first presidential candidate in more than a generation to run solely on private contributions.

Advocates like Craig Holman, a campaign finance expert for the nonprofit Public Citizen, say Obama could send a damaging message.

"He's still raising money from donors who want favors from the Obama administration," Holman said, adding that self-described reformers should "not be the ones presiding over the selling of the White House this time around."

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


June 10, 2008 Tuesday
THIRD EDITION


There's hope with Obama, but race is still a hang-up


BYLINE: sylvester brown, jr.


SECTION: METRO; Pg. B1


LENGTH: 777 words


"What's the deal?"

Barack Obama was coming to town and I complained to my wife about not getting a call or a visit from the assumed Democratic presidential nominee.

Of course, Vicki knew I was kidding. But she played along anyway: "It's probably because you were such a naysayer."

She's right. I was initially an "un-decider" when it came to Obama's campaign. I still kick myself when I think back to my brief, one-on-one meeting with the candidate last year.

There I was, all racked with doubt, trying to get Obama to share my gloomy perspective.

"Let's cut to the chase," I said.

"OK, let's," he responded.

I spouted reasons he couldn't win: fear of a black president, claims that he wasn't "black enough," his middle name (Hussein), dirty political tricks, and so on and so forth.

Obama just flashed that toothy smile of his and talked about the nation's desire for change and the unbeatable power of "hope." Once voters really get to know him, he said, "I have no doubts we'll do very well."

Well, I still had doubts back then. I even wrote about them.

Guess that's why he didn't call.

Still, a fella can dream, right? I certainly did - big time.

I imagined my "friend" Barack and I sitting in my den, sipping piña coladas, watching DVDs of the Rev. Jeremiah Wright sermons, discussing his Republican opponent, John McCain, and my potential gig as the next White House press secretary.

I suppose his fundraiser Monday was a tad more important than hanging out with his bud.

At the very least, Michelle Obama could have checked in. After all, I was among the few who defended her "First time in my adult life ... I am really proud of my country" comment.

I agreed with her, writing that I was pleasantly shocked we've evolved to a point where a woman or black man could be our next president.

Perhaps Mrs. Obama forgot that she was the one who pulled me aboard her husband's bandwagon when we talked in January.

Yes, I tried my negativity spiel on "The Closer." Michelle Obama wasn't having it. She whipped out the civil rights card, reminding me of the sacrifices of icons Medgar Evers, Rosa Parks and Martin Luther King Jr.

It's been quite a ride ever since. I've gone from "no hope" to "hopeful," back to "hopeless" and now, cautious optimism.

It's that Rev. Wright issue. It reminds me that race is still Obama's handicap.

According to a Pew Research Center study last week, the Rev. Wright "scandal" was the most widely heard campaign story so far, with more than 60 percent of those surveyed saying they heard a lot about Wright's speeches.

Exit polls during the Indiana and North Carolina primaries showed that nearly half the Democratic voters said the "Wright issue" was an important factor in choosing a candidate.

I was shocked when Pennsylvania Gov. Ed Rendell said in February that there were whites who aren't ready to vote for a black president. The statement wasn't surprising, but the fact that he said it publicly certainly was.

Apparently, he wasn't just talking about Pennsylvania.

A few months ago, a good lawyer friend of mine told me that some of his contemporaries weren't voting for Obama because they fear that, once elected, black activists such as the Revs. Jesse Jackson and Al Sharpton would have open access to the White House.

Now, these are not the sentiments of so-called "working-class whites" who, we're told, haven't connected with Obama. These are college-educated professionals clinging to this irrational notion.

While America was in a state of shock over words uttered by a black preacher years ago, I was stunned by the comments of a right-winger three months ago.

How did Pat Buchanan summon the gall to write that blacks ought to "thank God" they were brought here on slave ships, "introduced to Christian salvation" and "allowed" to reach great levels of "freedom and prosperity"?

Where was the outcry, the calls to denounce and reject? Buchanan's comments didn't anger as many as Wright's. Why? Because Buchanan is seen as a singular, loudmouthed windbag. He's no real threat.

But somehow, Wright is. As irrational as it is, blacks are widely viewed as a monolithic people. Apparently, we share the same passions and frustrations. Therefore, when Wright speaks, he's speaking for us all, including the black presidential candidate.

This explains my attitude of cautious optimism. Racial hang-ups are still deeply rooted in this country. How much has really changed since 1988, when an ad featuring a black convicted murderer, Willie Horton, scared voters away from Michael Dukakis?

Yes, I'm excited about Obama's chances, but I fear the obstacle of race.

Oops. There I go again - going all negative. It's probably a good thing Obama didn't call.

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


June 8, 2008 Sunday
FOURTH EDITION


Electoral map may be up for grabs Both McCain and Obama look for signs of shifting voting patterns.


BYLINE: By Bill Lambrecht Post-Dispatch Washington Bureau Chief


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A1


LENGTH: 1074 words


DATELINE: WASHINGTON


It's no mystery why Barack Obama will return Monday to the bellwether state of Missouri a week after sewing up the Democratic presidential nomination. But what was he doing in traditionally Republican Virginia on his first trip since clinching?

And what was John McCain thinking last week when he campaigned in Wisconsin, so heavily Democratic in some years that Michael Dukakis won it in 1988?

The answers lie in the belief of this year's nominees-in-waiting that there are new opportunities in some states in their quests for the 270 Electoral College votes needed to become president.

Changing voting patterns is a tall order: In 2004, 47 states voted in the same red-blue pattern of 2000. The only states to change were Iowa and New Mexico (to Republican) and New Hampshire (to Democratic).

Nonetheless, Obama and McCain see a playing field that is changing.

McCain is sending early signals of how the map is changing, running statewide television ads in Missouri, as well as in Michigan, Colorado and Nevada, and targeted ads in New Mexico. Meanwhile, Obama, who deployed a masterly small-state strategy to win the nomination, sees expanded opportunity in Virginia, North Carolina and perhaps even Georgia and Mississippi, with their large black voting blocs, by virtue of his status as the first African-American to win a major-party nomination.

Democrats also believe that the rising influence of Hispanics in American politics is on the verge of changing the electoral map in their favor, especially in New Mexico, Colorado and Nevada. With 15 percent of the population, Hispanics are America's largest minority. They could make up nearly 30 percent of the U.S. population by 2050.

"I think he has set the table for a new map," said Sen. Claire McCaskill, D-Mo., one of the Senate's most energetic Obama backers. "If the map turns out at the end of the process to be similar, he will have at least made this election about the people of the country rather than a chess match."

Hard To Flip

Campaigns begin by committing to the swing states. Often, they are states the other party barely won the time before: In 2004, George W. Bush narrowly captured Florida, Ohio and Nevada just as John Kerry (and Al Gore before him) scored narrow wins in states such as Michigan, Minnesota, Pennsylvania and Wisconsin.

Add to that the historic candidacy of Obama and an economy favoring Democrats, and you have the makings of a political map strategy.

Candidates will have geography in mind when choosing running mates. For instance, Southwestern governors Bill Richardson (New Mexico) and Janet Napolitano (Arizona) have been mentioned on the Democratic side.

For McCain, the electoral map likely will be a factor as he sizes up potential running mates, such as Florida Gov. Charlie Crist and ex-Pennsylvania Gov. Tom Ridge.

In his broader strategy, McCain sees openings in the upper Midwest and Democratic-leaning states where Obama lost to Hillary Clinton among working-class whites. McCain also has his eye on New Hampshire, which voted Democratic in 2004 but where he has twice won stirring primary victories.

"We think that McCain can expand the number of states where we have been competitive in the last two cycles, and places like Missouri and Iowa are key on the list," said Gentry Collins, a McCain regional director.

Thanks to his identity as a maverick, McCain also might compete strongly in Ohio, Pennsylvania and other states where another Republican might have faltered, party analysts believe.

"Hillary Clinton's terrific performance in Ohio really puts that state at risk for Obama," said Neil Newhouse, a GOP pollster in Washington.

Republicans view Michigan as primed for takeover. Obama's name wasn't on the ballot of that state's all-but-meaningless Democratic primary, and therefore he didn't build the type of organization he has in other states.

Michigan pollster Ed Sarpolus said that Mitt Romney, whose family has deep roots in the state, would clearly be an asset there for McCain.

"It's basically a 50-50 state here, and neither candidate really has an edge," Sarpolus said. "People are waiting to hear Obama's economic message. All they've heard is 'change' and his war message."

Hispanics Rising

Republicans see hope in Obama's difficulty attracting the votes of Hispanics. Clinton won more than 60 percent of Hispanic votes in the primary season, adding an exclamation point with her emphatic victory in Puerto Rico last Sunday.

Last week, McCain began running ads on Spanish-language radio stations in the Southwest.

Colorado GOP chair Dick Wadhams said that despite Democratic hopes to capture his state, he believes McCain can blunt Obama's inroads into the Hispanic vote while beating him handily in rural areas.

"I don't think Obama's discouraging words on rural voters will go over well in Colorado," Wadhams said, referring to Obama's remark this spring that certain voters cling to guns and religion out of bitterness.

But Democratic strategist Simon Rosenberg contended that McCain's ad buy in the Southwest was a "sign of weakness, not strength."

Despite Obama's problems with Hispanics, Rosenberg, president of the New Democratic Network think tank, argued that McCain has no chance to match George W. Bush's success in drawing 40 percent of the Hispanic vote.

"Obama is going to be able to communicate in Spanish that John McCain threw Hispanics overboard when he dropped the immigration bill," Rosenberg said, referring to McCain's decision to renounce some of his moderate views on immigration.

Wherever candidates end up going, it's a sure bet that Missouri will be on the travel schedule.

McCaskill said that winning statewide in Missouri, particularly in rural areas, is a challenge for any Democrat. But she added: "As people get comfortable with (Obama's) life story, when they see that he has traveled a difficult journey and earned everything he's gotten, I think they will be drawn to him."

Replied McCain strategist Collins: "The fact of the matter is that Senator McCain is a known quantity and has the experience to lead the country forward. I think he's a much more appealing candidate to middle America and to rural voters in places like Missouri."

FIRST EDITION


IN THE NEWS


BYLINE: from news services


SECTION: WEEKEND; Pg. C2


LENGTH: 723 words


Academic conference centers on 'Buffy' Aristotle. Nietzsche. Buffy?

The blond heroine of the campy television series "Buffy the Vampire Slayer," as well as other works by creator Joss Whedon, is to be the focus of an academic conference this weekend at Henderson State University. The show starring Sarah Michelle Gellar won cult fame and critical praise during its seven seasons on The WB and UPN networks.

Since it ended, the series has spawned enough academic books on the philosophy surrounding the roles of friendship and feminism to fill a 15-foot-wide bookshelf at the college in Arkadelphia, Ark., said Kevin Durand, an associate professor of philosophy.

"It has staying power," Durand said. "It's like I tell my students in philosophy a lot of times: We're not so much about necessarily finding all the answers as wanting to ask better questions. 'Buffy,' I think, does that. 'Buffy' never really leaves you with nice, pat answers. You have even more questions than when you started."

Ed McMahon talks about possible home foreclosure

Ed McMahon blames the possible foreclosure of his multimillion-dollar Beverly Hills house on a set of problems all too familiar to many Americans: a foundering economy, health problems and poor planning.

"If you spend more money than you make, you know what happens," McMahon said Thursday night on CNN's "Larry King Live." "You know, a couple of divorces thrown in, a few things like that. And, you know, things happen."

McMahon, 85, appeared with his wife, Pamela. The couple said they are $644,000 behind on their mortgage payments and are in negotiations with lender Countrywide Home Loans Inc. to set a foreclosure date.

McMahon, in a neck brace, said he had stopped working since he broke his neck in a fall 18 months ago. He didn't elaborate.

McMahon, who was Johnny Carson's sidekick on the "Tonight" show, said the house had been on the market for two years and that although 50 organizations or individuals had looked at it, no one had made an offer. Documents show McMahon has a $4.8 million mortgage on the home.

Publisher sues Victoria Gotti

Victoria Gotti, daughter of the late mob boss John Gotti, was sued by HarperCollins Publishers Worldwide for failing to complete a memoir about her life or return a $70,000 advance.

Gotti, 45, a former columnist for the New York Post and sister of accused gangster John A. "Junior" Gotti, starred in the reality television show "Growing Up Gotti" about her life with her three sons, which was broadcast on A&E.

Stars shine at NBA Finals

Local troubadour James Taylor welcomed his hometown team back to the NBA Finals on Thursday night in Boston, singing the national anthem before Game 1 of the Boston Celtics' 98-88 victory over the Los Angeles Lakers.

Actors Bruce Willis and Leonardo DiCaprio, who have made the TD Banknorth Garden a second home while filming movies in town, were also in the star-studded crowd. So were "Grey's Anatomy" star Ellen Pompeo and Chris Tucker of the "Rush Hour" movies.

The court was ringed by members of the New England Patriots - including Randy Moss, Richard Seymour and Rodney Harrison - and Celtics and Lakers alumni from Bill Russell and John Havlicek to Magic Johnson and Kareem Abdul-Jabbar.

The crowd also took time out to send its best wishes to Sen. Ted Kennedy, the Massachusetts Democrat who is recovering from brain surgery.

CNN's queen of quirk keeps

her sense of humor

Oh, man. One little joke about Barack Obama and that Middle Eastern fellow with the rhyming name, and her inbox is piled high with squawky offended e-mail. It's enough to make Jeanne Moos wish she were back interviewing three-legged pantyhose or even two-headed turtles.

"I'm used to doing dog stories," says CNN's queen of quirk, shaking her head as she leafs through outraged denunciations ranging from not particularly bright to how low can you sink? "Dogs don't write in. I've never gotten any angry e-mails from dogs."

If there's one lesson Moos has learned in the 12 months since becoming a regular on Situation Room, the network's daily campaign roundup, it's that political activists have much less of a sense of humor than the average canine. Whether she's musing on John McCain's difficulties using a teleprompter or Hillary Clinton's befuddlement at the technology of convenience-store coffee machines, Moos has drawn almost daily fire.

BYLINE: From News Reports


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A21

WASHINGTON

Hillary Clinton thanks her campaign staff

Hillary Clinton held a thank-you session at her house in Washington for people who worked on her campaign.

Hillary, Bill and Chelsea Clinton were dressed casually and were seen mingling with the guests, according to MSNBC.

Hillary Clinton spoke to the staff. Her comments were described as celebratory and touching by spokesman Mo Elleithee. She thanked people for putting their lives on hold to work for her.

She asked everybody to come to an event today in Washington at which she will acknowledge that Obama has won the nomination, saying it will be an important step in bringing the party together.

WASHINGTON

McCain has his best month of fundraising

John McCain's campaign raised at least $21.5 million in May, its best fundraising month yet, in a sign that its effort to draw donations in tandem with the Republican National Committee is yielding dividends.

Barack Obama reported raising $31 million in April. He has not yet released his May numbers.

CHICAGO

Obama makes plans for end of second term

Barack Obama made a surprise visit to a rally in Chicago supporting the city's bid to host the 2016 Olympics.

"In 2016, I'll be wrapping up my second term as president," Obama said. "So I can't think of a better way than to be marching into Washington Park ... as president of the United States and announcing to the world, 'Let the games begin!' "

The cheers were loud.

MOUNT LAUREL, N.J.

Congressman accuses Clinton campaign

U.S. Rep. Rob Andrews, D-N.J., who endorsed Clinton, said a high-ranking person in Hillary Clinton's campaign told him the campaign strategy was to win Jewish voters by exploiting tensions between Jews and blacks.

Andrews declined to name the caller.

The Clinton camp called Andrews' comments "sad and divisive."

POLL

Here are results of the CNN-Opinion Research Corp. poll on the presidential race conducted Wednesday and Thursday:

- Barack Obama, 47 percent

- John McCain, 43 percent

- Ralph Nader, 6 percent

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


June 7, 2008 Saturday
THIRD EDITION


Obama to attend fundraiser here


BYLINE: By Jo Mannies post-dispatch political correspondent


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A8


LENGTH: 203 words


DATELINE: ST. LOUIS


The two presumptive presidential nominees, Democrat Barack Obama and Republican John McCain, are kick-starting their campaigns in Missouri in moves that underscore the state's political importance.

Obama's campaign announced Friday that he'll spend Monday and Tuesday in Missouri - a longer visit that initially announced.

The candidate's first stop will be an evening fundraiser Monday at the Renaissance Grand Hotel downtown, where tickets will range from $500 to $2,300 apiece. The event is otherwise closed to the public, and press access will be limited to a few media outlets.

Obama's campaign schedule for Tuesday was unclear as of Friday, but local supporters say tentative plans call for some sort of issue-oriented event.

Meanwhile, McCain was slated today to launch TV ads in Missouri and nine other states deemed crucial to his White House bid. The first ad will focus on national security, his campaign has said.

McCain's campaign also announced Friday that it has hired a state campaign director, Dave Hageman, who now is executive director of an Ellisville-based political consulting firm, Victory Enterprises-Missouri.

From 2003 to 2007, he was executive director of the state House Republican Campaign Committee.

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


June 4, 2008 Wednesday
THIRD EDITION


McCain tries to upstage Obama


BYLINE: By Michael D. Shear and Juliet Eilperin Washington Post


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. A3


LENGTH: 411 words


DATELINE: NEW ORLEANS


Sen. John McCain wasted no time Tuesday night in launching his first general-election broadside against Sen. Barack Obama, casting the Illinois Democrat as an out-of-touch liberal who offers a false promise of change.

In a prime-time speech designed to upstage Obama on the night he claimed the Democratic nomination, McCain began what top aides and other Republicans promise will be an aggressive effort to claim the mantles of reform, experience and mainstream values. Obama is an "impressive man," McCain said, but one with a thin record.

"For all his fine words and all his promise, he has never taken the hard but right course of risking his own interests for yours; of standing against the partisan rancor on his side to stand up for our country," McCain said less than two hours before Obama spoke in the same St. Paul, Minn., arena where McCain, of Arizona, will claim the Republican nomination in September.

McCain began his speech by praising Sen. Hillary Rodham Clinton, who in the Democratic primary race won over many rural and working-class voters whom McCain hopes to capture in November.

"As the father of three daughters, I owe her a debt for inspiring millions of women to believe there is no opportunity in this great country beyond their reach," McCain said. "I am proud to call her my friend."

Two McCain aides said his speech was the beginning of a "great debate" on the direction of the country. It will be followed quickly by a television ad campaign aimed at reinforcing McCain's core message: that Obama's sweeping rhetoric offers little real promise of changing the political culture in Washington.

Confronting what his aides expect to be Obama's principal attack against him, McCain explicitly rejected the idea that he represents President George W. Bush's third term.

"Why does Sen. Obama believe it's so important to repeat that idea over and over again?" he asked. "Because he knows it's very difficult to get Americans to believe something they know is false." As evidence of his independence, McCain highlighted his breaks with Bush on Iraq, energy and climate change.

In his speech, Obama honored McCain's service but derided the Republican's claim to stand for change, linking him to what he called the "failed" foreign and economic policies of Bush.

"So I'll say this - there are many words to describe John McCain's attempt to pass off his embrace of George Bush's policies as bipartisan and new," Obama said. "But change is not one of them."
